The Ash'ari Objection on 'Kun' and the Refutation
An Ash'ari objection and the Imam's refutation through distinguishing command types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes the Ash'ari objection by distinguishing between a command of obligation and a command of creation, and why the command 'Kun' necessitates the existence of what is commanded.

Core Idea
Commands of creation necessitate existence; commands of obligation do not.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi recounts a debate with an Ash'ari theologian who argued that the divine command 'Kun' (Be) does not necessitate the existence of the thing commanded, whereas the attribute of takwin (bringing into existence) does. The Imam responds by making a crucial distinction between two types of commands: those that impose obligation and those that create. This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction and defends the Maturidi position on the attribute of takwin.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Ash'ari's claim that 'Kun' is a command and therefore does not guarantee existence, while takwin is an act that does. Note the logical structure.
The Ash'ari uses a contrast between command and act.
2
Identify the Imam's distinction
Find where the Imam introduces two types of commands: amr ijab (command of obligation) and amr takwin (command of creation). Understand how each has a different ruling.
The ruling of amr ijab is obligation, not existence; the ruling of amr takwin is bringing into existence.
3
Apply the distinction to the objection
See how the Imam shows that 'Kun' is a command of creation, so it must produce existence, otherwise it would imply deficiency. This refutes the Ash'ari objection.
The Imam also clarifies that the existence is not necessarily in pre-eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض أشعري والرد عليه
وكانت أتكلم مع بعض الأشعرية وألزمته فصل قوله كن، فزعم أن قوله كن أمر ، وليس من قضية الأمر وجود المأمور به لا محالة، فلا يوجب قدمه قدم المكون فأما التكوين فهو فعل، ومن قضية الفعل وجود المفعول لا محالة. فقلت: إنما لم يكن من قضية الأمر وجود المأمور به إذا كان الأمر أمر إيجاب فلم يجب به إلا ما هو حكمة وهو الوجوب، فأما الوجود فليس من حكمه، فأما الأمر إذا لم يكن أمر إيجاب بل كان أمر تكوين فحكمه التكوين فينبغي أن يوجد حكمه، ألا ترى أن في أمر الإيجاب لو لم يوجد المأمور به لما لزم الأمر نقص لأنه ما أمر ليوجد بل أمر ليجب، وقد ثبت ما أثبته بالأمر، وفي أمر التكوين لو لم يوجد المكون لزمه نقص، لأنه أمر ليوجد ، فلم يثبت ما أثبته بالأمر فكان دليل عجز ، فدل أن هذا الكلام باطل، وأن التكوين يوجب المكون، ومع ذلك لم يحصل المكون في الأزل لما مر . فكذا في التكوين، وأمر التكوين مناقضة للأشعرية لا محيص لهم عنها البتة، وكذا
An Ash'ari objection and the response to it.
I was speaking with some Ash'ari and I pressed him concerning the import of His saying 'Kun'. He claimed that His saying 'Kun' is a command, and it is not a necessary consequence of a command that what is commanded exists inevitably; thus, its pre-eternity does not necessitate the pre-eternity of the mukawwan. But takwin is an act, and it is a necessary consequence of an act that the effect exists inevitably. I said: That the existence of what is commanded is not a necessary consequence of the command is only if the command is a command of obligation (amr ijab); then only what is wisdom is necessitated, which is obligation (wujub). As for existence, it is not a ruling of it. But if the command is not a command of obligation but a command of creation (amr takwin), then its ruling is takwin, so its ruling should occur. Do you not see that in the command of obligation, if what is commanded does not exist, no deficiency is required of the commander, because he did not command it to exist, but rather to be obligatory; and he has established what he established by the command. In the command of creation, if the mukawwan does not exist, deficiency is required of him, because he commanded it to exist; then he has not established what he established by the command, so it would be evidence of incapacity. Thus this speech is false, and takwin necessitates the mukawwan. Nevertheless, the mukawwan is not brought about in pre-eternity for the reason already mentioned. So likewise in takwin, and the command of creation is a contradiction for the Ash'ariyya from which they have absolutely no escape. And likewise.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage records an objection from an Ash'ari and the Imam's response. The Ash'ari argued that the imperative 'Kun' (Be) is a command, and a command does not necessarily entail the existence of what is commanded. Therefore, the pre-eternity of 'Kun' does not necessitate the pre-eternity of the world (mukawwan). In contrast, he argued, takwin is an act (fi'l), and an act necessarily entails the existence of the effect (maf'ul). The Imam replies by distinguishing between two types of command: a command of obligation (amr ijab) and a command of creation (amr takwin). A command of obligation only imposes the obligation (wujub) on the commanded, not the actual existence of the thing. But the command 'Kun' is a command of creation; its purpose is to bring the thing into existence. If it failed to produce existence, that would imply deficiency (naqs) in the commander, indicating incapacity. Therefore, the command of creation does necessitate the existence of the commanded thing. However, the Imam emphasizes that this does not mean the world must be pre-eternal, because the command is for the thing to come into existence at its appointed time, not in pre-eternity. He concludes that the Ash'ari's argument is invalid, and that takwin (the act of creation) indeed necessitates the mukawwan, and the Ash'ari's position is a contradiction from which they cannot escape.

Key Terms
الأمر (amr)
الإيجاب (ijab)
التكوين (takwin)
المأمور (al-ma'mur)
المأمور به (al-ma'mur bihi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any command from God must bring about the existence of what is commanded.
Correction: Only commands of creation necessitate existence; commands of obligation only impose obligation.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Nasafi, why does the command 'Kun' necessitate the existence of what is commanded?
A) Because it is a command of obligation, which always brings about existence.
B) Because it is a command of creation, whose purpose is to bring the thing into existence, and failure would imply deficiency.
C) Because it is a physical act, not a command.
D) Because the Ash'ari admitted it.

Key Takeaway
The command 'Kun' is a command of creation, so it necessitates the existence of the created thing, otherwise it would imply deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'Kun' and it becomes, do you truly believe that God's creative command is distinct from His legislative commands to you? How does this distinction reshape your understanding of His commands in the Qur'an? Will you now approach His commands with greater certainty that He will bring about what He wills, or will you treat some as mere obligations without expecting their fulfillment?
?
The Imam argues that a command of obligation does not require existence—only duty. Are there commands of obligation in your life (e.g., prayers, fasting) that you treat as if they were commands of creation, expecting immediate visible results, and feeling deficient when they don't appear? Can you accept that the purpose of obligation is duty itself, not necessarily worldly outcome?
?
If you believed that God's commands of creation always succeed, why do you sometimes doubt that He will bring about what He has promised in your life? This lesson shows that failure of a creative command would imply deficiency in God. Let this truth push you to full certainty in Allah's power to create. What specific promise of God do you need to trust more deeply starting today?

Takwin Does Not Imply Inability
Refutation of objection that eternal takwin implies inability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the absence of a created thing at the time of eternal takwin does not indicate divine inability, using the analogy of divine will.

Core Idea
Eternal takwin brings things into being at their appointed times, not in pre-eternity.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection: if takwin is eternal, why isn't its effect (the created thing) also eternal? Doesn't the absence of the created thing imply that God is unable to create it? The author refutes this by drawing a parallel with the attribute of will (irada), showing that an attribute can be eternal while its effect appears at the appropriate moment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage carefully. The objection claims that an eternal takwin without an eternal made thing would be a sign of inability.
Note the phrase 'takwin and no made thing is inability'.
2
Follow the refutation
The author explains that the objection would only hold if takwin were intended to create the thing in pre-eternity, but it is not. Takwin's purpose is to bring things into existence at their own times.
3
Reflect on the analogy
The author draws an analogy with divine will (irada). Just as eternal will does not require an eternal willed object, eternal takwin does not require an eternal made thing.
This analogy clarifies that attributes can be eternal while their effects are temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التكوين ولا مكون عجز . قلنا: إنما يكون كذلك أن لو وجد التكوين ليكون المكون القدم، فلم يكن، وما كان التكوين لهذا بل ليوجد في وقته. ألا ترى أنه لا يقال وجود الإرادة ولا مراد عجز و اضطرار، لأنه إنما يكون كذلك أن لو كانت الإرادة ليكون المراد في الأزل فلم يكن ، فأما إذا كانت ليكون المراد في وقته، لم يلزم بعد المراد في الأزل ضرورة وعجز ، فكذا هذا .
Takwin and no made thing is inability. We said: it would be so only if takwin existed to make the made thing pre-eternal, but it did not be; and takwin was not for this, but to bring it into existence at its time. Do you not see that it is not said: existence of will and no willed thing is inability and compulsion, because it would be so only if will existed to make the willed thing in pre-eternity, but it did not be. As for if it existed to make the willed thing at its time, the absence of the willed thing in pre-eternity does not entail necessity and inability. So is this.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection that if takwin (bringing into existence) is an eternal attribute, then the absence of the created thing would imply that God is unable, because a cause without its effect is deficient. The author refutes this by distinguishing between the purpose of takwin. He argues that the objection would hold only if takwin were intended to produce the created thing in pre-eternity, but it is not; rather, it is intended to produce it at its proper time. He provides an analogy with the divine attribute of will (irada): it is not said that the existence of will without a willed thing implies inability, because will is for producing its object at the appointed time. Similarly, the absence of the made thing in pre-eternity does not indicate inability, because takwin is timed for its own moment.

Key Terms
التكوين
المكون
الإرادة
المراد
الأزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If takwin is eternal, the created thing must also be eternal; otherwise God is unable.
Correction: Takwin is intended to bring the thing into existence at its own time, not in pre-eternity, so the absence of the created thing in pre-eternity does not imply inability, just as eternal will does not require an eternal willed object.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the absence of the made thing in pre-eternity not imply inability?
A) Because takwin is not a real attribute.
B) Because takwin is intended to produce the made thing at its appointed time, not in pre-eternity.
C) Because God's power is limited to certain times.
D) Because the made thing cannot exist in pre-eternity due to its temporal nature.

Key Takeaway
The absence of a created thing at the time of eternal takwin does not indicate divine inability; rather, takwin brings things into being at their proper times, just as eternal will brings about its object at the appointed moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Distinguishing Attribute from Effect in Eternal Creation
Response to doubts about saying 'created in eternity' due to implication of eternal creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi scholars distinguish between the eternal attribute of creation and the temporal existence of created things, and how they respond to the objection that 'created in eternity' would imply eternal creation.

Core Idea
The eternal attribute of creation does not make created things eternal; only the attribute itself is eternal.

This lesson addresses a key doubt: if God's attribute of takwin (creative power) is eternal, why can't we say 'He created in pre-eternity'? The answer lies in a careful distinction between the attribute and its effect, and in maintaining proper expression to avoid misunderstanding. Imam Maturidi provides a general principle to guard against such missteps.

Guided Path
1
Understand the doubt
Read the passage and identify the doubt: if takwin is eternal, one should be able to say 'He created in pre-eternity' just as one says 'He knew' or 'He was able'.
The doubt arises from an apparent inconsistency in language.
2
Analyze the response for creation
Notice the author's distinction: saying 'He created' can refer to the eternal attribute (valid) or to the existence of the created thing (invalid). Pay attention to the concern about misleading listeners.
The same distinction applies to knowledge.
3
Apply the principle to knowledge
See the analogy: 'God knew the world existing at its time' is valid, but 'God knew the existence of the world in pre-eternity' is not, because it implies the world is pre-eternal.
This reinforces the need to specify times for objects of attributes.
4
Grasp the general principle
Study the principle from Sheikh Abu Mansur: when describing God absolutely, attributes apply eternally; when mentioning objects, specify their times to avoid implying their pre-eternity.
This principle guides proper expression in theological discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الجواب عما تعلقوا به من الشبه فنقول : قولكم لو كان التكوين أزليا لكان ينبغي أن يجوز أن يقال خلق في الأزل كما يصح أن يقال علم وقدر. وهذا باطل، لأن القائل لو قال خلق وأراد به ثبوت صفة الخلق لا وجود المخلوق كان جائزا ، وإن عنى به وجود المخلوق كان باطلا، وعند الإطلاق ربما يفهم منه قدم المخلوق فيمتنع عنه صيانة الأوهام السامعين عن الباطل. وهكذا نقول في العلم : إنه لو قال إن الله تعالى علم في الأزل العالم موجودا لوقت وجوده صح، ولو قال: إن الله تعالى علم وجود العالم في الأزل لا يجوز لما فيه من إيهام إثبات قدم المعلوم وهو العالم. قال الشيخ أبو منصور – رحمه الله -: الأصل في هذا أن الله تعالى إذا أطلق الوصف له وصف بما يوصف به من الفعل والعلم ونحوه يلزم الوصف به في الأزل، وإذا ذكر معه الذي هو تحت وصفه به من المعلوم والمقدور عليه والمراد والمكون يذكر فيه أوقات تلك الأشياء لئلا يتوهم قدم تلك الأشياء، وهذا كله راجع إلى محافظة الأدب في العبارة، والتمسك بما هذا سبيله عند وقوع الخلاف في الحقائق دون الألفاظ والعبارات مما يدفع إليه الحيرة والعجز. وقد خرج الجواب عما ذكروا بعد هذا : أن التكوين لو كان أزليا لكان المكون أزليا لأنه يقتضى
As for the response to what they clung to from the doubts, we say: your saying ‘If takwin were eternal, then it should be permissible to say ‘He created in pre-eternity’ just as it is valid to say ‘He knew and was able’.’ This is false, because if one says ‘He created’ and intends by it the establishment of the attribute of creation, not the existence of the created thing, it is permissible; but if he intends the existence of the created thing, it is false. And when used absolutely, sometimes the pre-eternity of the created thing is understood from it, so it is refrained from in order to protect the minds of listeners from falsehood. Similarly, we say concerning knowledge: if one says ‘God knew in pre-eternity the world existing at its time of existence’ it is valid; but if he says ‘God knew the existence of the world in pre-eternity’ it is not permissible because of the illusion of establishing the pre-eternity of the known, which is the world. Sheikh Abu Mansur – may God have mercy on him – said: The principle in this is that when God is described absolutely, He is described with what He is described with of action, knowledge, and the like, then the description applies to Him in pre-eternity. And when the thing that is under His description, such as the known, the power-affected, the willed, and the made, is mentioned alongside it, one mentions the times of those things so as not to give the illusion of the pre-eternity of those things. All of this goes back to preserving propriety in expression and adhering to such a course when disagreement occurs concerning realities, not words and expressions – something to which bewilderment and incapacity lead. And the response has come out concerning what they mentioned after this: that if takwin were eternal, the made thing would be eternal because it necessitates…
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage constitutes a refutation of further doubts raised by opponents concerning the eternal attribute of takwin. The specific doubt is: if takwin is eternal, why cannot one say 'God created in pre-eternity' just as one says 'God knew' and 'God is powerful'? The author responds by making a crucial distinction. The phrase 'He created' can be used in two senses: (1) intending the eternal attribute of creation (ṣifat al-khalq) – this is permissible; (2) intending the existence of the created thing (makhluq) – this is false, because it implies the pre-eternity of the created thing. To avoid misleading the listener, one should avoid absolute expressions that could be misunderstood. The same applies to knowledge: one may say 'God knew in pre-eternity the world existing at its time' but not 'God knew the existence of the world in pre-eternity' because the latter suggests that the known (the world) is pre-eternal. The author then quotes Sheikh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, who lays down a general principle: when describing God absolutely (i.e., without qualification), attributes like action and knowledge apply eternally. However, when mentioning the objects of those attributes (the known, the made, etc.), one must specify their times to avoid implying that those objects are eternal. This is a matter of maintaining proper expression (adab al-ibara) when disagreement is about realities, not mere words. The passage ends with an incomplete argument: if takwin is eternal, then the made thing would also be eternal because takwin necessitates it… (the argument cuts off).

Key Terms
الخلق
المخلوق
العلم
المعلوم
التكوين
المكون
الأزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that saying 'God created in pre-eternity' is equivalent to saying 'God knew in pre-eternity', and since both attributes are eternal, both should be permissible.
Correction: The passage shows that 'created' can refer to the temporal existence of the created thing, whereas 'knew' does not carry that implication. Therefore, proper expression requires distinguishing between the eternal attribute and its temporal effect.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it invalid to say 'God created the world in pre-eternity'?
A) Because creation is not an eternal attribute.
B) Because it implies that the world is pre-eternal.
C) Because God did not create the world.
D) Because the word 'created' cannot be used for God.

Key Takeaway
The eternal attribute of creation does not imply the pre-eternity of created things; proper expression must distinguish between the attribute itself and its temporal effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about God's attributes, do you guard your words to avoid even the slightest misunderstanding about His transcendence? Think of a phrase you commonly use—does it protect the listener's mind from false notions about God?
?
This lesson shows that even great scholars like Imam al-Maturidi were meticulous with language. How often do you check your own theological vocabulary against the Qur'an and Sunnah? Will you commit to refining one phrase this week?

Refuting the Objection: Takwin in Eternity and the Principle of Precise Expression
Nasafi refutes an objection about takwin and cites Abu Mansur's principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why saying 'Allah created in eternity' can be misleading unless the intended meaning is clarified, and how Imam Abu Mansur's principle resolves the apparent conflict between eternal attributes and temporal objects.

Core Idea
Eternal attributes do not imply eternal objects; precise wording prevents misunderstanding.

This passage addresses a common objection against the eternity of the attribute of takwin (bringing into existence). The objector claims that if takwin is eternal, then one should be able to say 'He created in eternity,' just as we say 'He knew in eternity.' Nasafi responds by carefully distinguishing between the attribute itself and its object, and then cites Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's foundational principle for describing Allah's attributes and their objects. This lesson will guide you through that distinction and the principle behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is presented: that if takwin is eternal, saying 'He created in eternity' should be valid, like saying 'He knew in eternity.' Notice the parallel being drawn.
The objection relies on an analogy between takwin and 'ilm.
2
Understand the two meanings of 'created'
Nasafi explains that 'created' can mean either the attribute of creating (which is eternal) or the existence of the created thing (which is temporal). When used absolutely, it may imply the latter, so it is avoided. Similarly, for knowledge, specifying the time of the known object is necessary.
This distinction is crucial: attribute vs. object.
3
Grasp Abu Mansur's general principle
Imam Abu Mansur lays down a rule: when Allah is described with an attribute (like act or knowledge), the attribute itself is affirmed in eternity; but when the objects of those attributes are mentioned, their times must be specified to avoid imagining them as eternal.
This principle preserves both divine eternity and temporal creation.
4
Reflect on the importance of precise expression
The passage concludes that all this is about maintaining propriety in wording when disagreement occurs about realities. The goal is to avoid perplexity and incapacity.
Adab fi al-'ibara (propriety in expression) is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الجواب عما تعلقوا به من الشبه فنقول : قولكم لو كان التكوين أزليا لكان ينبغي أن يجوز أن يقال خلق في الأزل كما يصح أن يقال علم وقدر. وهذا باطل، لأن القائل لو قال خلق وأراد به ثبوت صفة الخلق لا وجود المخلوق كان جائزا ، وإن عنى به وجود المخلوق كان باطلا، وعند الإطلاق ربما يفهم منه قدم المخلوق فيمتنع عنه صيانة الأوهام السامعين عن الباطل. وهكذا نقول في العلم : إنه لو قال إن الله تعالى علم في الأزل العالم موجودا لوقت وجوده صح، ولو قال: إن الله تعالى علم وجود العالم في الأزل لا يجوز لما فيه من إيهام إثبات قدم المعلوم وهو العالم. قال الشيخ أبو منصور – رحمه الله -: الأصل في هذا أن الله تعالى إذا أطلق الوصف له وصف بما يوصف به من الفعل والعلم ونحوه يلزم الوصف به في الأزل، وإذا ذكر معه الذي هو تحت وصفه به من المعلوم والمقدور عليه والمراد والمكون يذكر فيه أوقات تلك الأشياء لئلا يتوهم قدم تلك الأشياء، وهذا كله راجع إلى محافظة الأدب في العبارة، والتمسك بما هذا سبيله عند وقوع الخلاف في الحقائق دون الألفاظ والعبارات مما يدفع إليه الحيرة والعجز.
As for the answer to what they have clung to from the ambiguities, we say: Your statement that if takwin (bringing into existence) were eternal, then it should be permissible to say 'He created' in eternity just as it is correct to say 'He knew' and 'He had power' — this is false. Because if one said 'He created' and intended by it the affirmation of the attribute of creating (sifat al-khalq) and not the existence of the created thing, it would be permissible; but if he intended by it the existence of the created thing, it would be false. And when used absolutely, it might be understood to imply the preeternity of the created thing, so it is avoided out of caution for the listeners' minds from falsehood. Similarly we say regarding knowledge: if he said 'Verily Allah knew in eternity the world existing at the time of its existence,' it is correct; but if he said 'Verily Allah knew the existence of the world in eternity,' it is not permissible because of the suggestion it contains of affirming the preeternity of the known, which is the world. Shaykh Abu Mansur — may Allah have mercy on him — said: The principle in this is that when Allah is described absolutely, He is described with what He is described with of act, knowledge, and the like, and the description necessarily applies in eternity; but when that which is under His description — of the known, the empowered, the intended, and the brought-into-existence — is mentioned alongside, the times of those things are mentioned so that the preeternity of those things may not be imagined. All of this goes back to preserving propriety in expression and adhering to what is proper when disagreement occurs about realities (haqa'iq) rather than about words and expressions, for that is what leads to perplexity and incapacity.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection. The objector argued that if takwin (the attribute of bringing into existence) is eternal, then it should be permissible to say 'Allah created in eternity' just as we say 'He knew in eternity.' Nasafi refutes this by distinguishing between two intended meanings of 'created' (khalaqa): (1) the affirmation of the attribute of creating (sifat al-khalq) itself, which is eternal and valid, and (2) the existence of the created thing (al-makhluq), which is temporal and cannot be said to be eternal. When the word is used absolutely, it may mislead listeners into thinking the created thing is eternal, so it is avoided out of caution. Similarly, with knowledge: saying 'Allah knew in eternity the world existing at its time' is correct because it specifies the time of the known (the world), whereas saying 'Allah knew the existence of the world in eternity' suggests that the known is eternal, which is false. Nasafi then cites Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, who lays down a general principle: when Allah is described with an attribute like act or knowledge, the attribute itself is affirmed in eternity without temporal qualification; but when the objects of those attributes (the known, the empowered, the intended, the brought-into-existence) are mentioned together with the attribute, their times must be specified to avoid implying their preeternity. This adherence to cautious expression is about maintaining propriety in wording when the underlying realities (haqa'iq) are disputed, not about changing the realities themselves.

Key Terms
التكوين
الخلق
المخلوق
المعلوم
الأزل
الصفة
الأدب في العبارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an attribute like creation is eternal, then its object must also be eternal.
Correction: The attribute itself is eternal but its object is temporal; the wording must reflect this distinction to avoid implying preeternity of the object.

Checkpoint
Why is it impermissible to say 'Allah knew the existence of the world in eternity' according to this passage?
A) Because knowledge cannot be eternal.
B) Because it implies the preeternity of the known (the world).
C) Because Allah's knowledge is only about the present.
D) Because the world does not exist in any sense.

Key Takeaway
The attribute of creation is eternal, but its object (the created thing) is temporal; proper expression must distinguish between the two.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own speech about Allah's attributes, do you carefully distinguish between the attribute itself and its object, or have you ever said something that could imply a created thing is eternal? What one change will you make today to be more precise?
?
Abu Mansur says that adhering to propriety in expression prevents perplexity and incapacity. In what area of your faith do you feel perplexed, and could it be because you haven't learned to make this distinction? How will you seek clarity?
?
When you hear or read theological discussions, do you pay attention to the exact wording to avoid misunderstanding, or do you assume words have only one meaning? How can you train yourself to be more careful?

The Analogy of 'Kun' and Irada: Refuting the Objection
Nasafi uses analogy to refute the necessity of eternal mukawwan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy between takwin, 'Kun', and irada shows that an eternal attribute does not necessitate an eternal object.

Core Idea
An eternal attribute relates to a temporal object without contradiction.

Nasafi responds to the objection that eternal takwin would require an eternal mukawwan. He draws a parallel to the divine command 'Kun' (Be) and the divine will (irada). Both are eternal, yet what they bring into existence is temporal. This analogy demonstrates the flaw in the objector's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy
Read the Arabic passage where Nasafi presents the analogy. Notice how he uses the same structure: if eternal takwin implies eternal mukawwan, then eternal 'Kun' and eternal irada would also imply eternal objects, which is false.
Pay attention to the phrase 'wa-law kana... la-kana...'.
2
Identify the parallel
Identify the three pairs: (takwin, mukawwan), ('Kun', the object of 'Kun'), (irada, murad). Understand that the objector's reasoning would apply equally to the other two, leading to absurdity.
3
Understand the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that since the preeternity of 'Kun' and irada do not entail the preeternity of their objects, the same applies to takwin. This refutes the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد خرج الجواب عما ذكروا بعد هذا : أن التكوين لو كان أزليا لكان المكون أزليا لأنه يقتضى تعلقه بالمكون. قلنا : ولو كان خطاب كن أزليا لكان المكون أزليا، ولو كانت الإرادة أزلية لكان المراد أزليا، وحيث لم يقتض قدم خطاب "كن" وقدم الإرادة، قدم ما يتعلقان به فكذا هذا ثم نقول: إن كلامك هذا متناقض محال يبطل بعضه بعضا . وذلك لأن قولك : إن التكوين لو كان أزليا لكان المكون أزليا. ذكرت هذه القضية ولا دليل لك عليها وهي ممنوعة ، ثم أردت إثبات هذه القضية بما يوجب بطلانها، لأنك عللت لإثبات هذه القضية فقلت : لأنه يقتضي تعلقه بالمكون، وتعلقه بالمكون يوجب حدوث المكون لا قدمه على ما مر أن ما تعلق وجوده بغيره فهو محدث غير قديم ومن يقيم على دعواه دليلا يوجب ذلك بطلان دعواه، كان غير بصير بالمحاجة. ثم نقول له: قد قام الدليل على أن التكوين أزلي، وعين ذلك الدليل يدل
And the answer has come forth for what they mentioned after this: that if takwin were eternal, then the mukawwan (the brought-into-existence) would be eternal, because it necessitates its relation (ta'alluq) to the mukawwan. We say: and if the address 'Kun' (Be) were eternal, then the thing that is brought into existence by it would be eternal; and if the will (irada) were eternal, then the intended (murad) would be eternal. And since the preeternity of the address 'Kun' and the preeternity of the will do not necessitate the preeternity of what they are related to, so likewise is this. Then we say: Indeed your statement is contradictory, impossible, one part invalidating another. That is because you said: if takwin were eternal, then the mukawwan would be eternal. You stated this proposition without any proof for it, and it is denied. Then you attempted to prove this proposition by that which necessitates its own invalidation, because you gave a reason for proving this proposition saying: because it necessitates its relation (ta'alluq) to the mukawwan, and its relation to the mukawwan necessitates the temporality (huduth) of the mukawwan, not its preeternity, according to what has passed: that whose existence is dependent on another is temporal (muhdath), not eternal. And whoever establishes a proof for his claim that necessitates the invalidation of his claim is not skilled in argumentation. Then we say to him: the proof has been established that takwin is eternal, and that same proof indicates...
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The objector argues: if takwin (the attribute of bringing into existence) is eternal, then the mukawwan (the thing brought into existence) must also be eternal, because takwin is necessarily related to the mukawwan. Nasafi responds with an analogy: the divine command 'Kun' (Be) is eternal, yet the thing commanded to exist is not eternal; similarly, the divine will (irada) is eternal, but the intended (murad) is temporal. So the relation of an eternal attribute to its object does not require the object to be eternal. Nasafi then points out an internal contradiction in the objector's argument: the objector used the fact that takwin entails a relation (ta'alluq) to the mukawwan as grounds for the mukawwan's preeternity. But in fact, such relational dependence implies the mukawwan's temporality (huduth), not its preeternity, because anything whose existence depends on something else is temporal (muhdath). Thus the objector's own proof undermines his claim. Finally, Nasafi asserts that the proof for takwin being eternal is already established, and that very proof leads to the correct conclusion.

Key Terms
التكوين
المكون
خطاب كن
الإرادة
المراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an attribute is eternal and essentially related to its object, then the object must also be eternal.
Correction: This passage shows that the relation (ta'alluq) itself does not necessitate the object's preeternity. The analogies with 'Kun' and irada demonstrate that eternal attributes can have temporal objects.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Nasafi use to refute the objection?
A) The analogy of fire and burning.
B) The analogy of 'Kun' and irada.
C) The analogy of knowledge and the known.
D) The analogy of power and the powerful.

Key Takeaway
The relation of an eternal attribute to its effect does not require the effect to be eternal.

?

The Internal Contradiction in the Objector's Argument
Nasafi identifies a logical flaw in the objector's reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi exposes a logical contradiction in the objector's proof: the relation (ta'alluq) actually implies the temporality of the mukawwan.

Core Idea
The objector's own evidence disproves his claim.

After presenting the analogy, Nasafi delves deeper into the objector's reasoning. The objector argued that takwin's relation to the mukawwan necessitates the mukawwan's preeternity. But Nasafi points out that such relational dependence actually proves temporality, not preeternity. Thus the objector's proof undermines his own conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on contradiction
Read the Arabic where Nasafi says 'Then we say: Indeed your statement is contradictory...' Focus on understanding the logical structure of the objection and its refutation.
Notice the phrase 'mumtani' (impossible).
2
Identify the contradiction
The objector uses the relation (ta'alluq) to argue for preeternity. But Nasafi points out that dependence on another is the very mark of temporality (huduth). Therefore, the objector's evidence actually supports the opposite conclusion.
Review the principle: 'ma tawajjada wujooduhu bi-ghairihi fa-huwa muhdath'.
3
Appreciate Nasafi's critique
Nasafi calls the objector 'non-skilled in argumentation' for using a proof that invalidates his claim. This highlights the importance of sound reasoning in theological debate.

Refuting the Claim that Eternal Takwin Necessitates Eternal Mukawwan
Imam Nasafi refutes the objection that eternal takwin necessitates eternal mukawwan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the objection that if takwin is eternal, then the mukawwan must also be eternal, through analogy and by exposing the opponent's self-contradictory reasoning.

Core Idea
An eternal cause does not force an eternal effect if the effect is contingent.

In kalam, the relationship between a divine attribute and its effect is crucial. The opponent argues that if takwin is eternal, its product must be eternal. Imam Nasafi dismantles this claim through analogy with the divine command 'Be' and divine will, and by showing that the opponent's own reasoning actually proves the opposite—that the mukawwan is originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation once to grasp the flow of argument. Notice how the opponent's claim is presented and then countered.
Pay attention to the technical terms: takwin, mukawwan, irada, khitab kun.
2
Identify the opponent's argument and Nasafi's first response
The opponent claims that eternal takwin necessitates eternal mukawwan because takwin requires a relation to its object. Nasafi responds by pointing out that the eternal command 'Be' and the eternal will also relate to objects, yet those objects are not eternal. This shows the inference is invalid.
This is an argument by analogy (qiyas).
3
Understand Nasafi's second response: internal contradiction
Nasafi then argues that the opponent's own reasoning is self-defeating. If the relation to mukawwan implies anything, it implies the mukawwan's origination (huduth), not its eternity, because anything whose existence depends on another is originated. Thus, the opponent's proof undermines his claim.
This is a powerful refutation that exposes inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمكون يذكر فيه أوقات تلك الأشياء لئلا يتوهم قدم تلك الأشياء، وهذا كله راجع إلى محافظة الأدب في العبارة، والتمسك بما هذا سبيله عند وقوع الخلاف في الحقائق دون الألفاظ والعبارات مما يدفع إليه الحيرة والعجز. وقد خرج الجواب عما ذكروا بعد هذا : أن التكوين لو كان أزليا لكان المكون أزليا لأنه يقتضي تعلقه بالمكون. قلنا : ولو كان خطاب كن أزليا لكان المكون أزليا، ولو كانت الإرادة أزلية لكان المراد أزليا، وحيث لم يقتض قدم خطاب "كن" وقدم الإرادة، قدم ما يتعلقان به فكذا هذا ثم نقول: إن كلامك هذا متناقض محال يبطل بعضه بعضا . وذلك لأن قولك : إن التكوين لو كان أزليا لكان المكون أزليا. ذكرت هذه القضية ولا دليل لك عليها وهي ممنوعة ، ثم أردت إثبات هذه القضية بما يوجب بطلانها، لأنك عللت لإثبات هذه القضية فقلت : لأنه يقتضي تعلقه بالمكون، وتعلقه بالمكون يوجب حدوث المكون لا قدمه على ما مر أن ما تعلق وجوده بغيره فهو محدث غير قديم ومن يقيم على دعواه دليلا يوجب ذلك بطلان دعواه، كان غير بصير بالمحاجة.
And the caused thing (al-mukawwan) is mentioned [to specify] the times of those things, lest one imagine the eternity of those things. And all of this reverts to preserving propriety in expression, and adhering to this way when disagreement occurs over realities (al-haqa'iq), not over words and expressions, to which bewilderment and incapacity drive one. And the answer has come forth to what they mentioned after this: that if al-takwin (the act of bringing into existence) were eternal, then al-mukawwan (the brought-into-existence thing) would be eternal, because it necessitates its relation to al-mukawwan. We said: And if the address 'Be' were eternal, then the object of 'Be' would be eternal; and if the will (al-irada) were eternal, then the object of will (al-murad) would be eternal. And since the eternity of the address 'Be' and the eternity of the will did not necessitate the eternity of what they relate to, so likewise this. Then we say: Indeed, this speech of yours is contradictory, impossible, some of it nullifies others. That is because your saying: 'If al-takwin were eternal, then al-mukawwan would be eternal' – you stated this proposition and have no proof for it, and it is denied. Then you intended to prove this proposition by what necessitates its invalidity, because you gave as a reason for proving this proposition, saying: 'Because it necessitates its relation to al-mukawwan,' and its relation to al-mukawwan necessitates the origination (huduth) of al-mukawwan, not its eternity, as has passed that whatever's existence is dependent on another is originated (muhdath), not eternal. And whoever adduces for his claim a proof that necessitates the invalidity of his claim is not skilled in disputation.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation regarding the divine attribute of takwin (bringing into existence). The opponent argues that if takwin is eternal, then the mukawwan (the thing brought into existence) must also be eternal, because takwin requires a relation to its object. Imam Nasafi responds in two ways. First, by analogy: the divine command 'Be' and the divine will are also eternal, yet their objects (the things commanded and willed) are not eternal. Thus, the opponent's premise fails. Second, he points out an internal contradiction: the opponent's own reasoning—that relation to mukawwan implies its eternity—is actually false because, as previously established, anything whose existence depends on another is originated (hadith), not eternal. Hence, the opponent's proof actually undermines his claim, showing he is unskilled in argumentation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a cause is eternal, its effect must also be eternal.
Correction: An eternal cause can have a temporal effect; as seen with the eternal command 'Be' and the eternal will, whose objects are not eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Nasafi, what is the problem with the opponent's argument that if takwin is eternal then the mukawwan is eternal?
A) It contradicts the eternal nature of takwin.
B) It relies on an invalid analogy from the witnessed to the unseen.
C) It fails to consider that the command 'Be' and the will are also eternal without their objects being eternal.
D) It assumes that takwin is a separate entity.

Key Takeaway
An eternal cause does not necessitate an eternal effect; the opponent's argument is refuted by analogy and shown to be self-contradictory.

Will you now take time to seek the haqiqah behind the words you use in worship and reflection?
?
Imam Nasafi shows that a flawed argument can be self-refuting. Consider an area of your faith where you feel uncertain or troubled by a doubt. Evidence for the Eternality of Takwin and Its Acceptance
Nasafi presents a conditional argument distinguishing accepted and disputed claims about takwin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rhetorical structure of Nasafi's argument for the eternality of takwin and how it distinguishes between accepted and disputed claims.

Core Idea
The evidence for takwin's eternality is accepted in principle; the dispute is over its universal application.

Imam Nasafi presents a crisp logical challenge to his opponent. He asserts that the evidence for the eternality of takwin is already conceded, but then draws a distinction: acceptance depends on what exactly the opponent means by the claim. This short passage is a masterclass in framing a theological dispute precisely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice that it is a direct response to an opponent.
The passage is a single sentence but contains a conditional structure.
2
Identify the two conditional branches
Look for the phrase 'if you mean by it in every case' (وَإِنْ عَنَيْتَ بِهِ فِي كُلِّ حَالٍ). Nasafi is distinguishing two possible interpretations of the opponent's claim.
The first branch is implied: if the opponent means the general evidence, it is accepted.
3
Understand the phrase 'the same evidence indicates the whole matter'
Nasafi argues that the proof for takwin's eternality is part of a broader argument that the opponent cannot reject without contradiction. This ties the specific case to a general principle.
This is a common kalam strategy: showing that accepting the general implies accepting the specific.
4
Reflect on the point of dispute (mahall al-niza')
Nasafi identifies the precise point of disagreement: it is not the evidence itself, but whether it applies universally. This clarifies the debate.
Defining the mahall al-niza' is crucial in theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول له: قد قام الدليل على أن التكوين أزلي، وعين ذلك الدليل يدل الجملة فمسلم ونحن نقول به، وإن عنيت به في كل حال فهو غير مسلم وهو محل النزاع، فلم قلت ذلك؟
Then we say to him: The evidence has established that al-takwin is eternal, and that same evidence indicates the whole matter, so it is accepted and we affirm it. But if you mean by it in every case, it is not accepted – and it is the point of dispute – so why do you say that?
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This short passage is a rhetorical challenge. Imam Nasafi claims that the evidence for the eternality of takwin is clear and accepted by the opponent as well ('so it is accepted and we affirm it'). He then questions the opponent: if you accept the evidence in general but deny it specifically (i.e., for takwin), then you need to provide a reason. The phrase 'the same evidence indicates the whole matter' means that the proof for the eternality of takwin is part of a broader argument that the opponent cannot reject without contradiction.

Key Terms
daliil
takwin
musallam
mahall al-niza'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent outright rejects the evidence for the eternality of takwin.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that the opponent actually accepts the evidence in principle but denies its application to this specific case.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Nasafi say about the evidence for the eternality of takwin?
A) It is weak and must be rejected.
B) It is only based on the witnessed world.
C) It is accepted by both parties, but the opponent may mean it is not applicable in every case.
D) It is derived from the Quran alone.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's argument hinges on forcing the opponent to specify their claim, revealing that the evidence is already accepted in one sense and contested only in another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Decisive Dialectical Argument
Nasafi presents a decisive dialectical argument that forces the opponent into a contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi's decisive argument forces opponents into a logical contradiction regarding the relation of the world's existence to God's essence and attributes.

Core Idea
A logical dilemma forces opponents to either deny God's creation or admit a contradiction.

Nasafi presents a powerful dialectical argument that cuts through all sophistry. By asking a simple question about the relation of the world to God, he exposes inconsistencies in opposing views. This argument is a masterclass in logical reasoning in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the opening question
Carefully read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the argument begins with a direct question about the world's relation to God's essence and attributes.
2
Trace the two possible answers and their consequences
Follow the 'no' and 'yes' branches. Understand why saying 'no' leads to denying God as creator.
This is the first layer of the dilemma.
3
Analyze the follow-up about eternal vs. originated
If 'yes', the opponent is asked whether the relation is eternal (qadim) or originated (hadith). Explore both sub-branches.
Each sub-branch leads to a further dilemma.
4
Observe how the argument forces a contradiction
Notice that whether the opponent chooses eternal or originated, they end up contradicting their own position or admitting the world's eternity.
This is the decisive nature of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يقطع شغب الخصوم بأسرهم أن يقال: هل لوجود العالم تعلق بذات الباري وبصفة من صفاته؟ فإن قالوا: لا أنكروا كونه صانعا للعالم. وإن قالوا: نعم. قيل: أقديم ذلك المعنى أم محدث؟ فإن قالوا: محدث. سئلوا عن تعلق حدوثه بالباري أو بصفة من صفاته، فإن جوزوا وجود حادث لا بمحدث يلزمهم مثله في كل العالم. فإن قالوا: هو قديم. قيل: هل أوجب قدمه قدم العالم؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. فقد أقروا بقدم العالم، والتزموا ما ألزموا خصومهم، وإن قالوا: لا. أبطلوا دليلهم.
And that which cuts off the sophistry of the opponents entirely is that it is said: Does the existence of the world have a relation to the Essence of the Creator (al-Bari') and to some of His attributes? If they say: No – they deny that He is the Maker of the world. And if they say: Yes – it is said: Is that meaning eternal or originated? If they say: Originated – they are asked about the relation of its origination to the Creator or to some of His attributes. For if they allow an originated thing without an originator, the like of it would be incumbent upon them in the entire world. If they say: It is eternal – it is said: Did its eternity necessitate the eternity of the world? If they say: Yes – then they have affirmed the eternity of the world and accepted what they imputed to their opponents. And if they say: No – they have nullified their own evidence.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage presents a decisive argument (al-qati') that silences all sophistry. It is a logical dilemma. The opponent is first asked whether the world's existence has any relation (ta'allaq) to God's essence or attributes. If they deny it, they deny that God is the creator. If they affirm it, they are asked whether that relation (ma'na) is eternal (qadim) or originated (muhdath). If they say it is originated, they must explain what originated it, leading to an infinite regress. If they say it is eternal, they are asked whether this eternal relation necessitates the eternity of the world. If they say yes, they admit the world's eternity, which they oppose. If they say no, they undermine their own argument that an eternal cause (like takwin) must have an eternal effect.

Key Terms
dhat
sifat
qadim
hadith
ta'allaq
sani'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the relation between God and the world is a single concept without distinguishing between eternal and originated.
Correction: Nasafi forces a precise distinction: the relation (ma'na) must either be eternal or originated, and each leads to a contradiction for the opponent.

Checkpoint
In the decisive argument, if the opponent says the world's relation to God's essence and attributes is eternal, what follows?
A) It proves that God is the creator.
B) It forces them to either accept the world's eternity or nullify their own evidence.
C) It shows that the relation is only metaphorical.
D) It demonstrates that takwin is not an attribute.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's decisive argument uses a logical dilemma to force opponents into acknowledging either God's creative role or a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If someone asked you to prove that God created the world, would you rely on this logical argument or something else? Are you prepared to defend your faith rationally? This lesson forces you to see that denying any relationship between God and the world is equivalent to denying His creation. How does this challenge your own assumptions about God's involvement in your daily life?
?
The argument shows that any attempt to separate God from His creation leads to contradiction. In what ways do you live as if your actions are independent of God? Does your heart truly believe that every originated thing has an originator? If so, why do you sometimes act as if things happen by chance?
?
This logical dilemma is decisive against all sophistry. Are you equipped with such arguments in your own mind? Or do doubts linger? Commit to memorizing this argument and using it when your faith is tested.

God's Act Does Not Fall Under Will and Power
Nasafi refutes that God's act falls under will and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Maturidi theology, divine will and power apply to the object of an act, not the act itself.

Core Idea
Divine will and power move objects, not acts.

This passage from Nasafi clarifies a key distinction in divine attributes: when we say God wills to create, the will applies to the existence of the creation, not to the act of creating. This linguistic nuance protects divine transcendence and avoids attributing temporal change to God. Let's read the text carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its literal English translation. Notice the concise style of Nasafi's refutation.
The passage is one sentence long but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the claim and refutation
Identify what the opponents claim and how Nasafi rejects it. The key word is 'mamnu'' (rejected). Then note what he affirms: only the object (al-maf'ul) falls under will and power.
3
Understand the linguistic expansion example
Nasafi gives an example: 'He willed to bring the world into existence' – the true meaning is 'He willed its existence'. The phrase 'bring into existence' is a broadening of expression. Reflect on why language can be flexible in theological discourse.
This shows that the act itself (ijad) is not the direct object of will, but the existence (wujud) is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أن فعله تعالى يدخل تحت الإرادة والقدرة. ممنوع، إنما الداخل فيهما المفعول ولو قرن بما هو فعله نحو قولهم أراد إيجاد العالم. فمعناه أراد وجوده غير أنه قرن بالإيجاد توسعا في العبارة، وكذا القدرة على هذا.
And what they claim that His act, exalted be He, falls under will and power is rejected. Only the object acted upon falls under them. Even if it is coupled with what is His act, like their saying 'He willed to bring the world into existence' – its meaning is 'He willed its existence', except that it is coupled with 'bringing into existence' as a broadening of expression. And likewise power is upon this.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that God's act (fi'l) itself falls under His will and power. Nasafi states that this claim is rejected. Instead, only the object of the act (al-maf'ul) falls under will and power. He clarifies that when people say 'He willed to bring the world into existence', the actual meaning is 'He willed the world's existence'. The phrase 'bring into existence' is merely a linguistic expansion, not a distinct object of will. The same applies to power: power pertains to the object, not the act itself.

Key Terms
الفعل (fi'l)
المفعول (maf'ul)
الإرادة (irada)
القدرة (qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine will and power directly affect the act itself (e.g., the act of creating).
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that will and power only affect the object (the created thing), not the act. The act is a description of God, not an object of His will.

Checkpoint
What falls under divine will and power according to Nasafi?
A) God's act itself
B) The object acted upon
C) Both act and object
D) Neither act nor object

Key Takeaway
Divine will and power pertain to the object of an act, not the act itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you find yourself asking Allah to 'do' something (e.g., 'change my heart') or do you ask for the outcome (e.g., 'grant me a soft heart')? Since His will attaches to the object, does this lesson challenge you to rephrase your supplications to focus on the object you seek? How will you adjust your du'a starting today?
?
This passage distinguishes between the act of creating and the existence of creation. In your own life, are you more concerned with the act of worship (the motions, the words) or with the reality of worship (connecting to Allah)? Which aspect does your heart truly value? What will you do to align your focus with the object—Allah's pleasure—rather than the act?

Refuting the Mu'tazili Criterion for Attributes of Action
Nasafi presents a dilemma refuting the Mu'tazili distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazili criterion that attributes falling under God's power are attributes of action leads to logical contradictions.

Core Idea
What falls under power cannot separate essence from action attributes.

The Mu'tazila proposed a neat rule: if an attribute can be brought into being by God's power, it is an attribute of action; if not, it is an attribute of essence. But this rule fails when pressed on God's power to not create. Nasafi uses a clever dilemma to show that the rule leads to absurdities, exposing its invalidity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazili criterion
Read the first sentence that states the Mu'tazili claim: whatever falls under power is an attribute of action, otherwise it is of essence. This is the target of the refutation.
Notice how the criterion seems intuitive but is about to be challenged.
2
Follow the first horn of the dilemma
The refutation asks: does God have power to not create? If they say no, then creation/takwin is not under power, so it must be an attribute of essence. This undercuts their distinction.
This horn shows that the criterion would make creation an essence attribute, which the Mu'tazila would deny.
3
Follow the second horn
If they say yes, they are asked whether leaving off creation is an act. If yes, then God was leaving off creation in pre-eternity, so an act exists eternally, which they reject. If no, then power is over something not an act, contradicting their own rule.
Both sub-options lead to contradictions, invalidating the criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعم المعتزلة في الفرق بين صفة الذات وصفة الفعل إنما يدخل تحت القدرة فهو صفة فعل، وما لا يدخل تحت القدرة فهو صفة ذات. يجاب عنه بهذا . ثم يقال لهم: أيوصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على أن لا يخلق الخلق؟ فإن قالوا: لا. ثبت أن الخلق والتكوين صفة ذات يقتضيه فرقة إذ لم يوصف بالقدرة على أن لا يخلق وإن قالوا: نعم يوصف بالقدرة على أن لا يخلق قيل لهم: أتجعلون ترك التخليق فعلا أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. لزم وجود الفعل في الأزل إذ في الأزل كان تاركا لخلق العالم. وإن قالوا: لا، فقد أثبتوا القدرة على ما ليس بفعل، فبطل قولهم: إن ما يدخل تحت القدرة فهو صفة فعل.
And what the Mu'tazila claim in the distinction between attribute of essence and attribute of action: 'Whatever falls under power is an attribute of action, and whatever does not fall under power is an attribute of essence.' It is answered with this. Then it is said to them: Is God, exalted be He, described with power to not create the creation? If they say: No, it is established that creation and takwin are an attribute of essence that necessitates it, since He is not described with power to not create. And if they say: Yes, He is described with power to not create, it is said to them: Do you consider leaving off creation an act or not? If they say: Yes, then the existence of the act in pre-eternity is necessitated, since in pre-eternity He was leaving off creating the world. And if they say: No, then they have affirmed power over what is not an act, so their statement that 'whatever falls under power is an attribute of action' is invalidated.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazili distinction between attributes of essence and attributes of action based on falling under power. Nasafi first states the Mu'tazili claim: whatever falls under power is an attribute of action; otherwise it is of essence. He then presents a refutation by asking whether God has the power to not create. If they say no, then creation/takwin must be an attribute of essence, since it is not under power. If they say yes, then they must decide whether leaving off creation is an act. If they say yes, then God would have performed that act in pre-eternity (since He was always not creating), leading to an eternal act. If they say no, then they have affirmed power over something that is not an act, which contradicts their own criterion. Either way, their distinction fails.

Key Terms
صفة الذات (sifat al-dhat)
صفة الفعل (sifat al-fi'l)
القدرة (qudra)
التخليق (takhlīq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazili criterion that attributes under power are acts is a valid way to distinguish essence from action attributes.
Correction: This passage shows that the criterion leads to contradictions when applied to God's power to not create, thus it is invalid.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of the dilemma in Nasafi's refutation?
A) If the Mu'tazila say God has no power to not create, then creation is an attribute of essence.
B) If the Mu'tazila say God has power to not create, then leaving off creation must be an act.
C) If the Mu'tazila say leaving off creation is not an act, then they have power over a non-act.
D) If the Mu'tazila say creation is an attribute of action, then it must fall under power.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili distinction between essence and action attributes based on falling under power is invalid because it leads to contradictions about God's power to not create.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own understanding of Allah's attributes, do you rely on any unexamined assumptions that might be as inconsistent as the Mu'tazili criterion? What would it cost you to honestly question them?
?
This lesson reveals that even sincere theologians can be trapped by logical contradictions. In what areas of your faith might you be holding onto a 'reasonable' idea that actually clashes with revelation? Will you dare to revisit it?
?
The Mu'tazila wanted to protect Allah's transcendence, but their method led to absurdities. Think of a time you tried to 'protect' Allah's honor by over-rationalizing. Can you repent from that and submit to the revealed texts, even when they don't fit your logic?

The Mu'tazili Criterion of Negation and Affirmation and Its Refutation
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazili criterion and objects using Quran 10:18.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili criterion for distinguishing attributes of action from attributes of essence and how Nasafi refutes it using the Quranic verse about knowledge.

Core Idea
What can be denied is not necessarily an attribute of action.

The Mu'tazila proposed a simple rule: if an attribute can be both affirmed and negated of God, it is an attribute of action; if it can only be affirmed (not negated), it is an attribute of essence. Nasafi challenges this with a verse that seems to negate God's knowledge. This lesson examines that challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili criterion
Identify the rule: whatever is negated and affirmed is attribute of action; whatever is only affirmed is attribute of essence.
2
Examine the Quranic verse
Read the verse (Quran 10:18) and see how it implies a negation of knowledge.
The verse says 'do you inform Allah of what He does not know'.
3
Understand the dilemma
Nasafi asks: Is knowledge an attribute of essence or action? Either answer breaks the Mu'tazili position.
If action, they abandon their school; if essence, their criterion fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: إن ما ينفى ويثبت فهو صفة فعل، وما يثبت ولا ينفى فهو صفة ذات. فنقول: أليس أن الله تعالى قال: قُلْ أَتُنبِتُونَ اللَّهَ بِمَا لَا يَعْلَمُ فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَلَا فِي الْأَرْضِ فلابد من بلى. قيل لهم : ما تقولون فى العلم أصفة ذات هو أم صفة فعل؟ فإن قالوا: صفة فعل تركوا مذهبهم. وإن قالوا صفة ذات أبطلوا دليلهم. فإن قيل المراد منه نفي ذلك المذكور لا نفي العلم. قيل: عندنا إذا قيل : لم يرزق فلانا مالا، المراد منه نفي المال لا نفي صفته وكذا في قوله : لم يخلق لفلان ولدا المراد منه نفي الولد لا نفي الصفة عن ذاته. وفي الشاهد إنما يفهم من مثل هذا نفي الصفة لما مر من وجوب اتصال الفعل بالمفعول، فإذا نفي المفعول انتفى الفعل ضرورة، وفي الغائب الأمر بخلافه. ومن دأب خصومنا التسوية بين الشاهد والغائب من غير دليل يوجب التسوية، والنظر في الأشياء من حيث الظاهر دون مراعاة الحقائق ثم يقال للمعتزلة : أليس أنكم
And what they said: 'Whatever is negated and affirmed is an attribute of action, and whatever is affirmed and not negated is an attribute of essence.' So we say: Does not God, exalted be He, say: 'Say, do you inform Allah of what He does not know in the heavens and the earth?' (Quran 10:18) — so there must be a 'yes'. It is said to them: What do you say about knowledge? Is it an attribute of essence or an attribute of action? If they say: Attribute of action, they abandon their madhhab. If they say: Attribute of essence, they invalidate their proof. If it is said: The intended meaning is the negation of that mentioned thing, not the negation of knowledge. It is said: According to us, when it is said 'He did not provide so-and-so with wealth', the intended meaning is the negation of wealth, not the negation of His attribute; and likewise in His saying 'He did not create a son for so-and-so', the intended meaning is the negation of the son, not the negation of the attribute from His essence. But in the witnessed, from such expressions the negation of the attribute is understood because of the previously mentioned necessity of the connection of the act to the object; so when the object is negated, the act is necessarily negated. But in the absent, the matter is contrary to that. And it is the habit of our opponents to equate the witnessed and the absent without any evidence requiring equality, and to consider things from their outward appearance without regard to realities. Then it is said to the Mu'tazila: Do you not...
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage refutes another Mu'tazili criterion for distinguishing attributes: what can be both negated and affirmed is an attribute of action, while what is only affirmed (cannot be negated) is attribute of essence. Nasafi applies this to the attribute of knowledge. He cites the Quranic verse (10:18) which negates that God knows certain things. If the Mu'tazila say knowledge is an attribute of action because it can be negated (as in the verse), they abandon their own school (which considers knowledge as essence attribute). If they say it is attribute of essence, then their criterion fails because the verse negates knowledge. Then he addresses an objection: perhaps the negation is only of specific objects, not of knowledge itself. Nasafi explains that in the witnessed world, when we say 'He did not create a son', we negate the act of creation because the act is necessarily connected to its object. But in the divine (absent) case, negation of the object does not negate the attribute, because God's attributes are eternal and not dependent on objects. The opponents erroneously equate the witnessed and absent without proof.

Key Terms
النفي (nafy)
الإثبات (ithbat)
صفة الذات (sifat al-dhat)
صفة الفعل (sifat al-fi'l)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an attribute can be negated in the Quran, it must be an attribute of action.
Correction: The verb can be negated because of the object, not the attribute itself; the attribute of knowledge remains.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does Nasafi pose to the Mu'tazila regarding knowledge?
A) If knowledge is an attribute of action, they abandon their school; if it is essence, their criterion fails.
B) If knowledge is an attribute of essence, they must prove it; if action, they must deny it.
C) The verse contradicts the Quran, so they must reinterpret it.
D) Knowledge cannot be negated at all.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili criterion fails because it would classify knowledge as an attribute of action, contradicting their own position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's knowledge, do you ever subconsciously limit it to what He chooses to know? How does this verse (10:18) challenge your own assumptions about divine omniscience? Commit today to affirming that God knows all things, even those He does not act upon.
?
The Mu'tazila tried to define God by what can be negated. Are there ways you define God by negatives instead of letting the Quran describe Him? Commit to learning one attribute of God this week that is affirmed in the Quran without negation.
?
In your daily life, do you ever find yourself saying 'God would not do that' based on your own reasoning? Reflect on how this lesson shows the danger of imposing human criteria on God. Resolve to submit your intellect to revelation when it comes to divine attributes.

The Distinction Between Witnessed and Absent in Negation of Attributes
Nasafi explains that in the witnessed world negating the object negates the act, but in the absent it does not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why negation in the divine realm does not negate the attribute itself, unlike in the human realm.

Core Idea
In the divine, negating the object does not negate the attribute.

Nasafi anticipated an objection: maybe the negation in the verse is not of the attribute of knowledge itself but of its associated objects. He responds by explaining that in human experience, negating the object negates the act, but in the divine, the attribute remains. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The Mu'tazila might say the negation in the verse is about the objects, not the attribute itself.
2
Learn Nasafi's response
He uses examples: 'He did not provide wealth' negates wealth, not the attribute of provision.
In human language, negating the object implies negating the act.
3
Grasp the shahid/gha'ib distinction
In the witnessed world, act and object are necessarily connected; in the divine, they are not.
The Mu'tazila wrongly equate the two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: إن ما ينفى ويثبت فهو صفة فعل، وما يثبت ولا ينفى فهو صفة ذات. فنقول: أليس أن الله تعالى قال: قُلْ أَتُنبِتُونَ اللَّهَ بِمَا لَا يَعْلَمُ فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَلَا فِي الْأَرْضِ فلابد من بلى. قيل لهم : ما تقولون فى العلم أصفة ذات هو أم صفة فعل؟ فإن قالوا: صفة فعل تركوا مذهبهم. وإن قالوا صفة ذات أبطلوا دليلهم. فإن قيل المراد منه نفي ذلك المذكور لا نفي العلم. قيل: عندنا إذا قيل : لم يرزق فلانا مالا، المراد منه نفي المال لا نفي صفته وكذا في قوله : لم يخلق لفلان ولدا المراد منه نفي الولد لا نفي الصفة عن ذاته. وفي الشاهد إنما يفهم من مثل هذا نفي الصفة لما مر من وجوب اتصال الفعل بالمفعول، فإذا نفي المفعول انتفى الفعل ضرورة، وفي الغائب الأمر بخلافه. ومن دأب خصومنا التسوية بين الشاهد والغائب من غير دليل يوجب التسوية، والنظر في الأشياء من حيث الظاهر دون مراعاة الحقائق ثم يقال للمعتزلة : أليس أنكم
And what they said: 'Whatever is negated and affirmed is an attribute of action, and whatever is affirmed and not negated is an attribute of essence.' So we say: Does not God, exalted be He, say: 'Say, do you inform Allah of what He does not know in the heavens and the earth?' (Quran 10:18) — so there must be a 'yes'. If they say: Attribute of essence, they invalidate their proof. If it is said: The intended meaning is the negation of that mentioned thing, not the negation of knowledge. It is said: According to us, when it is said 'He did not provide so-and-so with wealth', the intended meaning is the negation of wealth, not the negation of His attribute; and likewise in His saying 'He did not create a son for so-and-so', the intended meaning is the negation of the son, not the negation of the attribute from His essence. But in the witnessed, from such expressions the negation of the attribute is understood because of the previously mentioned necessity of the connection of the act to the object; so when the object is negated, the act is necessarily negated. But in the absent, the matter is contrary to that. And it is the habit of our opponents to equate the witnessed and the absent without any evidence requiring equality, and to consider things from their outward appearance without regard to realities. Then it is said to the Mu'tazila: Do you not...
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage refutes another Mu'tazili criterion for distinguishing attributes: what can be both negated and affirmed is an attribute of action, while what is only affirmed (cannot be negated) is attribute of essence. Nasafi applies this to the attribute of knowledge. He cites the Quranic verse (10:18) which negates that God knows certain things. If the Mu'tazila say knowledge is an attribute of action because it can be negated (as in the verse), they abandon their own school (which considers knowledge as essence attribute). If they say it is attribute of essence, then their criterion fails because the verse negates knowledge. Then he addresses an objection: perhaps the negation is only of specific objects, not of knowledge itself. Nasafi explains that in the witnessed world, when we say 'He did not create a son', we negate the act of creation because the act is necessarily connected to its object. But in the divine (absent) case, negation of the object does not negate the attribute, because God's attributes are eternal and not dependent on objects. The opponents erroneously equate the witnessed and absent without proof.

Key Terms
الشاهد (shahid)
الغائب (gha'ib)
النفي (nafy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating an object in divine speech negates the divine attribute.
Correction: In the divine realm, attributes are independent of their objects, so negating the object does not negate the attribute.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does negating an object in the divine case not negate the attribute?
A) Because God's attributes are not connected to objects in the same way as in the witnessed world.
B) Because the Quranic verse is metaphorical.
C) Because the Mu'tazila always equate witnessed and absent.
D) Because knowledge cannot be negated at all.

Key Takeaway
In the divine, attributes are not contingent on their objects, so negating an object does not negate the attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God 'did not create a son,' does your heart instinctively suspect a limitation in His power? Where in your life have you judged God's actions by human standards, expecting Him to respond as people do? How will you actively remind yourself that His attributes are not tied to outcomes?
?
Think of a time when you felt God 'did not provide' for you. Did that experience shake your trust in His attribute of provision? How does this lesson’s distinction between the witnessed and the absent reshape your understanding of that moment? Commit to thanking Him for His unchangeable attributes even when you don’t see their effects.
?
The opponents considered only outward appearance without regard to realities. In what areas of your faith—such as understanding hardship, prayer not being answered, or the state of the Muslim ummah—are you tempted to judge by outward appearances? What specific step will you take this week to look beyond the surface and affirm the reality of God’s perfect attributes?
