Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Refuting the Power-Based Distinction
Refutes the power-based criterion for distinguishing attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite criterion that attributes of action fall under God's power leads to contradictions.

Core Idea
If creation is under God's power, refraining from creating becomes an eternal action, which is absurd.

The Mu'tazilites claim that any attribute falling under God's power is an attribute of action. The author begins his refutation by examining this claim through the example of creation. By asking whether God has power not to create, he exposes a dilemma that undermines their entire criterion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite criterion
Read the opening sentence stating that whatever falls under power is an attribute of action, and whatever does not is of essence.
2
Follow the first dilemma
The author asks: Is God described as having power not to create? Consider both possible answers and their consequences.
If they say no, creation becomes an attribute of essence. If they say yes, then refraining must be an action.
3
Analyze the refraining argument
If refraining is an action, it would have to exist eternally, which is problematic. If not, they affirm power over something that is not an action, contradicting their own statement.
Thus the criterion is invalidated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعم المعتزلة في الفرق بين صفة الذات وصفة الفعل إنما يدخل تحت القدرة فهو صفة فعل، وما لا يدخل تحت القدرة فهو صفة ذات. يجاب عنه بهذا . ثم يقال لهم: أيوصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على أن لا يخلق الخلق؟ فإن قالوا: لا. ثبت أن الخلق والتكوين صفة ذات يقتضيه فرقة إذ لم يوصف بالقدرة على أن لا يخلق وإن قالوا: نعم يوصف بالقدرة على أن لا يخلق قيل لهم: أتجعلون ترك التخليق فعلا أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. لزم وجود الفعل في الأزل إذ في الأزل كان تاركا لخلق العالم. وإن قالوا: لا، فقد أثبتوا القدرة على ما ليس بفعل، فبطل قولهم: إن ما يدخل تحت القدرة فهو صفة فعل. وما قالوا: إن ما ينفى ويثبت فهو صفة فعل، وما يثبت ولا ينفى فهو صفة ذات. فنقول: أليس أن الله تعالى قال: قُلْ أَتُنبِتُونَ اللَّهَ بِمَا لَا يَعْلَمُ فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَلَا فِي الْأَرْضِ فلابد من بلى. قيل لهم : ما تقولون فى العلم أصفة ذات هو أم صفة فعل؟ فإن قالوا: صفة فعل تركوا مذهبهم. وإن قالوا صفة ذات أبطلوا دليلهم. فإن قيل المراد منه نفي ذلك المذكور لا نفي العلم. قيل: عندنا إذا قيل : لم يرزق فلانا مالا، المراد منه نفي المال لا نفي صفته وكذا في قوله : لم يخلق لفلان ولدا المراد منه نفي الولد لا نفي الصفة عن ذاته. وفي الشاهد إنما يفهم من مثل هذا نفي الصفة لما مر من وجوب اتصال الفعل بالمفعول، فإذا نفي المفعول انتفى الفعل ضرورة، وفي الغائب الأمر بخلافه. ومن دأب خصومنا التسوية بين الشاهد والغائب من غير دليل يوجب التسوية، والنظر في الأشياء من حيث الظاهر دون مراعاة الحقائق ثم يقال للمعتزلة : أليس أنكم تقولون : إن الله تعالى يوصف بالقدرة على أفعال نفسه، ولا يوصف بالقدرة على أفعال غيره مع أنها مقدورة غيره؟ فلابد من بلى وإلا تركوا مذهبهم في مسألة خلق الأفعال. قيل لهم: فما تقولون أن القدرة صفة ذات أم صفة فعل؟ فإن قالوا: صفة فعل تركوا مذهبهم أنه قادر بذاته وإن قالوا صفة ذات أبطلوا فرقهم.
And what the Mu'tazilites claim regarding the distinction between the attribute of essence (ṣifat al-dhāt) and the attribute of action (ṣifat al-fiʿl) is: whatever falls under power (qudra) is an attribute of action, and whatever does not fall under power is an attribute of essence. It is answered with this. Then it is said to them: Is Allah described as having power not to create the creation? If they say: No. It is established that creation and bringing into being (takwīn) are an attribute of essence, necessitating their distinction, since He was not described as having power not to create. And if they say: Yes, He is described as having power not to create, it is said to them: Do you consider refraining from creating an action or not? If they say: Yes. Then the existence of the action in pre-eternity (al-azal) would be necessary, since in pre-eternity He was one who refrains from creating the world. And if they say: No, then they have affirmed power over what is not an action, so their statement that 'whatever falls under power is an attribute of action' is invalidated. And what they said: 'Whatever is negated and affirmed is an attribute of action, and whatever is affirmed and not negated is an attribute of essence.' So we say: Is it not that Allah said: 'Say: Do you inform Allah of what He does not know in the heavens and on earth?' (10:18) Then there must be a 'yes' (i.e., He does know). It is said to them: What do you say about knowledge (al-ʿilm): is it an attribute of essence or an attribute of action? If they say: Attribute of action, they abandon their school. If they say: Attribute of essence, they invalidate their argument. If it is said: The intended meaning is the negation of that mentioned (the object of knowledge), not the negation of knowledge. It is said: According to us, when it is said: 'He did not provide so-and-so wealth,' the intended meaning is the negation of the wealth, not the negation of His attribute. And similarly in His statement: 'He did not create for so-and-so a child,' the intended meaning is the negation of the child, not the negation of the attribute from His essence. In the witnessed (al-shāhid), the negation of the attribute is understood from such expressions due to the necessary connection of the act to the object; so when the object is negated, the act is necessarily negated. In the unseen (al-ghāʾib), the matter is otherwise. It is the practice of our opponents to equate the witnessed and the unseen without proof that necessitates equality, and to consider things based on outward appearance without regard to realities. Then it is said to the Mu'tazilites: Is it not that you say: Allah is described as having power over His own acts, and He is not described as having power over the acts of others, even though they are others' objects of power? Then there must be a 'yes', otherwise they abandon their school regarding the creation of acts. It is said to them: What do you say: is power an attribute of essence or an attribute of action? If they say: Attribute of action, they abandon their school that He is powerful (qādir) by His essence. If they say: Attribute of essence, they invalidate their distinction.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite distinction between attributes of essence and attributes of action. The author presents three arguments in sequence. First, he examines the Mu'tazilite claim that whatever is under God's power is an attribute of action. He asks: Is God described as having power not to create? If they say no, then creation is an attribute of essence because it is not under power (since they cannot claim He has power to refrain). If they say yes, then the author asks: Is refraining from creating an action? If yes, then refraining would have existed eternally, which is problematic. If no, then they have affirmed power over something that is not an action, contradicting their criterion. Second argument: The Mu'tazilites also say that whatever can be negated and affirmed is an attribute of action, while what is only affirmed is essence. The author uses Quran 10:18, forcing them to admit that God knows what He is informed of. He then asks: Is knowledge an attribute of essence or action? If they say action, they abandon their school because they consider knowledge an attribute of essence. If they say essence, their argument fails because the verse negates the object, not knowledge itself. The author explains the difference between the witnessed and unseen: in the witnessed, negating the object negates the act, but in the unseen it does not. The opponents wrongly equate them. Third argument: The Mu'tazilites claim God has power over His own acts but not over the acts of others. The author asks: Is power an attribute of essence or action? If they say action, they deny that God is powerful by His essence. If they say essence, they invalidate their distinction. Thus, all three arguments refute the Mu'tazilite position.

Key Terms
صفة الذات
صفة الفعل
القدرة
التكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any attribute under God's power must be an attribute of action.
Correction: The refutation shows this leads to absurdities, so the criterion is false.

Checkpoint
What is the outcome if the Mu'tazilites say God has power not to create but then deny that refraining is an action?
A) Their criterion is upheld.
B) They affirm power over a non-action, contradicting their own statement.
C) They admit refraining is an eternal action.
D) They concede that creation is an attribute of essence.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite power-based criterion is self-defeating because it leads to either admitting an eternal action or affirming power over non-actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider God's attributes, do you unconsciously limit His power to only His actions? How might this lesson correct that subtle error in your heart, and what will you do to align your belief with the truth?
?
If God's creation is an eternal attribute of His essence, how does that affect your sense of dependence on Him? Are you content knowing that He necessarily creates, or does that disturb your notion of His freedom? Examine your heart: what do you truly believe about why God created you?
?
The Mu'tazilites appeared to defend God's transcendence, but their criterion failed. What seemingly logical beliefs about God might you hold that actually undermine His perfection? Will you submit to revelation even when it challenges your reasoning?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Refuting the Negation-Based Distinction and the Argument from Power over Acts
Refutes the second and third Mu'tazilite criteria

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite distinction based on negatability and the claim about power over own acts are refuted using scriptural and logical arguments.

Core Idea
What can be negated is not necessarily an action; knowledge is never negated, yet it is not an action.

The Mu'tazilites also propose that an attribute that can be affirmed and negated is an attribute of action, while one that is only affirmed is of essence. The author refutes this by examining the attribute of knowledge using a Qur'anic verse. He then addresses their claim that God has power only over His own acts, and shows that this leads to contradictions about the nature of power itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second criterion
The Mu'tazilites say: whatever can be negated and affirmed is an attribute of action; what is only affirmed is essence. The author uses the verse 'Do you inform Allah of what He does not know?' to challenge this.
Note that the verse negates knowledge of specific objects, not knowledge itself.
2
Follow the argument about knowledge
The author asks whether knowledge is an attribute of essence or action. If they say action, they contradict their own school. If they say essence, their criterion fails because the verse appears to negate knowledge (though really it negates the object).
This shows the criterion is flawed.
3
Learn the shahid/gha'ib distinction
The author explains that in the witnessed world, negating the object negates the act, but in the unseen it does not. The opponents wrongly equate the two domains.
This distinction undermines their analogy.
4
Examine the third argument
The Mu'tazilites say God has power over His own acts but not over others' acts. The author asks: Is power an attribute of essence or action? Either answer leads to a contradiction.
If power is an action, God is not powerful by essence. If essence, their distinction collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعم المعتزلة في الفرق بين صفة الذات وصفة الفعل إنما يدخل تحت القدرة فهو صفة فعل، وما لا يدخل تحت القدرة فهو صفة ذات. يجاب عنه بهذا . ثم يقال لهم: أيوصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على أن لا يخلق الخلق؟ فإن قالوا: لا. ثبت أن الخلق والتكوين صفة ذات يقتضيه فرقة إذ لم يوصف بالقدرة على أن لا يخلق وإن قالوا: نعم يوصف بالقدرة على أن لا يخلق قيل لهم: أتجعلون ترك التخليق فعلا أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. لزم وجود الفعل في الأزل إذ في الأزل كان تاركا لخلق العالم. وإن قالوا: لا، فقد أثبتوا القدرة على ما ليس بفعل، فبطل قولهم: إن ما يدخل تحت القدرة فهو صفة فعل. وما قالوا: إن ما ينفى ويثبت فهو صفة فعل، وما يثبت ولا ينفى فهو صفة ذات. فنقول: أليس أن الله تعالى قال: قُلْ أَتُنبِتُونَ اللَّهَ بِمَا لَا يَعْلَمُ فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَلَا فِي الْأَرْضِ فلابد من بلى. قيل لهم : ما تقولون فى العلم أصفة ذات هو أم صفة فعل؟ فإن قالوا: صفة فعل تركوا مذهبهم. وإن قالوا صفة ذات أبطلوا دليلهم. فإن قيل المراد منه نفي ذلك المذكور لا نفي العلم. قيل: عندنا إذا قيل : لم يرزق فلانا مالا، المراد منه نفي المال لا نفي صفته وكذا في قوله : لم يخلق لفلان ولدا المراد منه نفي الولد لا نفي الصفة عن ذاته. وفي الشاهد إنما يفهم من مثل هذا نفي الصفة لما مر من وجوب اتصال الفعل بالمفعول، فإذا نفي المفعول انتفى الفعل ضرورة، وفي الغائب الأمر بخلافه. ومن دأب خصومنا التسوية بين الشاهد والغائب من غير دليل يوجب التسوية، والنظر في الأشياء من حيث الظاهر دون مراعاة الحقائق ثم يقال للمعتزلة : أليس أنكم تقولون : إن الله تعالى يوصف بالقدرة على أفعال نفسه، ولا يوصف بالقدرة على أفعال غيره مع أنها مقدورة غيره؟ فلابد من بلى وإلا تركوا مذهبهم في مسألة خلق الأفعال. قيل لهم: فما تقولون أن القدرة صفة ذات أم صفة فعل؟ فإن قالوا: صفة فعل تركوا مذهبهم أنه قادر بذاته وإن قالوا صفة ذات أبطلوا فرقهم.
And what the Mu'tazilites claim regarding the distinction between the attribute of essence (ṣifat al-dhāt) and the attribute of action (ṣifat al-fiʿl) is: whatever falls under power (qudra) is an attribute of action, and whatever does not fall under power is an attribute of essence. It is answered with this. Then it is said to them: Is Allah described as having power not to create the creation? If they say: No. It is established that creation and bringing into being (takwīn) are an attribute of essence, necessitating their distinction, since He was not described as having power not to create. And if they say: Yes, He is described as having power not to create, it is said to them: Do you consider refraining from creating an action or not? If they say: Yes. Then the existence of the action in pre-eternity (al-azal) would be necessary, since in pre-eternity He was one who refrains from creating the world. And if they say: No, then they have affirmed power over what is not an action, so their statement that 'whatever falls under power is an attribute of action' is invalidated. And what they said: 'Whatever is negated and affirmed is an attribute of action, and whatever is affirmed and not negated is an attribute of essence.' So we say: Is it not that Allah said: 'Say: Do you inform Allah of what He does not know in the heavens and on earth?' (10:18) Then there must be a 'yes' (i.e., He does know). It is said to them: What do you say about knowledge (al-ʿilm): is it an attribute of essence or an attribute of action? If they say: Attribute of action, they abandon their school. If they say: Attribute of essence, they invalidate their argument. If it is said: The intended meaning is the negation of that mentioned (the object of knowledge), not the negation of knowledge. It is said: According to us, when it is said: 'He did not provide so-and-so wealth,' the intended meaning is the negation of the wealth, not the negation of His attribute. And similarly in His statement: 'He did not create for so-and-so a child,' the intended meaning is the negation of the child, not the negation of the attribute from His essence. In the witnessed (al-shāhid), the negation of the attribute is understood from such expressions due to the necessary connection of the act to the object; so when the object is negated, the act is necessarily negated. In the unseen (al-ghāʾib), the matter is otherwise. It is the practice of our opponents to equate the witnessed and the unseen without proof that necessitates equality, and to consider things based on outward appearance without regard to realities. Then it is said to the Mu'tazilites: Is it not that you say: Allah is described as having power over His own acts, and He is not described as having power over the acts of others, even though they are others' objects of power? Then there must be a 'yes', otherwise they abandon their school regarding the creation of acts. It is said to them: What do you say: is power an attribute of essence or an attribute of action? If they say: Attribute of action, they abandon their school that He is powerful (qādir) by His essence. If they say: Attribute of essence, they invalidate their distinction.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite distinction between attributes of essence and attributes of action. The author presents three arguments in sequence. First, he examines the Mu'tazilite claim that whatever is under God's power is an attribute of action. He asks: Is God described as having power not to create? If they say no, then creation is an attribute of essence because it is not under power (since they cannot claim He has power to refrain). If they say yes, then the author asks: Is refraining from creating an action? If yes, then refraining would have existed eternally, which is problematic. If no, then they have affirmed power over something that is not an action, contradicting their criterion. Second argument: The Mu'tazilites also say that whatever can be negated and affirmed is an attribute of action, while what is only affirmed is essence. The author uses Quran 10:18, forcing them to admit that God knows what He is informed of. He then asks: Is knowledge an attribute of essence or action? If they say action, they abandon their school because they consider knowledge an attribute of essence. If they say essence, their argument fails because the verse negates the object, not knowledge itself. The author explains the difference between the witnessed and unseen: in the witnessed, negating the object negates the act, but in the unseen it does not. The opponents wrongly equate them. Third argument: The Mu'tazilites claim God has power over His own acts but not over the acts of others. The author asks: Is power an attribute of essence or action? If they say action, they deny that God is powerful by His essence. If they say essence, they invalidate their distinction. Thus, all three arguments refute the Mu'tazilite position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the negation of an object necessarily negates the attribute in the unseen, just as in the witnessed.
Correction: The author distinguishes between the witnessed and unseen; in the unseen, negating the object does not negate the attribute because there is no necessary connection like in the physical world.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what is the problem if the Mu'tazilites say that power is an attribute of action?
A) They would have to deny God's power.
B) They would contradict their belief that God is powerful by His essence.
C) They would admit that power is not an attribute.
D) They would invalidate their distinction.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite distinction fails on multiple fronts: the negation criterion is undermined by the nature of knowledge and the shahid/gha'ib distinction, and the power criterion leads to a dilemma about the attribute of power itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazilites built a system on the assumption that what applies to creation applies to God. Where in your own thinking about God might you be unconsciously imposing human limitations or patterns? Will you examine those assumptions today?
?
This lesson shows that negating an object does not necessarily negate the attribute in the unseen. Do you sometimes assume that because you don't see God's action in a situation, He is not acting? How can you train yourself to trust His attributes even when you don't see their effects?
?
The author refutes a criterion that seemed logical but was flawed. Are there beliefs or practices you hold that you have never questioned because they 'make sense' to you? Would you be willing to hold them up to the Quran and Sunnah, even if it means abandoning a comfortable understanding? Answer honestly: what would that cost you?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Refuting the Ash'ari Criterion with Justice and Bounty
First argument: dilemma with justice and bounty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi refutes the Ash'ari distinction between attributes of essence and action by applying it to justice and bounty.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari distinction collapses when applied to justice and bounty.

The Ash'aris define an attribute of essence as one whose negation implies a deficiency. The Maturidi author challenges this by asking about justice and bounty: can God be described as not just or not beneficent? This leads to a dilemma that undermines the Ash'ari criterion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari definition
Read the opening lines where the Ash'ari distinction is stated: an attribute of essence is one whose negation entails a deficiency, while an attribute of action does not.
Notice the logical structure: the definition is binary.
2
Follow the dilemma about justice and bounty
The author asks whether it is permissible to say God is not just or beneficent. Trace the two possible answers and their consequences.
If yes, they'd be declared unbelievers. If no, then according to their definition, justice and bounty must be attributes of essence.
3
Analyze the dilemma's conclusion
If they say justice and bounty are attributes of essence, they must affirm them eternally, which they deny. If they say they are attributes of action, they invalidate their own distinction because negation would not imply deficiency, contradicting their premise.
This shows the definition is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال للأشعرية ففرقكم بين صفة الذات وبين صفة الفعل أن ما يلزم بنفيه نقيصه فهو صفة ذات وما لا يلزم بنفيه نقيصة فهو صفة فعل. قيل لهم: هل يجوز أن يقال: إن الله تعالى ليس بعادل ولا متفضل؟ فإن قالوا: نعم تسارع الناس إلى إكفارهم. وإن قالوا: لا، قيل لهم ما تقولون أن الفضل والعدل من قبيل صفات الذات أم من قبيل صفات الفعل ؟ فإن قالوا من قبيل صفات الذات. تركوا مذهبهم في ذلك ولزمهم أن يصفوه في الأزل بالعدل والتفضل. وإن قالوا: من قبيل صفات الفعل أبطلوا فرقهم. ويقال لهم: أي نقيصة تلزم بنفي الكلام؟ فإن قالوا: العجز والآفة. قيل: ليس كل من لا يتكلم بمأوف ولا عاجز وإن قالوا: السكوت. قيل: السكوت ليس بآفة ربما يعد في الشاهد محمدة فضيلة وربما يعد الكلام رذيلة، ولهذا قال قائلهم : ما إن ندمت على سكوت مرةً ولقد ندمت على الكلام مرارًا فإن قالوا: لو قيل إنه تعالى موصوف بالسكوت إن كان لا يلزم بنفي السكوت
And it is said to the Ash'aris: Your distinction between the attribute of essence and the attribute of action is that whatever necessarily entails a deficiency by its negation is an attribute of essence, and whatever does not necessarily entail a deficiency by its negation is an attribute of action. It is said to them: Is it permissible to say: Allah is not just (ʿādil) and not beneficent (mutafaḍḍil)? If they say: Yes, then people would rush to declare them unbelievers. And if they say: No, it is said to them: What do you say: are bounty (al-faḍl) and justice (al-ʿadl) of the category of attributes of essence or of the category of attributes of action? If they say: Of the category of attributes of essence, they abandon their school in that, and it would be necessary for them to describe Him in pre-eternity (al-azal) with justice and bounty. And if they say: Of the category of attributes of action, they invalidate their distinction. And it is said to them: What deficiency would necessarily follow from the negation of speech (al-kalām)? If they say: Incapacity (al-ʿajz) and defect (al-āfa). It is said: Not everyone who does not speak is defective or incapable. And if they say: Silence (al-sukūt). It is said: Silence is not a defect; perhaps in the witnessed (al-shāhid) it is considered praiseworthy and a virtue, and perhaps speech is considered a vice. For this reason, one of their poets said: 'I have never regretted silence once, and I have regretted speech many times.' Then if they say: If it is said that He, exalted, is described with silence, then if it does not necessarily follow from the negation of silence...
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage refutes the Ash'ari distinction between attributes of essence and action. The Ash'aris define an attribute of essence as that whose negation implies a deficiency, and an attribute of action as that whose negation does not. The author presents two arguments. First, he asks about justice and bounty: Is it permissible to say God is not just or beneficent? If they say yes, they would be declared unbelievers. If they say no, then these must be attributes of essence according to their criterion (since negation is impermissible, implying deficiency). But if they are attributes of essence, then God would have them eternally, which the Ash'aris deny. If they say they are attributes of action, they invalidate their own distinction because then negation would not imply deficiency, contradicting their claim that negation is impermissible. Second, the author discusses speech. What deficiency follows from negating speech? If they say incapacity or defect, he replies that not being able to speak does not imply defect; many beings do not speak. If they say silence, he argues that silence is not a defect; in fact, it is often praiseworthy, while speech can be blameworthy. He quotes a poet. The argument cuts off, but the point is that negation of speech does not entail a deficiency, thus speech would be an attribute of action according to their criterion, which the Ash'aris reject because they consider speech an attribute of essence. Hence their distinction is invalid.

Key Terms
صفة الذات
صفة الفعل
العدل
التفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Ash'ari distinction is consistent and clear.
Correction: This passage shows it leads to absurdities when tested with justice and bounty.

Checkpoint
What happens if the Ash'aris say justice and bounty are attributes of action?
A) They must affirm them eternally.
B) They invalidate their own distinction.
C) They are declared unbelievers.
D) They must say God is not just.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari distinction between essence and action attributes fails when tested with justice and bounty, as it leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Allah that seemed logically consistent, only to realize it contradicted clear revelation? Are you willing to abandon such a belief, even if it's uncomfortable?
?
This passage shows that denying Allah's justice and bounty is tantamount to disbelief. Do you truly live as if Allah is perfectly just and generous in every situation, or does your heart sometimes doubt His goodness when faced with trials?
?
The Ash'ari dilemma reveals how human frameworks can distort divine attributes. What are some of your own assumptions about Allah that might be more rooted in human reasoning than in the Qur'an and Sunnah? Will you commit to examining them today?

Refuting the Ash'ari Claim about Speech and Deficiency
Second argument: speech and deficiency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi refutes the Ash'ari claim that speech is an attribute of essence by showing that its negation does not imply a deficiency.

Core Idea
Negation of speech does not imply deficiency; silence can be praiseworthy.

The author continues his refutation by examining the attribute of speech. The Ash'aris consider speech an attribute of essence because they believe its negation would imply a deficiency. The author challenges this by asking what deficiency would follow. He considers and refutes two possible answers: incapacity/defect and silence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on speech
Read the passage starting from 'ويقال لهم: أي نقيصة تلزم بنفي الكلام؟' and follow the argument.
The author poses a question and considers two answers.
2
Evaluate the first answer: incapacity
The Ash'aris might say negation of speech implies incapacity or defect. The author counters that not speaking does not necessarily entail defect; many beings are not speakers.
Think of animals or inanimate objects.
3
Evaluate the second answer: silence
The Ash'aris might say negation of speech is silence, which is a deficiency. The author rebuts by noting that silence is often praiseworthy, while speech can be blameworthy, citing a poet.
This shows the evaluation is context-dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال للأشعرية ففرقكم بين صفة الذات وبين صفة الفعل أن ما يلزم بنفيه نقيصه فهو صفة ذات وما لا يلزم بنفيه نقيصة فهو صفة فعل. قيل لهم: هل يجوز أن يقال: إن الله تعالى ليس بعادل ولا متفضل؟ فإن قالوا: نعم تسارع الناس إلى إكفارهم. وإن قالوا: لا، قيل لهم ما تقولون أن الفضل والعدل من قبيل صفات الذات أم من قبيل صفات الفعل ؟ فإن قالوا من قبيل صفات الذات. تركوا مذهبهم في ذلك ولزمهم أن يصفوه في الأزل بالعدل والتفضل. وإن قالوا: من قبيل صفات الفعل أبطلوا فرقهم. ويقال لهم: أي نقيصة تلزم بنفي الكلام؟ فإن قالوا: العجز والآفة. قيل: ليس كل من لا يتكلم بمأوف ولا عاجز وإن قالوا: السكوت. قيل: السكوت ليس بآفة ربما يعد في الشاهد محمدة فضيلة وربما يعد الكلام رذيلة، ولهذا قال قائلهم : ما إن ندمت على سكوت مرةً ولقد ندمت على الكلام مرارًا فإن قالوا: لو قيل إنه تعالى موصوف بالسكوت إن كان لا يلزم بنفي السكوت
And it is said to the Ash'aris: Your distinction between the attribute of essence and the attribute of action is that whatever necessarily entails a deficiency by its negation is an attribute of essence, and whatever does not necessarily entail a deficiency by its negation is an attribute of action. It is said to them: Is it permissible to say: Allah is not just (ʿādil) and not beneficent (mutafaḍḍil)? If they say: Yes, then people would rush to declare them unbelievers. And if they say: No, it is said to them: What do you say: are bounty (al-faḍl) and justice (al-ʿadl) of the category of attributes of essence or of the category of attributes of action? If they say: Of the category of attributes of essence, they abandon their school in that, and it would be necessary for them to describe Him in pre-eternity (al-azal) with justice and bounty. And if they say: Of the category of attributes of action, they invalidate their distinction. And it is said to them: What deficiency would necessarily follow from the negation of speech (al-kalām)? If they say: Incapacity (al-ʿajz) and defect (al-āfa). It is said: Not everyone who does not speak is defective or incapable. And if they say: Silence (al-sukūt). It is said: Silence is not a defect; perhaps in the witnessed (al-shāhid) it is considered praiseworthy and a virtue, and perhaps speech is considered a vice. For this reason, one of their poets said: 'I have never regretted silence once, and I have regretted speech many times.' Then if they say: If it is said that He, exalted, is described with silence, then if it does not necessarily follow from the negation of silence...

Commentary
This passage refutes the Ash'ari distinction between attributes of essence and action. The Ash'aris define an attribute of essence as that whose negation implies a deficiency, and an attribute of action as that whose negation does not. The author presents two arguments. First, he asks about justice and bounty: Is it permissible to say God is not just or beneficent? If they say yes, they would be declared unbelievers. If they say no, then these must be attributes of essence according to their criterion (since negation is impermissible, implying deficiency). But if they are attributes of essence, then God would have them eternally, which the Ash'aris deny. If they say they are attributes of action, they invalidate their own distinction because then negation would not imply deficiency, contradicting their claim that negation is impermissible. Second, the author discusses speech. What deficiency follows from negating speech? If they say incapacity or defect, he replies that not being able to speak does not imply defect; many beings do not speak. If they say silence, he argues that silence is not a defect; in fact, it is often praiseworthy, while speech can be blameworthy. He quotes a poet. The argument cuts off, but the point is that negation of speech does not entail a deficiency, thus speech would be an attribute of action according to their criterion, which the Ash'aris reject because they consider speech an attribute of essence. Hence their distinction is invalid.

The Dilemma of Divine Power: Essence or Action?
Objection to Ash'ari distinction regarding power as attribute of essence or action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari dilemma regarding whether God's power is an attribute of His essence or an attribute of action, and why it challenges their position on creation of actions.

Core Idea
God's power cannot be both essential and restricted — the Ash'ari face a logical dilemma.

This passage presents a powerful objection to the Ash'ari view that God has power over His own actions but not over the actions of others. The objector forces a choice: if power is an attribute of essence, it must apply universally; if it is an attribute of action, then God is not powerful by His essence. Let's see how this dilemma unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the passage carefully. Note how the objector begins by pointing out the inconsistency in the Ash'ari position: God is said to have power over His own acts but not over others' acts, even though those acts are possible for Him.
The objector claims this forces a 'yes' response.
2
Identify the Forced Choice
The objector then asks whether power (qudra) is an attribute of essence or of action. This is the crux of the dilemma: the Ash'ari must choose one horn.
3
Trace the Consequences
Follow each option: if they say it is an attribute of action, they contradict their doctrine that God is powerful by His essence. If they say it is an attribute of essence, they nullify their distinction between God's power over Himself and over others.
Both options are problematic for the Ash'ari.
4
Reflect on the Broader Debate
Consider how this dilemma ties into the larger debate about the creation of actions and divine attributes. Why does this matter for understanding God's nature?
This passage is a classic example of kalam dialectic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقولون : إن الله تعالى يوصف بالقدرة على أفعال نفسه، ولا يوصف بالقدرة على أفعال غيره مع أنها مقدورة غيره؟ فلابد من بلى وإلا تركوا مذهبهم في مسألة خلق الأفعال. قيل لهم: فما تقولون أن القدرة صفة ذات أم صفة فعل؟ فإن قالوا صفة فعل تركوا مذهبهم أنه قادر بذاته وإن قالوا صفة ذات أبطلوا فرقهم.
You say: Indeed Allah is described as having power over His own actions, and is not described as having power over the actions of others, while they are possible for others? So there must be a [response] of yes, otherwise they abandon their doctrine regarding the issue of the creation of actions. It was said to them: So what do you say—is power an attribute of essence or an attribute of action? If they say: an attribute of action, they abandon their doctrine that He is powerful by His essence; and if they say: an attribute of essence, they nullify their distinction.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the Ash'ari position on divine power. The objector begins by noting the Ash'ari claim that God is described as having power over His own acts but not over the acts of others, even though those acts are possible for Him. This inconsistency forces the Ash'ari to affirm (with 'yes') that God does have power over others' acts, or else abandon their doctrine of creation of actions. The argument then asks whether power is an attribute of essence or of action. If they say it is an attribute of action, they contradict their own view that God is powerful by His essence (qādir bi-dhātih). If they say it is an attribute of essence, then their distinction between God's power over His own acts and over others' acts collapses, because an essential attribute would apply universally. Thus the Ash'ari are trapped in a dilemma.

Key Terms
قدرة
صفة ذات
صفة فعل
قادر بذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dilemma is merely semantic and does not affect the Ash'ari position.
Correction: The dilemma rests on the metaphysical distinction between essential and accidental attributes. If power is essential, it must be universal; if it is accidental (action), then God is not essentially powerful. This has serious theological consequences.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the Ash'ari say that power (qudra) is an attribute of action (ṣifat fiʿl)?
A) They affirm that God is powerful by His essence.
B) They abandon their doctrine that God is powerful by His essence.
C) They maintain their distinction between power over self and others.
D) They are forced to deny the creation of actions.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari cannot consistently claim both that God is powerful by His essence and that His power applies only to His own acts; they must either abandon essential power or accept universal power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself believing God is all-powerful in theory, yet subconsciously limiting His control over certain areas of your life—like your future, your relationships, or your mistakes? How does this dilemma expose that inconsistency, and what specific area will you surrender to His universal power today?
?
If God's power were truly essential and unlimited, would you still take credit for your successes or blame yourself for failures as if you were the ultimate cause? How would viewing every outcome as directly from God change the way you react to both?
?
This lesson reveals a logical tension in a theological school. But what about your own faith—are you holding two contradictory beliefs about God's attributes without realizing it? Will you commit to examining your assumptions tonight, asking God to show you any inconsistency in your heart?

The Ash'ari Criterion and Its Refutation via Justice and Generosity
Logical refutation of Ash'ari distinction via justice and generosity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari criterion for distinguishing essence and action attributes is shown to be inconsistent when applied to justice and generosity.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari criterion self-destructs when tested on justice and generosity.

The passage presents a logical dilemma to the Ash'ari position. It first states their criterion, then asks a simple question about justice and generosity, leading to a contradiction. This lesson walks through the argument step by step, showing how the criterion fails.

Guided Path
1
State the Ash'ari criterion
Read the first part where the Ash'ari distinction is defined: an attribute whose negation entails deficiency is essential; otherwise it is an action attribute.
Understand the criterion clearly before testing it.
2
Pose the dilemma on justice and generosity
The objector asks: is it permissible to say God is not just and not generous? Consider both possible answers.
The question forces the Ash'ari to apply their own criterion.
3
Analyze the consequences of each answer
If they say yes, they risk disbelief. If they say no, then justice and generosity must be essential attributes according to their own rule.
Notice the catch: if no, then deficiency is implied if negated, so essential.
4
Conclude the inconsistency
The Ash'ari are forced either to admit justice and generosity are essential (which they deny) or to violate their own criterion. Thus their distinction is nullified.
The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مناقشة كلام الأشعرية في صفة الذات والفعل:
ويقال للأشعرية ففرقكم بين صفة الذات وبين صفة الفعل أن ما يلزم بنفيه نقيصه فهو صفة ذات وما لا يلزم بنفيه نقيصة فهو صفة فعل. قيل لهم: هل يجوز أن يقال: إن الله تعالى ليس بعادل ولا متفضل؟ فإن قالوا: نعم تسارع الناس إلى إكفارهم. وإن قالوا: لا، قيل لهم ما تقولون أن الفضل والعدل من قبيل صفات الذات أم من قبيل صفات الفعل ؟ فإن قالوا من قبيل صفات الذات. تركوا مذهبهم في ذلك ولزمهم أن يصفوه في الأزل بالعدل والتفضل. وإن قالوا: من قبيل صفات الفعل أبطلوا فرقهم.
A discussion of the Ash'ari statement regarding the attribute of essence and the attribute of action: And it is said to the Ash'aris: Your distinction between the attribute of essence and the attribute of action is that whatever entails deficiency by its negation is an attribute of essence, and whatever does not entail deficiency by its negation is an attribute of action. It was said to them: Is it permissible to say that Allah is not just and not generous? If they say: Yes, the people would hasten to declare them disbelievers. And if they say: No, it was said to them: What do you say—are generosity and justice of the category of attributes of essence or of the category of attributes of action? If they say: of the category of attributes of essence, they abandon their doctrine in that and it becomes necessary for them to describe Him in pre-eternity as just and generous. And if they say: of the category of attributes of action, they nullify their distinction.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage attacks the Ash'ari criterion for distinguishing attributes of essence from attributes of action. According to the Ash'aris, an attribute of essence is one whose negation entails a deficiency in God, while an attribute of action is one whose negation does not. The objector applies this criterion to the attributes of justice (ʿadl) and generosity (faḍl). He asks whether it is permissible to say that God is not just and not generous. If they say yes, they would be deemed disbelievers. If they say no, then the negation of justice and generosity would entail deficiency, so by their own criterion these should be attributes of essence. But the Ash'aris typically consider them attributes of action. If they admit they are essential, they must describe God in pre-eternity (azal) as just and generous, which conflicts with their view that such attributes arise only at the time of action. If they insist they are actions, they violate their own criterion and nullify the distinction. Thus the Ash'ari position is inconsistent.

Key Terms
صفة الذات
صفة الفعل
عدل
فضل
نقيصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari criterion is a neutral and consistent way to classify divine attributes.
Correction: This argument shows the criterion fails when applied to justice and generosity, because the Ash'aris themselves would not deny these of God, yet they consider them action attributes.

Checkpoint
What follows if the Ash'ari say that justice and generosity are attributes of essence?
A) They can still deny that God is just in pre-eternity.
B) They must describe God as just and generous in pre-eternity.
C) They nullify their own distinction between essence and action.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari criterion for distinguishing essence and action attributes is inconsistent when applied to justice and generosity, revealing a flaw in their classification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own understanding of God's attributes, are you willing to follow logical consequences wherever they lead, even if they challenge your preconceptions? How do you respond when a tension arises between what you hold and what reason dictates?
?
If you were to apply a criterion like the Ash'ari one to your own faith, would it reveal inconsistencies? Can you think of a belief you hold that might not survive a rigorous logical test?
?
The Ash'aris faced a dilemma: either affirm justice and generosity as eternal or abandon their criterion. Are you prepared to let go of a cherished school or opinion when the truth demands it? What would it cost you to change your position on a deeply held theological point?

Refuting the Claim That God's Silence Implies Deficiency
Refutation of the claim that silence implies deficiency; defense of God's perfection without speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi response to the Ash'ari argument that negating speech from God entails deficiency, and why the inability to do the impossible is not a defect.

Core Idea
Silence is not a defect; the inability to do the impossible is not a deficiency.

The Ash'ari argue that if God does not speak, He is deficient, implying incapacity or defect. But is that necessarily true? In this passage, the Maturidi theologian responds by examining the nature of silence and the limits of power. He shows that silence is often praiseworthy in the created world, and that lacking power over impossible things does not constitute a defect. This defense helps clarify the Maturidi view that speech is not an essential attribute of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and the English translation. Notice the back-and-forth dialectical structure: the Ash'ari objection, the Maturidi reply, a new objection, and another reply.
The passage includes a line of poetry praising silence.
2
Identify the first objection and its refutation
The Ash'ari first assert that negating speech implies incapacity or defect. The Maturidi counters that not every silent being is defective, and silence can even be praiseworthy.
The poetry quote supports this.
3
Examine the second objection and its refutation
The Ash'ari then argue that if negating silence is not a deficiency, then affirming silence (i.e., God being silent) would entail deficiency because God would be unable to command and forbid. The Maturidi replies that lacking power over impossible things (like commanding while silent) is not a deficiency.
The refutation uses the analogy of God's inability to combine opposites.
4
Understand the final conclusion
The passage concludes that since no deficiency arises from withholding speech, speech cannot be an essential attribute of God (by the Ash'ari's own criterion). Thus the distinction between essence and action attributes is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: أي نقيصة تلزم بنفي الكلام؟ فإن قالوا: العجز والآفة. قيل: ليس كل من لا يتكلم بمأوف ولا عاجز وإن قالوا: السكوت. قيل: السكوت ليس بآفة ربما يعد في الشاهد محمدة فضيلة وربما يعد الكلام رذيلة، ولهذا قال قائلهم : ما إن ندمت على سكوت مرةً ولقد ندمت على الكلام مرارًا
فإن قالوا: لو قيل إنه تعالى موصوف بالسكوت إن كان لا يلزم بنفي السكوت نقيصة فقد يلزم ضرورة ثبوته نقيصه وهو العجز ، فإنه تعالى لا يوصف بالقدرة مع بقاء السكوت على الأمر لعباده بالمحاسن والنهي لهم عن القبائح، ولا وجه إلى القول بقدم السكوت لأنه حينئذ يكون أزليا ويكون العدم عليه محالا.
قيل لهم زوال القدرة عما يستحيل ثبوته لقيام ضده وعن إعدام ما هو أزلي لا يعد عجزا ولا نقيصة، ألا ترى أنه تعالى لا يوصف بالقدرة على الجمع بين المتضادين ولا على إفناء ذاته وصفاته، ثم لا نقيصه تلحقه عندكم بتركه الأمر والنهي إذ ترك التكليف وإهمال الناس عندكم حكمة ، ولا حسن قبل الأمر ليصبر بترك الأمر به خارجا عن الحكمة، ولا قبيح ليصير بترك النهي عنه خارجا عن الحكمة، فإذا لا تلحقه النقيصة بترك الكلام أو انتفائه عن ذاته وهو مع صفات الذات فبطل الفرق بين الجانبين.
And it is said to them: What deficiency is entailed by the negation of speech? If they say: incapacity and defect. It is said: Not everyone who does not speak is defective or incapable. And if they say: silence. It is said: Silence is not a defect; perhaps in the witnessed world it is counted as praiseworthy virtue, and perhaps speech is counted as vice, and so one of their poets said: 'I have never regretted silence once, but I have regretted speech many times.' Then if they say: If it is said that He is described with silence, if the negation of silence does not entail deficiency, then necessarily its affirmation entails deficiency, which is incapability, for He cannot be described with power while silence remains regarding commanding His servants to good deeds and forbidding them from evil deeds. And there is no way to say that silence is pre-eternal, because then it would be eternal and non-existence would be impossible for it. It was said to them: The removal of power from what is impossible to exist due to the existence of its opposite, and from annihilating what is eternal, is not counted as incapacity or deficiency. Do you not see that He is not described as having power over combining opposites, nor over annihilating His essence and attributes? Then no deficiency attaches to Him according to you by His abandoning command and prohibition, since abandoning obligation and leaving people [free] is, according to you, wisdom. There is no good before the command such that refraining from commanding it would be outside wisdom, and no evil such that refraining from forbidding it would be outside wisdom. So if no deficiency attaches to Him by abandoning speech or its absence from His essence, and it is with the attributes of essence, then the distinction between the two sides is invalidated.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Ash'ari claim that negating the attribute of speech (kalām) from God entails a deficiency. The objector first asks what deficiency would result. If the Ash'ari say incapacity or defect, the objector replies that not being able to speak does not imply defect—many creatures are silent without being defective. If they propose that silence itself is a defect, the objector counters that silence is often praiseworthy, as the cited line of poetry indicates. The Ash'ari then shift their argument: if negating silence does not cause deficiency, then affirming silence would cause deficiency, because if God were silent, He could not command and forbid, implying incapacity. The objector replies that the loss of power over something impossible (like commanding while silent) is not a deficiency. Just as God cannot combine opposites or annihilate Himself, these impossibilities do not imply defect. Furthermore, according to Ash'ari principles, there is no good or evil prior to revelation, so refraining from command or prohibition is not necessarily against wisdom. Therefore, no deficiency arises from withholding speech. Since the negation of speech does not entail deficiency, speech cannot be an attribute of essence by their own criterion. Thus the Ash'ari distinction between essence and action attributes is invalidated.

Key Terms
كلام
سكوت
عجز
آفة

Correction: Silence can be praiseworthy, and inability to do impossible things is not a deficiency.

Are you willing to surrender the need to see immediate results and accept that His restraint itself may be a form of perfect wisdom?

Divine silence and the problem of deficiency
Nasafi presents an objection about divine silence and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine silence about commanding good and forbidding evil would imply deficiency, and how Nasafi refutes the objection that inability to do the impossible is always not a deficiency.

Core Idea
Not all inability is deficiency; silence on possible actions is.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a subtle objection: if God cannot be described as having power to do impossible things, then why would silence be a deficiency? After all, silence is just the absence of speech. Nasafi clarifies that the objection misses a crucial distinction: the relevant silence here is not about impossible actions but about possible ones—specifically, commanding good and forbidding evil. By unpacking this argument, we see how Maturidi theology carefully distinguishes between types of power and deficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Get a feel for the flow of the objection and the response.
2
Identify the objection and the refutation
The passage begins with an objection (lines 1–3) and then gives the refutation (starting from 'قيل لهم'). Mark where each part begins and ends.
The objection claims that silence would imply inability, but inability for impossible things is not a deficiency.
3
Understand the key distinction
The refutation hinges on distinguishing inability for impossible actions (e.g., combining opposites) from inability for possible actions (like commanding and forbidding). The author argues that only the latter is a true deficiency.
Notice how the author uses the example of God not being able to combine opposites to show that such inability is not a deficiency, but then argues that silence about commanding good and forbidding evil is different because those actions are possible for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقيصة فقد يلزم ضرورة ثبوته نقيصه وهو العجز ، فإنه تعالى لا يوصف بالقدرة مع بقاء السكوت على الأمر لعباده بالمحاسن والنهي لهم عن القبائح، ولا وجه إلى القول بقدم السكوت لأنه حينئذ يكون أزليا ويكون العدم عليه محالا. قيل لهم زوال القدرة عما يستحيل ثبوته لقيام ضده وعن إعدام ما هو أزلي لا يعد عجزا ولا نقيصة، ألا ترى أنه تعالى لا يوصف بالقدرة على الجمع بين المتضادين ولا على إفناء ذاته وصفاته، ثم لا نقيصه تلحقه عندكم بتركه الأمر والنهي إذ ترك التكليف وإهمال الناس عندكم حكمة ، ولا حسن قبل الأمر ليصبر بترك الأمر به خارجا عن الحكمة، ولا قبيح ليصير بترك النهي عنه خارجا عن الحكمة، فإذا لا تلحقه النقيصة بترك الكلام أو انتفائه عن ذاته وهو مع صفات الذات فبطل الفرق بين الجانبين.
A deficiency, for inevitably upon its establishment a deficiency is necessary, which is inability. For He, exalted, is not described with power while silence remains regarding commanding His servants to good and forbidding them from evil. And there is no way to say that silence is eternal because then it would be eternal and non-existence would be impossible for Him. It was said to them: The removal of power over what is impossible to establish due to the existence of its opposite, and over the annihilation of what is eternal, is not considered inability or deficiency. Do you not see that He, exalted, is not described with power to combine opposites nor to annihilate His essence and attributes, yet no deficiency attaches to Him according to you by His leaving command and prohibition, since leaving obligation and neglecting people is wisdom according to you. There is no good before the command to become by leaving its command outside wisdom, and no evil to become by leaving its prohibition outside wisdom. So no deficiency attaches to Him by leaving speech or its negation from His essence, while it is among the attributes of essence. Thus the distinction between the two sides is invalid.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that inability to do impossible things is not a deficiency. The author (Nasafi) refutes by arguing that while inability to do the impossible (e.g., combining opposites) is indeed not a deficiency, the silence in question would involve not commanding good and forbidding evil, which are possible actions for God. Thus, such silence would imply deficiency. The author uses the example of God's power: God is not described as having power to combine opposites or annihilate Himself, yet this does not entail deficiency. Similarly, according to the opponents, leaving command and prohibition is considered wisdom, not deficiency. Therefore, the objection fails because the analogy between inability to do the impossible and silence is flawed: silence here is a potential action, not an impossibility.

Correction: This passage shows that while inability to do the impossible is not a deficiency, inability to do possible actions (like commanding good and forbidding evil) is a deficiency. The objection conflates two different types of inability.

Allah's Eternal Attribute of Creation vs. Temporal Createdness
Nasafi explains the Maturidi position on creation and eternity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah is called eternally Creator but the world is not created in eternity, and the Maturidi distinction between attribute and addition.

Core Idea
Eternal attribute, temporal product: creation is in the attribute, not the world.

Nasafi tackles a subtle point: if Allah is everlastingly Creator, does that make the world everlastingly created? He argues no, because the attribute of creation subsists in Allah, but the world's being 'created' depends on the actual attachment of creation to it. This lesson unfolds that crucial distinction and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Nasafi parallels creation with other attributes like knowledge and will.
Pay attention to the terms 'khaliq' and 'makhluq'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Look for the contrast between 'was Creator' (kana khaliqan) and 'was the world created' (kana al-alam makhluqan). Understand why the non-existent cannot be created.
3
Understand the analogy with will
See how the same logic applies to will: Allah is eternally Willing, but not eternally Willing a specific world. Reflect on the warning about expressions vs. realities.
The conclusion warns against confusing literal expressions with deeper realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا نقول إنه تعالى كان في الأزل خالقا لقيام صفة الخلق، كما نقول : كان عالما قادرا مريدا . ولا نقول أيضا كان العالم في الأزل مخلوقا، لأن المعدوم لا يكون مخلوقا، لأنه إنما يصير مخلوقا بقبوله أثر التخليق، فقبل تعلق الخلق به لا يكون مخلوقا، وما لم يصر مخلوقا لا يضاف الخالق إليه، فلا يقال كان خالقا للعالم لأن الإضافة تثبت بتعليق الخلق بالمخلوق، فكانت الإضافة من مقتضيات التعلق لا من مقتضيات وجود صفة الخلق، واعتبر هذا بالإرادة فإن الله تعالى كان في الأزل مريدا ولا يقال: كان في الأزل مريدا وجود العالم ووجود العالم مراده في الأزل لما مر من الفرق، أو يمتنع عن الإضافة مخافة ثبوت وهم قدم ما دخل تحت الصفة، وهذا كله من باب العبارات دون الحقائق. وقد بينا الكلام في الحقيقة فكان التعلق بالعبارة ضربا من الإفلاس والحيرة.
Then we say: He, exalted, was in eternity Creator due to the establishment of the attribute of creation, just as we say: He was Knowing, Powerful, Willing. And we do not say that the world was created in eternity, because the non-existent cannot be created, for it only becomes created by receiving the effect of creation. So before the attachment of creation to it, it is not created. And as long as it has not become created, the Creator is not attributed to it. So it is not said: He was Creator of the world, because the addition is established by the attachment of creation to the creature. Thus the addition is from the requirements of attachment, not from the requirements of the existence of the attribute of creation. And consider this with the will: For Allah was in eternity Willing, and it is not said: He was in eternity Willing the existence of the world, and the existence of the world was His will in eternity, due to the distinction that has passed, or He refrains from addition for fear of establishing the illusion of the pre-eternity of what enters under the attribute. And all of this is from the category of expressions, not realities. And we have explained the speech concerning reality, so clinging to expression is a kind of bankruptcy and confusion.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the Maturidi position that Allah is eternally Creator in terms of the attribute, but not in terms of the product. The author distinguishes between the eternal attribute (sifa) of creation (khalq) and the temporal addition (idafa) of being 'Creator of the world' which depends on the actual act of creation. The non-existent (ma'dum) cannot be created, so the world is not created in eternity. The addition of 'Creator' to the world only occurs when creation attaches to the creature. This is analogized to the attribute of will: Allah is eternally Willing (murid), but one does not say He was eternally willing the specific existence of the world, because that would imply the willed object is eternal. The conclusion warns that this discussion is about expressions (ibarat) rather than realities, and clinging to expressions is a sign of intellectual bankruptcy.

Key Terms
الأزل
الخلق
الصفة
الإضافة
التعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That saying 'Allah is Creator in eternity' implies the world is eternal.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies the distinction between the eternal attribute and the temporal product.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say we cannot say 'Allah was Creator of the world in eternity'?
A) Because the attribute of creation is not eternal.
B) Because the world is eternal and thus not created.
C) Because the addition 'Creator of the world' depends on actual creation, which is temporal.
D) Because Allah's will is not eternal.

Key Takeaway
Allah's attribute of creation is eternal, but the world's being created is a temporal event; the two must not be conflated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Creator,' do you truly believe His creative power was active before you existed, or do you unconsciously think of it as beginning with creation? How does that affect your sense of dependence on Him every moment?

The passage warns against clinging to expressions without grasping the underlying reality. Is there a theological phrase you repeat (e.g., 'Allah is eternal') without letting it penetrate your heart? What would change if you truly lived as if He alone is eternal and you are utterly contingent?
?
If your understanding of Allah's attributes is limited by your own timeline, how might that be a form of intellectual arrogance? Will you commit today to studying His attributes with humility, letting them reshape your worship rather than fitting them into your assumptions?

Refutation of the Ash'ari Objection on the Eternity of the Attribute of Creation
Al-Nasafi refutes the Ash'ari objection that Allah is called Khaliq only upon creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidis argue that Allah's attribute of creation is eternal, and why the Ash'ari analogy to human prophets fails.

Core Idea
Allah's eternal self-praise proves His attribute of creation is eternal.

In this passage, al-Nasafi addresses a key Ash'ari objection: how can Allah be eternally called 'Creator' if creation hasn't happened yet? He presents a Quranic proof and then refutes the analogy to human figures like Ibrahim. The argument hinges on the difference between informing about a future attribute and praising oneself with an attribute that must already be present.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Maturidi proof from the Quran
Read the initial argument: Allah describes Himself as al-Khaliq in the eternal Quran, so the attribute must be eternal. Notice the premise that divine speech is eternal.
This is a direct proof from Surah al-Hashr.
2
Examine the Ash'ari objection
The Ash'ari responds that the verse merely informs that Allah will create, analogizing to verses about Ibrahim and Muhammad. Grasp this objection fully.
The analogy is key to the refutation.
3
Analyze al-Nasafi's refutation
Al-Nasafi distinguishes: creatures are not praised for future attributes, but Allah, being eternally existent, praises Himself with these titles. If the attribute were not eternal, His praise would be like wishing—impossible for Allah.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Conclude the lesson
Understand that this establishes the eternity of the attribute of creation, and note that al-Nasafi mentions other proofs he omits for brevity.
The discussion is part of a larger debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعلق بعض أصحابنا في المسألة على الأشعرية بقوله تعالى: ﴿ هُوَ اللَّهُ الْخَلِقُ الْبَارِئُ الْمُصَوِّرُ ﴾ فالله تعالى وصف نفسه في الأزل بأنه الخالق البارئ المصور، إذ كلامه أزلي، ووصف نفسه بما ليس من المحامد استهزاء بنفسه – جل ربنا عن ذلك. فاعترض عليه بعض الأشعرية فقال : ليس فيه أكثر من أنه أخبر أنه خالق وذلك لا يوجب ثبوت المخبر عنه في الأزل كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ إِبْرَاهِيمَ لَعَلِيمٌ ﴾ وَ﴿مُحَمَّدٌ رَّسُولُ اللَّهِ ﴾ ألا ترى أن معناهما إخبار أن إبراهيم متى وجد يوجد بهذه الصفة وكذا محمد ، فكذا كنا يكون معناه أنه متى يخلق يكون خالقا . قيل لهم : هذا القدر في إبراهيم ومحمد وغيرهما من المخلوقين مستقيم، لأنهم لم يمتدحوا بما ليس لهم بل الله أخبر أنهم متى يوجدون ينالون هذه الفضيلة. فأما الله تعالى فهو كان موجودًا في الأزل ووصف نفسه بذلك وتمدح، لا أن أخبر أنه يصير كذلك، فلو لم تكن الصفة ثابتة لكان تمدحا بما سيصير موصوفا به، فإنه وهو للحال موجود غير مستحق لتلك المحمدة، فيصير كالمتمني ثبوت ما ليس بثابت له، والمتشرف بما لم يثبت له بعد والمحب أن يمدح بما لم يفعل بعد، وهذا كله أمارة النقص والحاجة والسفه. ولصحة مذهبنا دلائل أخر من كتاب الله تعالى تركنا ذكرها لأن الكلام في هذه المسألة قد طال جدا.
And some of our companions argued in the issue against the Ash'aris with His statement Exalted: 'He is Allah, the Creator, the Maker, the Fashioner.' So Allah Exalted described Himself in pre-eternity as the Creator, the Maker, the Fashioner, since His speech is eternal. And describing Himself with what is not among praises would be mockery of Himself – our Lord is exalted above that. So some Ash'ari objected and said: There is nothing in it more than that He informed that He is a Creator, and that does not necessitate the existence of the informed-about in pre-eternity, as in His statement Exalted: 'Indeed Ibrahim was forbearing' and 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah.' Do you not see that their meaning is an informing that Ibrahim, whenever he exists, is found with this attribute, and likewise Muhammad. So likewise the meaning [of 'Creator'] would be that whenever He creates, He is Creator. It was said to them: This amount concerning Ibrahim, Muhammad, and others among the creatures is correct, because they were not praised for what is not theirs; rather Allah informed that whenever they exist they attain this virtue. As for Allah Exalted, He was existent in pre-eternity and described Himself with that and praised Himself, not that He informed that He becomes so. So if the attribute were not established, it would be praise of Himself with what He will become qualified with. For He, while He exists in the present time, is not deserving of that praiseworthiness. So He would become like one wishing for the establishment of what is not established for him, and one honoring himself with what has not yet been established for him, and one loving to be praised for what he has not yet done. And all of this is a mark of deficiency, need, and foolishness. And for the correctness of our school there are other proofs from the Book of Allah that we have left off mentioning because discussion of this issue has become very lengthy.
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an Ash'ari objection. It begins with a Maturidi proof from the Quran: Allah described Himself as al-Khaliq, al-Bari, al-Musawwir in pre-eternity, because His speech is eternal. An Ash'ari objects that this only informs that He will create, not that He is Creator eternally, analogizing to Abraham and Muhammad where the verses inform of future qualities. Al-Nasafi's reply distinguishes: creatures are not praised for what they don't yet possess; but Allah, being eternally existent, praises Himself with these titles in the eternal Quran. If the attribute were not eternally subsisting, His praise would be like wishing or seeking honor for what is not yet actual, which is impossible for Allah. Therefore the attribute of creation is eternal. He then notes that there are other proofs he omits for brevity.

Correction: Allah's eternal self-praise with the title 'al-Khaliq' proves the attribute of creation is eternally subsisting in His essence.

C) Because the Ash'ari misinterpreted the Quran.
D) Because the verses about Ibrahim are abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Allah's eternal self-praise in the Quran proves that His attribute of creation is eternal, not merely a future relational property.

What does that tell you about the importance of understanding Allah's attributes correctly?

One Action, Many Names: The Unity of Act and Diversity of Labels
Al-Nasafi explains how one action receives different names according to its effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single action can receive different names depending on its effects, and why this principle is foundational for understanding divine attributes.

Core Idea
One action, many names — the name depends on the effect, not the act itself.

Al-Nasafi addresses a key conceptual principle: the same act can be described by different names based on what it produces. This principle applies to human actions and, by analogy, to divine actions. The passage uses vivid examples to show that naming follows customary causality, not multiplicity in the agent. This lesson unpacks that logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from a general statement to specific examples.
The passage ends mid-sentence due to a page break; focus on the part present.
2
Identify the pattern of naming
The passage gives a series of examples where the same action (e.g., moving a hand) is called different names based on its effect. List the effects and their corresponding names.
Look for the pattern: 'if the result is X, it is called Y'.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself: If human actions can have many names while being one act, what does that suggest about divine actions and attributes? The commentary will help connect this to discussions about Allah's attributes.
This connects the lesson to broader themes in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلموا - رحمكم الله - أن الفعل الواحد تختلف أساميه باختلاف آثاره ومعقولاته فمهما كان الحاصل به حياة يسمى إحياء، ومهما كان الحاصل به موتا يسمى إماتة ومهما كان الحاصل به حركة يسمى تحريكا ومهما كان الحاصل به سكونا يسمى تسكينا، فإن من حرك يده يسمى ذلك منه فعلا، فإن صار ذلك سببا من حيث العادة لوجود ألم في شخص سمي إيلاما وإن كان سببا لحصول انكسار يسمى كسرا، وإن كان سببا لحصول انقطاع في محل يسمى قطعا، وإن صار سببا لحصول انزهاق الروح من محل يسمى قتلا، وإن صار سببا لحصول
Then know – may Allah have mercy on you – that the single action varies in its names according to its effects and its intelligibles. So whenever what results from it is life, it is called 'giving life' (iḥyāʾ); and whenever what results from it is death, it is called 'causing death' (imāta); and whenever what results from it is movement, it is called 'causing movement' (taḥrīk); and whenever what results from it is stillness, it is called 'causing stillness' (taskīn). For one who moves his hand, that from him is called an action (fiʿl). If it becomes a cause, by way of custom, for the existence of pain in a person, it is called 'causing pain' (īlām); and if it is a cause for the occurrence of a break, it is called 'breaking' (kasr); and if it is a cause for the occurrence of a cut in a place, it is called 'cutting' (qaṭʿ); and if it becomes a cause for the occurrence of the departure of the soul from a place, it is called 'killing' (qatl); and if it becomes a cause for the occurrence...
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage is a conceptual clarification about action and its naming. Al-Nasafi teaches that a single act can receive different names based on its effects (āthār) and what is understood from it (maʿqūlāt). He gives examples: if the result is life, it's iḥyāʾ; if death, imāta; if movement, taḥrīk; if stillness, taskīn. He then gives a concrete example: moving one's hand is simply an act (fiʿl). But if that act customarily causes pain, it is called īlām; if it causes a break, it is called kasr; if a cut, qaṭʿ; if the soul leaves the body, qatl. The action itself is one, but its names differ by effect. This shows that divine actions may also have multiple names depending on what they produce, not implying multiplicity in the divine essence. The passage ends mid-sentence due to page break.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Different names imply different actions.
Correction: The names differ by effect, not by the action itself; the action is one, but its names vary according to what it produces.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what determines the name of an action?
A) The intention of the agent.
B) The effect or result that customarily follows.
C) The time at which it occurs.
D) The material it is made of.

Key Takeaway
The naming of an action depends on its customary effect, not on the action itself, showing that diversity of names does not imply multiplicity in the actor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label someone's action as 'hurtful' or 'kind', do you reflect on the fact that your label depends on how it affected you? Could the same action be named differently by another person or by Allah? How often do you rush to judge based on your own perspective without considering that the action itself might be one but its names are many? Will you pause before judging today?
?
Consider a single action you performed today (e.g., a word you spoke). What names would it receive based on the effects it had on others? Are you satisfied with those names? If not, what can you change so that your action is named well? This lesson reminds you that your action is not defined solely by your intention but also by its outcome. How does this challenge you to be more mindful of how your actions affect others?
?
The passage uses moving the hand as an example of a single action that can be called 'causing pain', 'breaking', or 'killing' depending on what it produces. In your own life, do you often think of your actions as having fixed labels? How might seeing them as flexible according to their effects change the way you approach your daily interactions? Commit today to think of your actions in terms of their potential outcomes and strive for outcomes that earn beautiful names.
