ويحققه أن الإرادة لو كانت هي الفعل، والفعل والمفعول عندهم واحد لكانت الإرادة هي الفعل وهو المراد، فكان السواد (۱) إرادة، وكذا البياض والحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق، والموت والجهل وجميع أنواع الأعراض، وكذا الجواهر والأجسام. ثم التقرير بعد هذا من وجهين أحدهما أن الإرادة مع أنها عند جميع أهل من خواص الحي كالعلم والقدرة وغيرهما لو كانت كذلك (لجاز ذلك ) (٢) أيضا في العلم والقدرة وكان العلم هو المعلوم والقدرة هي المقدور، ولكان كل سواد وبياض وحركة وسكون وغير ذلك من الأعراض والجواهر والأجسام علمًا الله تعالى وقدرة وحيث لم يجز ذلك في العلم والقدرة لأنهما من خواص الحي، فلو كان كل معلوم علما وكل مقدور قدرة ، وصار السواد والبياض وما وراءها من الأعراض والأجسام علما وقدرة (۳) مع أن تلك الأشياء ليست من خواص الحي، لخرج (٤) العلم والقدرة () من أن يكونا من خواص الحي، وذلك ممتنع فامتنع أن يكون المعلوم علما، والمقدور قدرة، وكذا هذا في الإرادة. والتقرير الثاني أن الإرادة لما كانت من خواص الحي، والسواد والبياض وغيرهما لم تكن من خواصه، فلو كان المراد هو عين الإرادة، والمراد ليس من أخص أوصاف الحي، فإن السواد والبياض والاجتماع والافتراق وغيرهما من الأعراض ، ليست من خواص الحي . لخرجت الإرادة من أن تكون من أخص أوصاف الحي. فينبغي أن تتصف الجمادات والموات بالإرادة بطريق الحقيقة، وحيث لم يجر وكان ذلك محالا (دل) أن كون الإرادة) (۱) فعلا محال.
And it verifies that if irada (divine will) were the act, and the act and the object acted upon are one according to them, then irada would be the act and it would be the object willed. Thus blackness would be irada, likewise whiteness, motion, rest, conjunction, separation, death, ignorance, and all kinds of accidents, likewise atoms and bodies. Then the exposition after this is from two aspects: First, that irada, while according to all people it is among the properties of the living like knowledge and power and others, if it were so, then that would also be permissible in knowledge and power, so knowledge would be the known and power would be the powerful, and every blackness, whiteness, motion, rest, and other accidents, atoms, and bodies would be knowledge of Allah Almighty and power. And since that is not permissible in knowledge and power because they are properties of the living, if every known were knowledge and every powerful were power, and blackness, whiteness, and what follows them of accidents and bodies became knowledge and power, while those things are not properties of the living, then knowledge and power would exit from being properties of the living, and that is impossible. So it is impossible that the known be knowledge and the powerful be power. Likewise this in irada. The second exposition: Since irada is among the properties of the living, and blackness, whiteness, and others are not among His properties, if the object willed were the very irada, and the object willed is not among the most specific attributes of the living, for blackness, whiteness, conjunction, separation, and others of the accidents are not among the properties of the living, then irada would exit from being among the most specific attributes of the living. So it would be necessary that inanimate objects and dead things be described with irada in a real sense. And since this does not occur and is impossible, it indicates that irada being an act is impossible.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine will (irada) is not identical to the act. Nasafi begins by stating a hypothetical that if irada were the act, and act and object are one, then everything—accidents like blackness, whiteness, motion, rest, etc., and also atoms and bodies—would be irada. He then presents two arguments. The first argument draws an analogy with knowledge and power. If irada were the act, then by the same token knowledge would be the known and power the powerful, leading to absurdities that contradict the fact that knowledge and power are specific properties of the living. Since that is impossible, the same impossibility applies to irada being the act. The second argument focuses on the exclusive attribute of life. Irada is a property specific to living beings. But the objects willed (e.g., blackness, whiteness) are not properties of the living. If irada were the act (i.e., the object willed), then irada would no longer be a unique property of the living, and inanimate objects would also have irada, which is absurd. Therefore, irada cannot be the act.

Key Terms
إرادة
فعل
خواص الحي
مراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that God's will is the same as His act, so the act is nothing but will expressed.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if that were true, then will would not be an exclusive attribute of God, and even inanimate objects would have will, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise in Nasafi's second argument against identifying irada with the act?
A) The act is created, but irada is eternal.
B) Irada is a property exclusive to living beings, while the objects willed are not.
C) Irada is a single attribute, while acts are many.
D) The act is known by its effect, while irada is not.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's second argument proves that divine will cannot be the act because that would strip will of its exclusive status as a property of the living.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that because God's will is mighty, your own will is insignificant? How does this argument about will being a mark of life challenge you to exercise your will with purpose, knowing it is a gift from the Living God?
?
If your will and your actions are distinct, why do you sometimes act without truly willing it? When you sin, do you attribute it to a lack of will or to a split between your will and your action? How can you bring them into alignment?
?
Nasafi uses logical reasoning to protect the truth about God's attributes. Are you willing to examine your own beliefs about God with the same rigor—even if it exposes inconsistencies you prefer to ignore?

Refuting the Claim That Divine Will Means Command
Nasafi refutes equating irada with amr using verse and two absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine will (irada) cannot be equated with divine command (amr), and the two absurd consequences that follow from that equation.

Core Idea
Divine will and command are distinct: command can be disobeyed, will always occurs.

Some theologians claimed that when God's will relates to human actions, it simply means His command. Nasafi refutes this by showing that if irada were amr, it would lead to logical absurdities. He uses a Quranic verse and deduces two impossible consequences, confirming that will and command are separate attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Note the verse and the two arguments.
2
Understand the verse's implication
Nasafi quotes verse 10:99: 'If your Lord had willed, all would believe.' If irada meant command, the verse would become 'If your Lord commanded, all would believe.' Realize the shift.
The verse uses 'willed', not 'commanded'.
3
Examine the first absurd consequence
If irada were command, then those who didn't believe were not commanded, so they wouldn't be sinners for rejecting faith.
This contradicts the Islamic teaching that unbelievers are sinful.
4
Examine the second absurd consequence
If irada were command, then everyone God commands must become believers, but we see many who are commanded yet do not believe, which would be a contradiction in God's action.
God's command is obeyed by some, not all.
5
Conclude with Nasafi's verdict
Nasafi concludes that the claim is false and misguided. Understand why this distinction is crucial in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد بالإرادة ليس الأمر] ودليل بطلان ما قالوا أن إرادته تعالى إذا أضيفت إلى فعل العباد كان المراد منها الأمر قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ جَمِيعًا م (۲) فلو كانت الإرادة أمرا، لكان تقدير الآية ولو أمر ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلها جميعا. وفيه فساد من وجهين: أحدهما أنه يؤدي إلى أنه تعالى لم يأمر من لم يؤمن، ولو كان ذلك، لم يكن بترك الإيمان عاصيا . والآخر أن كل من أمره ينبغي أن يكون مؤمنا، ورأينا كثيرا ممن أمره ولم يؤمن، فكان فيه تناقض وتكذيب وذلك محال. فدل أن ما قاله هؤلاء باطل وضلال.
[What is meant by irada is not command.] And the proof of the invalidity of what they said: that His will, exalted, when added to the actions of servants, what is meant by it is command, is the saying of the Almighty: {And if your Lord had willed, all those on earth would have believed, all of them.} (10:99) [So] if irada were command, then the estimation of the verse would be: 'If your Lord commanded, all those on earth would have believed, all of them.' And in this is corruption from two aspects: First, it leads to the conclusion that the Almighty did not command those who did not believe, and if that were so, then one would not be a sinner for leaving faith. Second, everyone whom He commands ought to be a believer, but we see many whom He commands and who do not believe, thus involving contradiction and denial, and that is impossible. So it indicates that what these people said is false and misguided.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that when God's will (irada) is related to human actions, it simply means His command (amr). Nasafi first cites the verse from Sura Yunus: 'And if your Lord had willed, all on earth would have believed.' If irada were command, the verse would read: 'If your Lord commanded, all would believe.' He then shows two absurd consequences. First, it would imply that God did not command those who disbelieve, so they would not be sinners. Second, it would require that everyone God commands must become a believer, yet we see many who are commanded but do not believe. This would lead to contradiction and falsehood, which is impossible for God. Hence the claim is invalid and misguided.

أمر
عباد
عاصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will (irada) and His command (amr) are the same when it comes to human actions.
Correction: Nasafi shows they are distinct: will is always fulfilled, command can be disobeyed. Equating them leads to absurdities that contradict scripture and reason.

Checkpoint
What is the first absurd consequence Nasafi mentions if irada were command?
A) God would be commanding disbelief.
B) God would not have commanded those who disbelieve, so they would not be sinners.
C) Everyone would be forced to believe.
D) The verse would be contradictory.

Key Takeaway
Divine will (irada) is not the same as command (amr); will always occurs, but command can be disobeyed, and equating them leads to theological absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'if your Lord had willed, all on earth would have believed,' do you truly accept that God's will is behind every person's faith or lack thereof? How does this reality shape your attitude toward those who reject faith—do you see them as merely objects of God's will, or as responsible sinners who disobey His command?
?
This lesson distinguishes between God's will (which always occurs) and His command (which can be disobeyed). Examine your own life: are there areas where you treat God's commands as optional, as if they were merely suggestions rather than binding orders that you are accountable to obey? What would change if you truly lived as someone who knows that every command carries the weight of divine authority and that disobedience is sin?
?
The second absurdity shows that many who are commanded do not believe. Do you find yourself among those who hear God's command but do not act? What specific command of God are you currently neglecting, and what will you do today to obey it, knowing that your disobedience is not a failure of God's will but a failure of your own response?

Refutation That Divine Will Is Desire
Nasafi refutes equation of will and desire, cites two Mu'tazili views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that divine will (irada) cannot be equated with desire (shahwa), and how two Mu'tazili theologians defined desire to further clarify the distinction.

Core Idea
God's will is not desire because desire implies need and benefit, which do not apply to Him.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a critical misunderstanding about the nature of divine will. Some might think that God's will is analogous to human desire, but Nasafi shows that desire is a specific type of will that seeks benefit or pleasure. Since God is completely free from need, His will cannot be desire. He then contrasts two Mu'tazili definitions of desire, further clarifying the concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand Nasafi's argument and the two reported views.
Notice that the passage ends abruptly with 'وهو' – the main refutation is still complete.
2
Identify Nasafi's core refutation
Find the sentence where Nasafi defines shahwa as a specific irada aimed at benefit and applies it to God. Understand why God's irada cannot be desire.
The key point: shahwa seeks benefit, but God is not benefited.
3
Examine al-Jubbai's distinction
Read al-Jubbai's claim that desire can coexist with aversion while will cannot. Study the examples of the thirsty faster and medicine taker.
This helps to show that desire and will are conceptually different.
4
Consider Abu Hashim's definition
Note Abu Hashim's definition of shahwa as involving sensory pleasure. Reflect on how this definition also excludes God, who has no senses.
Both definitions reinforce the idea that God's will is not desire.
5
Reflect on the theological importance
Ask yourself why it matters to distinguish divine will from desire. Consider how this preserves God's transcendence and perfection.
This distinction prevents anthropomorphism and upholds tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: إن الإرادة هي الشهوة قلنا : ليس على الإطلاق بل الشهوة إرادة مخصوصة وهي إرادة ما فيه نفع إما لذة وإما غيرها، والله تعالى لا ينتفع بشيء فلا تكون إرادته اشتهاء. وزعم الجبائي (۳) أن الشهوة للشيء قد تجامع الكراهية له. وإرادة الشيء لا تجامع كراهيته قال: ألا ترى أن الصائم الذي اشتد عطشه يشتهي شرب الماء ولا يريده ، وشارب الدواء يريد شربه ولا يشتهيه فكانت الشهوة عنده توقان النفس إلى الشيء. وزعم أبو هاشم (٤) أن الشهوة ما فيه لذة للحواس. وهو
And what they said: that irada is shahwa (desire). We say: not absolutely, rather shahwa is a specific irada, and it is volition of something that has benefit, either pleasure or other than it, and Allah Almighty is not benefited by anything, so His irada is not desiring. And al-Jubbai claimed that desire for a thing may coexist with aversion to it, while irada for a thing does not coexist with aversion to it. He said: Do you not see that the fasting person who is extremely thirsty desires to drink water but does not will it, and the one who drinks medicine wills to drink it but does not desire it? So according to him, shahwa is the yearning of the soul for something. And Abu Hashim claimed that shahwa is that which contains sensual pleasure. And it is...
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that divine irada is the same as shahwa (desire). Nasafi begins by stating that shahwa is a specific type of irada, namely the volition of something that brings benefit (pleasure or otherwise). Since God is not in need of any benefit, His irada cannot be desire. He then reports two Mu'tazili views. Al-Jubbai argued that desire for something can coexist with aversion to it, whereas irada does not; he gave the example of a thirsty faster who desires water but does not will it (because of the fast), and a medicine taker who wills the drink but does not desire it. Thus, for al-Jubbai, shahwa is the soul's yearning. Abu Hashim defined shahwa as that which involves sensory pleasure. The passage ends abruptly, but the main refutation is clear: divine irada cannot be reduced to desire because God is beyond benefit and pleasure.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God's will is similar to human desire, implying need or passion.
Correction: Nasafi shows desire necessarily involves seeking benefit or pleasure, which does not apply to God. Thus divine will is categorically different.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject that divine irada is shahwa (desire)?
A) Because desire always coexists with aversion.
B) Because desire is a specific type of irada that seeks benefit, and God is free from need.
C) Because desire is only for sensory pleasure.
D) Because desire is temporal and irada is eternal.

Key Takeaway
God's will is not desire; desire implies benefit and need, which are absent in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The fasting person desires water but does not will it because of obedience. In what area of your life is your desire overriding your will to obey Allah? What concrete step can you take today to bring your actions in line with His command, even if your feelings resist?
?
You serve a God who is completely free from need—He does not will things for His own benefit. Does your worship reflect that reality, or are you treating Allah as if He needs your prayers and obedience? How can you purify your intention so that you worship Him solely because He is worthy, not because you expect a return?

Divine Will Is Not an Accident
Nasafi argues that irada cannot be an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine will (irada) cannot be classified as an accident (ʿaraḍ) based on the properties of living beings.

Core Idea
Accidents belong to the living; if irada were an accident, inanimate objects would have will.

This passage presents a concise logical argument against the view that divine will is an accident. Since accidents are specific to living beings, and inanimate objects are not described as having will, it follows that will cannot be an accident. Let's see how Imam Nasafi builds this proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the premise: accidents are properties of the living. Then trace the conclusion.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice the conditional reasoning: if irada were an accident, it would be among the specific attributes of the living. But inanimate objects do not have irada, so it cannot be an accident.
3
Consider the implication
This refutes those who claimed divine will is a created accident in Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأعراض ، ليست من خواص الحي . لخرجت الإرادة من أن تكون من أخص أوصاف الحي. فينبغي أن تتصف الجمادات والموات بالإرادة بطريق الحقيقة، وحيث لم يجر وكان ذلك محالا (دل) أن كون الإرادة فعلا محال.

المراد بالإرادة ليس الأمر]

ودليل بطلان ما قالوا أن إرادته تعالى إذا أضيفت إلى فعل العباد كان المراد منها الأمر قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ جَمِيعًا ﴾ فلو كانت الإرادة أمرا، لكان تقدير الآية ولو أمر ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلها جميعا. وفيه فساد من وجهين: أحدهما أنه يؤدي إلى أنه تعالى لم يأمر من لم يؤمن، ولو كان ذلك، لم يكن بترك الإيمان عاصيا . والآخر أن كل من أمره ينبغي أن يكون مؤمنا، ورأينا كثيرا ممن أمره ولم يؤمن، فكان فيه تناقض وتكذيب وذلك محال. فدل أن ما قاله هؤلاء باطل وضلال.
Accidents are not among the properties of the living. So irada (will) would be excluded from being among the most specific attributes of the living. Then it would be necessary that inanimate objects and dead things be described with irada in reality, and since this is not the case and that is impossible, it indicates that irada being an act is impossible.

What is meant by irada is not command.

And the proof of the falsity of what they said: that His irada, when added to the actions of slaves, means command is His saying exalted: 'And if your Lord had willed, all those on earth would have believed, all together.' So if irada were a command, then the interpretation of the verse would be: 'And if your Lord had commanded, all those on earth would have believed, all together.' And in this is corruption in two respects: one is that it leads to the consequence that He did not command the one who did not believe, and if that were the case, then he would not be disobedient by leaving belief. And the other is that everyone He commands ought to be a believer, but we see many whom He commands and they do not believe, so there is contradiction and denial, and that is impossible. So it indicates that what these people said is false and misguided.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage refutes two false views on divine will (irada). First, the author argues that irada cannot be an accident (ʿaraḍ) because accidents are properties of living things. If irada were an accident, it would be among the specific attributes of living beings, yet we do not describe inanimate objects with irada, so it cannot be an accident. The phrase 'كون الإرادة فعلا' likely means 'irada being an act/accident' is impossible.

Second, turning to the denial that irada is a command (amr), the author adduces a Quranic verse: 'If your Lord had willed, all on earth would have believed.' If irada meant command, the verse would read 'if your Lord had commanded...'—but then two absurdities follow: (1) God would not have commanded the unbelievers, so they would not be disobedient, and (2) everyone commanded would necessarily believe, which contradicts observable reality. Therefore the identification of irada with command is false.

Key Terms
إرادة
أعراض
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will is an accident (created attribute).
Correction: Accidents are properties of living bodies; Allah is not a body, and irada is an eternal attribute, not an accident.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise in the argument that irada cannot be an accident?
A) Accidents are properties of the living.
B) Allah's will is eternal.
C) Inanimate objects have no will.
D) The Quran proves irada is not an accident.

Key Takeaway
Divine will (irada) cannot be an accident because accidents are specific to living beings, and we do not attribute will to inanimate objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah’s will is always present and active, even when you feel He is silent? How does this knowledge change your patience in dua?

?

Divine Will Is Not a Command
Nasafi refutes the identification of irada with amr using Quran and rational critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine will (irada) cannot be equated with command (amr) using a Quranic proof and rational consequences.

Core Idea
If irada meant command, the Quranic verse would become contradictory and false.

Some argued that when Allah's will relates to human actions, it means command. Imam Nasafi refutes this using a Quranic verse that would then lead to two absurd consequences. Let's examine the proof.

Guided Path

Read the Quranic argument

2
Identify the two absurdities

3
Draw the conclusion
The conclusion is that equating irada with amr leads to contradiction and denial of reality, so the claim is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأعراض ، ليست من خواص الحي . لخرجت الإرادة من أن تكون من أخص أوصاف الحي. فينبغي أن تتصف الجمادات والموات بالإرادة بطريق الحقيقة، وحيث لم يجر وكان ذلك محالا (دل) أن كون الإرادة فعلا محال.

المراد بالإرادة ليس الأمر]

ودليل بطلان ما قالوا أن إرادته تعالى إذا أضيفت إلى فعل العباد كان المراد منها الأمر قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ جَمِيعًا ﴾ فلو كانت الإرادة أمرا، لكان تقدير الآية ولو أمر ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلها جميعا. وفيه فساد من وجهين: أحدهما أنه يؤدي إلى أنه تعالى لم يأمر من لم يؤمن، ولو كان ذلك، لم يكن بترك الإيمان عاصيا . والآخر أن كل من أمره ينبغي أن يكون مؤمنا، ورأينا كثيرا ممن أمره ولم يؤمن، فكان فيه تناقض وتكذيب وذلك محال. فدل أن ما قاله هؤلاء باطل وضلال.
Accidents are not among the properties of the living. So irada (will) would be excluded from being among the most specific attributes of the living. Then it would be necessary that inanimate objects and dead things be described with irada in reality, and since this is not the case and that is impossible, it indicates that irada being an act is impossible.

What is meant by irada is not command.

And the proof of the falsity of what they said: that His irada, when added to the actions of slaves, means command is His saying exalted: 'And if your Lord had willed, all those on earth would have believed, all together.' So if irada were a command, then the interpretation of the verse would be: 'And if your Lord had commanded, all those on earth would have believed, all together.' And in this is corruption in two respects: one is that it leads to the consequence that He did not command the one who did not believe, and if that were the case, then he would not be disobedient by leaving belief. And the other is that everyone He commands ought to be a believer, but we see many whom He commands and they do not believe, so there is contradiction and denial, and that is impossible. So it indicates that what these people said is false and misguided.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage refutes two false views on divine will (irada). First, the author argues that irada cannot be an accident (ʿaraḍ) because accidents are properties of living things. If irada were an accident, it would be among the specific attributes of living beings, yet we do not describe inanimate objects with irada, so it cannot be an accident. The phrase 'كون الإرادة فعلا' likely means 'irada being an act/accident' is impossible.

Second, turning to the denial that irada is a command (amr), the author adduces a Quranic verse: 'If your Lord had willed, all on earth would have believed.' If irada meant command, the verse would read 'if your Lord had commanded...'—but then two absurdities follow: (1) God would not have commanded the unbelievers, so they would not be disobedient, and (2) everyone commanded would necessarily believe, which contradicts observable reality. Therefore the identification of irada with command is false.

Key Terms
إرادة
أمر
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will (irada) is equivalent to command (amr) when related to human actions.
Correction: Irada is distinct from amr; equating them leads to Quranic contradictions and undermines human responsibility for disbelief.

Checkpoint
What are the two absurdities that follow if irada is identified with command?
A) God would have commanded all, and all would obey.
B) Unbelievers would not be commanded and thus not sinful; all commanded would necessarily believe.
C) The Quran would be contradictory, and prophecies would fail.
D) Humans would have no free will, and God would be unjust.

Key Takeaway
Divine will (irada) cannot be identified with command (amr) because it would contradict the Quran and observed reality of disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall a specific sin you have committed, do you ever feel a sense of fatalism—that since Allah willed it, you were not truly responsible? How does the lesson's distinction between irada and amr pierce through that excuse and leave you without defense before your Lord?
?
If Allah's command were identical to His will, then every command of His would necessarily be fulfilled, and you would never have disobeyed. But you have. What does that reality—that you have rejected His command even though He willed otherwise—say about your own heart's rebellion? Take a moment to name one command you have knowingly violated, and ask yourself: what prevented you from obeying?
?
Consider the weight of the verse: 'If your Lord had willed, all would have believed.' Yet you know people who do not believe. Does this lesson help you see that their unbelief is not due to a lack of command from Allah, but rather a choice in the face of His command? How does that change how you view your own duty to call them to faith and your own gratitude for being guided?

Distinguishing Divine Will from Human Desire
Nasafi distinguishes divine will from human desire and refutes the equation of irada with shahwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between divine will (irada) and human desire (shahwa), and why identifying them leads to theological absurdity.

Core Idea
Divine will is not desire; confusing them confuses the necessary with the impossible.

Imam Nasafi now tackles a critical distinction: is God's will like human desire? Some theologians equated them, but this leads to attributing need and sensory pleasure to Allah. In this passage, Nasafi defines both terms, refutes two Mu'tazili views, and concludes that conflating them is logically impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a definition, two refutations, and a conclusion.
The passage begins with a rejection of the equation, then discusses al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim.
2
Understand the two refutations
Al-Jubba'i distinguishes desire from will by the co-occurrence of dislike; Abu Hashim restricts desire to sensory pleasures. Note Nasafi's counterexamples.
The fasting person example illustrates al-Jubba'i's point; the intellectual discovery example refutes Abu Hashim.
3
Grasp the final argument
Nasafi concludes that equating irada with shahwa confuses the necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud) with the impossible (mumtani'), which is an absurdity.
This argument is decisive: it shows the equation leads to logical contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإرادة ليست هي الشهوة]

وما قالوا: إن الإرادة هي الشهوة قلنا : ليس على الإطلاق بل الشهوة إرادة مخصوصة وهي إرادة ما فيه نفع إما لذة وإما غيرها، والله تعالى لا ينتفع بشيء فلا تكون إرادته اشتهاء. وزعم الجبائي أن الشهوة للشيء قد تجامع الكراهية له. وإرادة الشيء لا تجامع كراهيته قال: ألا ترى أن الصائم الذي اشتد عطشه يشتهي شرب الماء ولا يريده ، وشارب الدواء يريد شربه ولا يشتهيه فكانت الشهوة عنده توقان النفس إلى الشيء. وزعم أبو هاشم أن الشهوة ما فيه لذة للحواس. وهو باطل، لأن الإنسان قد يشتهي استخراج العلم بالدقائق واستنباطه. فإذا استنبطه فرح به والتذ بفرحه، وليس ذلك من وجهة حواسه فدل أنها لا تختص بالحواس، وإنما الصحيح ما بينا من الفرق بين الشهوة والإرادة، على أنا أقمنا دلائل توجب كون الباري مريدا، وقامت دلالة امتناع اتصافه بالشهوة، ولا شك أن ما هو واجب الوجود، غير ما هو ممتنع الوجود، فمن جعل الإرادة شهوة فقد جعل الواجب ممتنعا والممتنع واجبا، وذلك محال ، وبالله العصمة والتوفيق.
Irada is not desire.

And what they said: that irada is desire, we say: not absolutely, rather desire is a specific irada, and it is the irada of something that contains benefit, either pleasure or other than it, and Allah exalted is not benefited by anything, so His irada is not desire. And al-Jubba'i claimed that desire for a thing may coexist with dislike of it, while irada of a thing does not coexist with dislike of it. He said: Do you not see that the fasting person who is very thirsty desires to drink water but does not will it, and the one who drinks medicine wills to drink it but does not desire it. So desire according to him is the yearning of the soul for a thing. And Abu Hashim claimed that desire is what brings pleasure to the senses. And it is false, because a person may desire to extract knowledge of subtle matters and deduce it. When he deduces it, he rejoices and delights in his joy, and that is not from the direction of his senses, so it indicates that it is not specific to the senses. Rather the correct view is what we have explained of the distinction between desire and irada, and indeed we have established proofs that necessitate that the Creator is willing, and proof has been established of the impossibility of His being described with desire. There is no doubt that what is necessarily existent is other than what is impossible of existence, so whoever makes irada desire has made the necessary impossible and the impossible necessary, and that is impossible. And with Allah is protection and success.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage refutes the identification of divine will (irada) with desire (shahwa). The author first defines desire as a specific type of irada, namely the volition of something beneficial, whether pleasure or other. Since Allah is not benefited by anything, His irada cannot be desire.

The author then examines two Mu'tazili views. Al-Jubba'i distinguishes desire from will by noting that desire can coexist with dislike (the thirsty person desires water but does not will it due to fasting), whereas will cannot. Abu Hashim restricts desire to sensory pleasures, but the author refutes this by pointing out that humans desire intellectual discovery, which is not sensory. Thus desire is not limited to the senses.

Finally, the author asserts that proofs establish that the Creator is willing (murid) and cannot be described with desire. Conflating irada with shahwa would confuse the necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud) with the impossible, which is absurd.

Key Terms
إرادة
شهوة
الجبائي
أبو هاشم
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equating divine will (irada) with human desire (shahwa) is acceptable because both involve wanting something.
Correction: Desire is a specific type of irada that involves seeking benefit or pleasure. Since Allah is free from need and sensory experience, His irada cannot be desire; equating them leads to logical contradictions about necessary existence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubba'i, what is the difference between desire (shahwa) and will (irada)?
A) Desire never coexists with dislike, will can
B) Desire coexists with dislike, will does not
C) Desire is for sensory pleasures, will is not
D) There is no difference

Key Takeaway
Divine will is categorically different from human desire; equating them is both theologically and logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you desire something deeply, do you ever assume that Allah’s will operates like your desire—driven by need or emotional craving? How does this passage expose that assumption as a subtle form of anthropomorphism? What will you do the next time you catch yourself thinking that way?
?
If you truly internalized that Allah’s will is free from any trace of benefit or need, would your trust in His decree change? Specifically, when a du‘ā’ of yours goes unanswered, do you feel He was ‘unwilling’ like someone who refuses you out of reluctance? How can you train your heart to see His irada as perfect and uncompromised?
?
Think of a situation where your desires conflicted with what you knew was right. The passage says desire can coexist with dislike, but divine will cannot. Since you are human, your will is often split. Are you comfortable with that weakness? Or does it drive you to rely more on Allah’s singular, unbending will in your life? What is one concrete step you can take today to align your will with His, even when your desires pull the other way?

Allah's Eternal Will and Refutation of Al-Najjar
Nasafi refutes al-Najjar's denial of an eternal attribute of will and shows his reliance on Mu'tazili premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming an eternal attribute of will for Allah is necessary and how al-Najjar's denial stems from two false Mu'tazili premises.

Core Idea
Allah's will is a real eternal attribute, not reducible to His essence.

We have already established that Allah is willing. Now we confront a deeper question: is He willing by a will or without? Al-Najjar, following the Mu'tazila, denied an attribute of will. In this passage, Nasafi exposes the flawed premises that led him to this conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation to understand the content.
Notice the strong refutation tone.
2
Identify the reductionist view dismissed
Nasafi first rejects a minimal interpretation of 'willing'. Note what that interpretation is and why it is insufficient.
3
Spot the disagreement
Find where Nasafi presents the two positions: by a will vs. without a will. Identify who holds each.
Al-Najjar's position is highlighted.
4
Analyze the two false premises
The passage states al-Najjar's error comes from two premises. List them and see how they lead to his conclusion.
These are borrowed from the Mu'tazila.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why affirming a real attribute of will is important for understanding Allah's volition.
This connects to the broader Aqeedah topic of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله تعالى مريد بإرادة أزلية]

وما زعموا أن معنى قولنا: إن الله تعالى أراد فعل كذا معناه أنه ليس بساه ولا مكره عليه ولا مضطر قد بينا فساده ثم إذا ثبت أن الله تعالى مريد، واختلفوا بعد ذلك أنه مريد بإرادة أم هو مريد بغير إرادة؟ فذهب من قال: بأنه تعالى مريد إلى أنه مريد بإرادة، وقال الحسين بن محمد النجاري البصري رئيس النجارية: إنه تعالى مريد لا بإرادة بل هو مريد بذاته. وإنما وقع هو في هذا القول لضرورة مقدمتين كاذبتين اعتقد صحتهما فأدتاه إلى هذه النتيجة الكاذبة. أحد المقدمتين أنه ساعد المعتزلة في القول باستحالة قيام صفة بذات الباري جل وعلا والثانية أنه ساعدهم في القول بأن ما كان من صفات الذات فهو عين الذات.
Allah exalted is willing by an eternal will.

And what they claimed that the meaning of our saying: 'Indeed Allah willed such and such action' is that He is not heedless nor coerced nor compelled — we have already shown its falsity. Then when it is established that Allah is willing, they disagreed afterwards whether He is willing by a will or without a will? So those who said He is willing went to the view that He is willing by a will. And al-Husayn ibn Muhammad al-Najjar al-Basri, the head of the Najjariyya, said: He is willing without a will, rather He is willing by His essence. He only fell into this saying due to the necessity of two false premises which he believed to be correct, leading him to this false conclusion. One of the two premises is that he agreed with the Mu'tazila on the impossibility of an attribute subsisting in the essence of the Creator, and the second is that he agreed with them in saying that whatever is an attribute of the essence is identical to the essence.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage establishes that Allah is willing (murid) by an eternal will (irada azaliyya) and refutes the view of al-Najjar (head of the Najjariyya) that Allah is willing by His essence without an attribute of will. The author first dismisses a reductionist interpretation that 'willing' merely means 'not heedless, coerced, or compelled' as already refuted. Then he presents the disagreement: the orthodox view vs. al-Najjar's position.

Al-Najjar's error stems from two false premises borrowed from the Mu'tazila: (1) that no attribute can subsist in the divine essence (istihala qiyam sifa bi-dhat al-Bari), and (2) that essential attributes (sifat al-dhat) are identical to the essence (ayn al-dhat). Accepting these leads to denying a real, distinct attribute of will. The author implies that the correct view affirms a real, eternal will as an attribute distinct from but subsisting in the divine essence.

Key Terms
إرادة
مريد
إرادة أزلية
النجاري
صفات الذات
عين الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah is willing by His essence without any attribute of will.
Correction: Allah is willing by an eternal will that subsists in His essence, distinct from His essence.

Checkpoint
What two false premises did al-Najjar accept from the Mu'tazila that led him to deny that Allah is willing by an eternal will?
A) That attributes cannot subsist in the divine essence and that essential attributes are identical to the essence
B) That Allah has no attributes and that will is created
C) That attributes are eternal and that will is separate
D) That Allah is not willing and that will is a human concept

Key Takeaway
Affirming an eternal attribute of will for Allah is necessary; denying it leads to contradictions rooted in false Mu'tazili premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you truly believe that Allah's will is a real and active force that is in control, or do you subconsciously rely on your own efforts as if they are independent? Spend the next 10 minutes writing down one area of your life where you need to explicitly submit to His will and recite a prayer acknowledging His real eternal will.
?
Al-Najjar's error began with false premises about who Allah is. What false assumptions about Allah's attributes might you be holding without realizing? How can you actively replace them with the truth affirmed in this lesson, starting today?
?
If Allah's will is not merely an abstract concept but a real eternal attribute, then your relationship with Him is deeply personal and direct. In what specific situation have you acted as if His will is distant or ineffective? Confess that to Him now and commit to trusting His will in that situation.

Refuting the Equation of Divine Will with Desire
Al-Nasafi refutes equating will with desire through rational and logical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational distinction between divine will (irada) and sensory desire (shahwa), and why equating them leads to a logical contradiction regarding Allah's necessary existence.

Core Idea
Divine will is not sensory desire; confusing them makes the necessary impossible.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi directly confronts a mistaken equation between Allah's will and human desire. He offers a clear rational argument showing that will, especially the delight in intellectual discovery, transcends the senses. Understanding this distinction protects us from attributing changeable, sensory-based attributes to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting that al-Nasafi begins by rejecting the claim as false (bāṭil). Observe the structure: a counterexample, a conceptual distinction, and a logical argument from necessity.
The passage is dense but carefully reasoned.
2
Understand the counterexample
The passage gives a human example: a person desires to deduce subtle knowledge and feels joy upon success, which is not sensory. Recognize that this shows will can exist without sensory desire.
3
Grasp the distinction and implication
Al-Nasafi asserts the established difference between shahwa and irada, and then argues that since Allah is necessary of existence and shahwa is impossible for Him, equating them is contradictory.
4
Reflect on the logic of necessary vs. impossible
The final argument uses the categories of wajib al-wujud and mumtani' al-wujud. Understand that making two opposites identical is logically impossible (muhāl).
This is a foundational kalam principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باطل، لأن الإنسان قد يشتهي استخراج العلم بالدقائق واستنباطه. فإذا استنبطه فرح به والتذ بفرحه، وليس ذلك من وجهة حواسه فدل أنها لا تختص بالحواس، وإنما الصحيح ما بينا من الفرق بين الشهوة والإرادة، على أنا أقمنا دلائل توجب كون الباري مريدا، وقامت دلالة امتناع اتصافه بالشهوة، ولا شك أن ما هو واجب الوجود، غير ما هو ممتنع الوجود، فمن جعل الإرادة شهوة فقد جعل الواجب ممتنعا والممتنع واجبا، وذلك محال، وبالله العصمة والتوفيق.
False, because a person may desire to extract knowledge of subtle matters and to deduce it. When he deduces it, he rejoices and delights in his joy, and that is not from the direction of his senses. So it indicates that [the will] is not exclusive to the senses. Rather the correct [view] is what we have explained of the distinction between desire (shahwa) and will (irada). Moreover, we have established proofs that necessitate that the Creator (al-Bari) is willing (murid), and there is established evidence of the impossibility of His being described with desire (shahwa). And there is no doubt that that which is necessary of existence is other than that which is impossible of existence. So whoever makes will (irada) to be desire (shahwa) has made the necessary impossible and the impossible necessary, and that is impossible. And with Allah is protection and success.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. Imam al-Nasafi begins by responding to an objection or claim that will is nothing but a type of desire (shahwa). He argues that this is false (bāṭil) because humans often feel a desire (yashtahī) to extract and deduce knowledge from subtle points (istikhraj al-ʿilm bi-l-daqāʾiq). When they succeed, they feel joy (fariḥa) and delight (taladhdhadha). This joy is not from the direction of the senses (laysa dhālika min wijhati ḥawāssihī), thus indicating that will (irāda) is not exclusive to the senses. The correct distinction is between shahwa and irāda. Furthermore, proofs have been established that Allah is murīd (willing), and evidence shows that He cannot be described with shahwa (desire). Since Allah is wājib al-wujūd (necessary of existence) and shahwa is mumtaniʿ al-wujūd (impossible of existence) for Him, equating irāda with shahwa would be a contradiction—making the necessary impossible and vice versa. The passage concludes with a supplication for divine protection and success.

Key Terms
شهوة (shahwa)
إرادة (irada)
مريد (murid)
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud)
ممتنع الوجود (mumtani' al-wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All will involves sensory desire.
Correction: The passage shows that intellectual will and joy are non-sensory, so will can exist without sensory desire.

Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Nasafi use to prove that will is not exclusive to the senses?
A) The fact that humans delight in sensory pleasures.
B) The fact that humans delight in intellectual discovery and reasoning.
C) The fact that animals have will.
D) The fact that will is always accompanied by desire.

Key Takeaway
Equating Allah's will with human sensory desire is rationally impossible because it would make the Necessary Being subject to impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a strong desire for something, do you ever justify it as being part of Allah's will for you? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and what specific step will you take this week to distinguish your desire from seeking Allah’s will?
?
Have you ever excused a sin by telling yourself that Allah must have willed it because you desired it? In light of this lesson, how does equating your desire with divine will make the necessary impossible? What one thing can you change today to align your will with Allah’s command?
?
This passage warns against confusing the Creator with creation. In what area of your life have you unconsciously treated Allah as if He shares your weaknesses or needs? Write down one practical commitment you will make to affirm His perfection in that area.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
8 min
The Necessity of an Attribute of Will for Allah
Al-Nasafi refutes al-Najjar's view on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi argues that Allah's will must be an eternal attribute of essence, not reducible to His essence.

Core Idea
Allah's will is an eternal attribute of essence, not His essence itself.

Al-Nasafi presents the disagreement about whether Allah is willing by an attribute of will or by His essence. He refutes al-Najjar's position by showing it relies on two false premises borrowed from the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Note how al-Nasafi structures his argument: from assertion to refutation of an earlier claim, then to the specific disagreement.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main assertion and disagreement
The passage opens with the assertion that Allah is willing with an eternal will. Then it notes a disagreement: is He willing by a will or without? Find this distinction.
Al-Najjar's position is that Allah is willing by His essence.
3
Analyze al-Najjar's two false premises
Al-Nasafi explains that al-Najjar adopted two incorrect views from the Mu'tazila: (1) that an attribute cannot subsist in Allah's essence, and (2) that attributes of essence are not additional meanings. Identify these in the text.
These premises are the root of the error.
4
Understand the third premise and the refutation
The third premise (correct) is that Allah wills all that occurs. Al-Najjar concluded that will is an attribute of essence because it is general and not negated. But al-Nasafi says this conclusion is false because it builds on Mu'tazilite denial of distinct attributes.
The same proofs against Mu'tazila apply to al-Najjar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله تعالى مريد بإرادة أزلية. وما زعموا أن معنى قولنا: إن الله تعالى أراد فعل كذا معناه أنه ليس بساه ولا مكره عليه ولا مضطر قد بينا فساده. ثم إذا ثبت أن الله تعالى مريد، واختلفوا بعد ذلك أنه مريد بإرادة أم هو مريد بغير إرادة؟ فذهب من قال: بأنه تعالى مريد إلى أنه مريد بإرادة، وقال الحسين بن محمد النجاري البصري رئيس النجارية: إنه تعالى مريد لا بإرادة بل هو مريد بذاته. وإنما وقع هو في هذا القول لضرورة مقدمتين كاذبتين اعتقد صحتهما فأدتاه إلى هذه النتيجة الكاذبة. أحد المقدمتين أنه ساعد المعتزلة في القول باستحالة قيام صفة بذات الباري جل وعلا والثانية أنه ساعدهم في القول بأن ما كان من صفات الذات فهو أرجع إلى الذات وليس بمعنى وراء الذات، وما كان من صفات الفعل فهو عين المفعول وساعدهم أن الفرق بين صفة الذات وصفة الفعل أن ما لا ينفي بل يتعمم فهو صفة ذات كالعلم والقدرة. وما يثبت وينفى ولا يتعمم فهو صفة فعل على ما مر من بيان مذاهب المعتزلة والمقدمة الثالثة وهي صحيحة أنه ساعد أهل الحق في خلق أفعال العباد، وأنه تعالى أراد كل ما علم حدوثه أن يحدث جسما كان ذلك أو عرضا اختيارا كان ذلك أم اضطرارًا قبيحا كان أم حسنا. فكانت الإرادة على هذا عامة في المحدثات، أجمع، وهي التي يصح تعلق الإرادة بها لا القديم ولا صفاته، وكانت مما يثبت ولا ينفى، إذ لا يقال كان كذا بإرادته ولم يكن كذا بإرادته فكانت من قبيل صفات الذات، وكانت راجعة إلى الذات، فكان الباري جل وعلا مريدا لذاته كما كان قادرا عالما لذاته. وكل دليل أقمناه على بطلان مذهب المعتزلة في مسألة الصفات، هو الدليل لبطلان مذهب النجار ها هنا.
Allah Most High is willing with an eternal will. And what they claimed that the meaning of our saying 'Allah Most High willed such an action' is that He is not heedless nor coerced upon it nor compelled—we have shown its falsity. Then when it is established that Allah Most High is willing (murid), they differed afterwards whether He is willing with a will (irada) or He is willing without a will? Those who say that He is willing went to the view that He is willing with a will. And al-Husayn ibn Muhammad al-Najjar al-Basri, the head of the Najjariyya, said: He Most High is willing not by a will, but He is willing by His essence (bi-dhatihi). He only fell into this saying due to two false premises which he believed to be sound, and they led him to this false conclusion. One of the two premises is that he supported the Mu'tazila in the saying of the impossibility of an attribute subsisting in the essence of the Creator (al-Bari) Most High. And the second is that he supported them in the saying that whatever is of the attributes of the essence (sifat al-dhat) is referred back to the essence and is not a meaning beyond the essence, and whatever is of the attributes of action (sifat al-fi'l) is identical to the effect. And he supported them that the distinction between an attribute of the essence and an attribute of action is that that which is not negated but rather is general (yat'ammam) is an attribute of the essence like knowledge and power. And that which is affirmed and negated and is not general is an attribute of action, according to what has passed of exposition of the Mu'tazilite schools. And the third premise, which is true, is that he supported the People of Truth (ahl al-haqq) in the creation of the actions of servants, and that He Most High willed everything whose occurrence He knew to occur, whether it be a body or an accident, whether it be optional or compelled, whether it be ugly or good. So on this basis, the will (irada) was general with respect to originated things (al-muhdathat) all together, and it is that to which the attachment of will is valid, not the eternal nor His attributes. And it was among that which is affirmed and not negated, for it is not said 'He was such by His will and He was not such by His will'. So it was of the category of attributes of the essence (sifat al-dhat), and it referred back to the essence. So the Creator Most High was willing by His essence just as He was powerful, knowing by His essence. And every proof we have established for the invalidity of the Mu'tazilite doctrine on the issue of attributes is the proof for the invalidity of the doctrine of al-Najjar here.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument and refutation. It begins with the statement that Allah is willing with an eternal will (murīd bi-irāda azaliyya). Then it addresses a claim that the meaning of 'Allah willed' is merely negation of heedlessness, coercion, or compulsion—which is shown as false. After establishing that Allah is murīd, the text discusses the disagreement: is He murīd by an irāda (will) or without? Most say by irāda; al-Najjar says by His essence (bi-dhātihi). Al-Nasafi then explains that al-Najjar's view stems from two false premises (muqaddimatān kādhibatān): (1) He agreed with Mu'tazila that an attribute cannot subsist in Allah's essence. (2) He agreed with them that attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) are not additional meanings beyond the essence, but are referred back to the essence, while attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fiʿl) are identical to the effect. He also agreed with their distinction: attributes that are not negated and are general are ṣifāt al-dhāt (like knowledge and power); those that are affirmed and negated and not general are ṣifāt al-fiʿl. A third premise (which is correct) is that he agreed with Ahl al-Haqq on the creation of human actions and that Allah wills everything that occurs—good or bad, optional or compelled. So irāda is general to all originated things (muḥdathāt) and is not negated; thus it belongs to ṣifāt al-dhāt and refers back to the essence, making Allah murīd bi-dhātihi like He is ʿālim (knowing) and qādir (powerful) bi-dhātihi. But this conclusion is false because it is built on the Mu'tazilite denial of distinct attributes. Therefore, the same proofs used against Mu'tazila about attributes invalidate al-Najjar's doctrine.

إرادة
صفات الذات
صفات الفعل
النجارية
المعتزلة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah's will is identical to His essence, not an additional attribute.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows al-Najjar's conclusion is based on false Mu'tazilite premises; the correct view is that will is an eternal attribute of essence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, which two claims did al-Najjar agree with the Mu'tazila on that led to his false conclusion?
A) That Allah is capable of creating human actions and that will is general.
B) That attributes cannot subsist in Allah's essence and that attributes of essence are not additional meanings.
C) That will is a created accident and that knowledge is eternal.
D) That Allah's will is identical to His knowledge and that humans create their own actions.

Key Takeaway
Allah's will is an eternal attribute of essence (sifat al-dhat), not reducible to His essence, as proven by the refutation of al-Najjar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief about Allah's attributes simply because it seemed logically tidy, without submitting to what the Salaf taught? What false premise might you be unknowingly holding that could lead you away from the truth about Allah?
?
When you face trials that contradict your plans, do you truly believe that every event—whether good or evil—is willed by Allah with His eternal will, or does your heart rebel against His decree? How would your daily reactions change if you lived by this belief?
?
Al-Najjar's error began with two false premises he adopted from a flawed school. Are you aware of any influences in your own theological understanding that might be rooted in a defective source? Will you commit to examining every premise you hold about Allah's attributes against the Quran and the way of the Salaf, no matter how comfortable it seems?

The Subsistence of Divine Will: Two Positions
Al-Nasafi presents positions on the subsistence of divine will and begins a rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major positions on whether Allah's will subsists in His essence and why the Mu'tazila position is considered rationally impossible.

Core Idea
Divine will must subsist in Allah's essence, not float as an accident.

Al-Nasafi begins by affirming that Allah is willing with a will that subsists in His essence. He then presents a disagreement among those who affirm a divine will: does it subsist in the essence or not? Ahl al-Haqq and the Karramiyya say yes; the Mu'tazila say it is a created accident not in a place. The passage sets up a rational argument against the latter view, though the proof is cut short.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of the sentence flow. Notice the key terms like 'قائمة بذاته', 'إرادة حادثة', and 'لا في محل'.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on the positions presented.
2
Identify the two camps
Find the two groups mentioned: Ahl al-Haqq (supported by Karramiyya) and the Mu'tazila (Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim). Note what each says about the will.
3
Understand the key terminology
Learn the meanings of 'qa'im bi-dhatihi' (subsisting in essence), 'irada haditha' (created will), and 'la fi mahall' (not in a place). These define the debate.
4
Consider the incomplete proof
The passage begins to argue that the Mu'tazila view is impossible because it implies an accident without a locus. Reflect on why this is a problem in kalam.
You can complete the reasoning: an accident cannot exist without a substance, so the will must subsist in the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله تعالى مريد بإرادة قائمة بذاته. ثم بعد ذلك اختلف القائلون بأنه تعالى مريد بإرادة، أنه مريد بإرادة قائمة بذاته أم مريد بإرادة قائمة لا بذاته. فقال أهل الحق : إنه مريد بإرادة قائمة بذاته. وساعدهم على هذا الكرامية، وقال أبو الهذيل العلاف وأبو علي الجبائي وابنه أبو هاشم: إن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة حادثة لا في محل. وما أقمناه من الدلالة قبل هذا على استحالة قيام عرض
Allah Most High is willing with a will subsisting in His essence (qa'im bi-dhatihi). Then after that, those who say that He is willing with a will differed: is He willing with a will subsisting in His essence, or willing with a will subsisting not in His essence? The People of Truth (ahl al-haqq) said: He is willing with a will subsisting in His essence. The Karramiyya supported them on this. And Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, and his son Abu Hashim said: Allah Most High is willing with a created will (irada haditha) not in a place (la fi mahall). And what we have established of evidence before this of the impossibility of an accident subsisting...
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage is a classification of positions and a brief argument. It begins with a heading stating that Allah is willing with a will subsisting in His essence (qāʾima bi-dhātihī). Then it explains that among those who affirm a will (irāda), there is a disagreement about its subsistence. Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth, i.e., Ash'aris and Maturidis) hold that the will subsists in the essence (qa'im bi-dhātihī). The Karramiyya supported them. The Mu'tazila (Abu al-Hudhayl, al-Jubba'i, and his son) said that Allah is willing with a created will (ḥāditha) not in a place (lā fī mahall). The text then begins to present a proof: 'And what we have established of evidence before this of the impossibility of an accident subsisting...' — here the passage cuts off, but the intended argument is that the Mu'tazilite view implies an accident subsisting without a locus, which is impossible (istiḥāla qiyām ʿaraḍ). Therefore, the will must subsist in the essence. The passage ends abruptly.

Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that their position leads to a logical impossibility: an accident (created will) cannot exist without a locus, so it must subsist in the essence.

Key Takeaway
The divine will must subsist in Allah's essence; the view that it is a floating accident is rationally untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Mu'tazilite Attributes of Essence vs. Action
Defines Mu'tazilite distinction between essence and action attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite distinction between permanent attributes of essence and transient attributes of action.

Core Idea
Attributes of essence are eternal; attributes of action are temporal and identical to the act.

The Mu'tazila developed a specific classification of divine attributes to solve the problem of God's relationship with creation. This passage presents their key distinction between attributes that are inseparable from God's essence and those that are merely descriptions of His actions. Understanding this difference is crucial for grasping later theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the key terms and how they are used to define the two categories.
The passage is concise; let the author's own wording guide your understanding.
2
Identify the two categories and their criteria
Find where the passage lists the distinguishing features: permanence (يتعمم) for essence attributes, and being subject to affirmation/negation for action attributes.
Essence attributes are never negated; action attributes can be both affirmed and negated.
3
Reflect on the implication of 'identical to the object acted upon'
Consider why the Mu'tazila equate action attributes with the act itself. How does this relate to their overall theology?
This identification avoids attributing eternal qualities to God that might imply change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أرجع إلى الذات وليس بمعنى وراء الذات، وما كان من صفات الفعل فهو عين المفعول وساعدهم أن الفرق بين صفة الذات وصفة الفعل أن ما لا ينفي بل يتعمم فهو صفة ذات كالعلم والقدرة. وما يثبت وينفى ولا يتعمم فهو صفة فعل على ما مر من بيان مذاهب المعتزلة
It returns to the essence and is not a meaning beyond the essence, and whatever is of the attributes of action is identical to the object acted upon. They (Mu'tazila) are aided by the fact that the difference between an attribute of essence and an attribute of action is that what is not negated but rather is permanent is an attribute of essence like knowledge and power. And what is affirmed and negated and is not permanent is an attribute of action, as has passed in the exposition of the schools of the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage defines the Mu'tazilite distinction between attributes of essence and attributes of action. It states that attributes of action are identical to the object acted upon. The difference is that attributes of essence are permanent (never negated), while attributes of action can be both affirmed and negated and are not permanent. Examples of essence attributes are knowledge and power. This establishes the basis for their later arguments about God's will.

Key Terms

عين المفعول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila completely deny all divine attributes.
Correction: They distinguish between eternal essence attributes (e.g., knowledge, power) and temporal action attributes that are identical to God's acts.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila as described, what distinguishes an attribute of essence from an attribute of action?
A) Attributes of essence are negated, attributes of action are affirmed.
B) Attributes of essence are permanent, attributes of action are not permanent.
C) Attributes of essence are created, attributes of action are eternal.
D) Attributes of essence are identical to the object, attributes of action are not.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite distinction hinges on permanence: essence attributes are everlasting, while action attributes are transient and identical to God's acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Which attributes do you forget are always present, and how might remembering them change your daily walk with God?
?
This lesson shows that the Mu'tazila see actions of God as identical to the acts themselves. Does your understanding of God's actions ever separate them from His essence? How does that affect your trust in His eternal nature?
?
The distinction between permanent and temporary attributes challenges you to consider: Do you rely more on God's eternal attributes (like knowledge and power) or on His specific actions in your life? What would change if you focused on His permanent attributes today?

The Principle: No Accident Without a Locus
Opening refutation against accident without locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite view that God's will is a created accident without a locus leads to absurd consequences, namely that all beings would share that attribute equally.

Core Idea
An accident without a locus would describe every being, making all equally willing.

The Mu'tazilites claimed that God's will is a created accident existing without a substratum. This lesson examines the first refutation: if an accident can subsist without a locus, then no particular essence is more qualified to be described by it, leading to the absurd conclusion that every being in the universe would be described by that will.

Guided Path
1
Read the opposing view
Start by reading the passage identifying the Mu'tazilite position: that Allah is willing (murid) by a created will (irada haditha) not in a locus (la fi mahal).
The named proponents are Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, Abu Ali al-Jubbai, and his son Abu Hashim.
2
Understand the established proof
Notice the author references a previous proof about the impossibility of an accident subsisting by itself (qiyam 'ard bi-nafsihi) without a locus. This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Follow the logical implication
The author argues: if the will exists without a locus, then no essence is more deserving of it than another. Therefore, Allah and every being in the world would be described as 'willing' by that will. This extends to all accidents.
This leads to an absurdity (muhal), which the author says has already been refuted.
4
Reflect on the consequence
Ask yourself: Why would this consequence be impossible? Consider the uniqueness of God's attributes and the universal implication.
This sets up the necessity of a locus for accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو الهذيل العلاف وأبو علي الجبائي وابنه أبو هاشم: إن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة حادثة لا في محل. وما أقمناه من الدلالة قبل هذا على استحالة قيام عرض بنفسه من غير محل يوجد فيه فهو الدليل ها هنا. يحققه أنها إذا قامت لا في محل لم يكن ذات أولى بالاتصاف بها من غيره، فينبغي أن يكون الله تعالى وجميع من في العالم مريدين بتلك الإرادة. وكذا هذا في سائر الأعراض. وهو محال وقد مر ذلك. يحققه أن عندهم كما يجوز وجود إرادة لا في محل، كذا يجوز وجود فناء لا في محل، فإن الله تعالى إذا أراد فناء المحدثات أحدث فناء لا في محل هو مضاد للحادثات أجمع فيفني به الجميع، ولا قدرة له على إفناء بعض العالم مع إبقاء البعض وهذا هو مذهب الجبائي وابنه. فيقال لهما : إذا كان الله تعالى هو المريد بإرادة حادثة لا في محل هلا كان هو الفاني بفناء حاث لا في محل، فلم كان كونه مريدا بهذه الإرادة دون غيره أولى من كونه فانيا بذلك الفناء دون غيره ؟ ولم كان كون غيره فإنما بذلك الفناء دونه أولى من كون غيره مريدا بتلك الإرادة دونه؟ وهذا مما لا محيص لهم عنه، ويدل عليه أن الإرادة لو جاز وجودها لا في محل وإن كان لا يتصور قيام الحياة لا في محل لكان ينبغي أن يجوز وجودها في محل لا حياة فيه، بل وجودها في محل لا حياة فيه أقرب من المعقول من وجودها لا في محل لما أنه من حيث إنها عرض يفتقر إلى المحل لاستحالة قيام الأعراض بذواتها ، ومن حيث إنها من خواص الحي يفتقر إلى وجود الحياة، وعند وجودها لا في محل انعدام كلا الشرطين، وعند وجودها في محل لا حياة فيه وجد أحد الشرطين وهو الشرط العام لجميع الأعراض، وانعدم ما هو الشرط الخاص لوجود ما هو من خواص
And Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf and Abu Ali al-Jubbai and his son Abu Hashim said: Allah is willing by a created will not in a locus. And the proof we have established before this on the impossibility of an accident subsisting by itself without a locus in which it exists is the proof here. Its verification: that if it subsists not in a locus, then no essence is more deserving of being described by it than another, so it would follow that Allah and everyone in the world are willing by that will. And likewise in the case of other accidents. That is impossible, and that has passed. Its verification: that according to them, just as it is possible for a will to exist not in a locus, so it is possible for an annihilation (fana') to exist not in a locus. For if Allah wills the annihilation of originated things, He creates an annihilation not in a locus that is contrary to all originated things collectively, and He annihilates all of them by it, and He has no power to annihilate some of the world while preserving others—and this is the school of al-Jubbai and his son. So it is said to them: If Allah is the willing by a created will not in a locus, why would He not be the annihilator (al-fani) by a created annihilation not in a locus? For why is His being willing by this will to the exclusion of others more appropriate than His being annihilated by that annihilation to the exclusion of others? And why is another's being annihilated by that annihilation to His exclusion more appropriate than another's being willing by that will to His exclusion? This is something they cannot escape. And it indicates that if will could exist not in a locus, even though the subsistence of life not in a locus is inconceivable, then it would be necessary that it be permissible for it to exist in a locus without life. Rather, its existence in a locus without life is closer to reason than its existence without any locus, because from the perspective that it is an accident needing a locus due to the impossibility of accidents subsisting by themselves, and from the perspective that it is a specific property of the living needing the existence of life. And when it exists not in a locus, both conditions are absent. And when it exists in a locus without life, one condition is found—the general condition for all accidents—while the specific condition for the existence of what is a specific property is absent.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that God's will is a created accident not in a locus. It begins by stating the opposing position (Abu al-Hudhayl, al-Jubbai, and his son). The first argument: if an accident exists without a locus, then no essence is more qualified to be described by it, so all beings would be willing—an absurdity. The second argument uses the analogy of annihilation (fana'): if will can exist without a locus, then so can annihilation; God could create such an annihilation, but then it would apply equally to all, and the questioner asks why God is described by will but not by annihilation. This reveals inconsistency. Finally, the author discusses conditions: will, as an accident, requires a locus (general condition), and as a property of the living, it requires life (specific condition). If it exists without a locus, both are absent; if it exists in a non-living locus, one condition is present, which is more reasonable. The argument aims to show that the opponents' position leads to logical problems and contradicts the nature of accidents.

Key Terms
إرادة حادثة
لا في محل
عرض
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An accident can exist without a locus it inheres in.
Correction: The passage argues that an accident existing without a locus would lead to the absurdity that all beings equally share that attribute, so a locus is necessary.

Checkpoint
What is one consequence mentioned if God's will existed as an accident not in a locus?
A) Only God would be willing.
B) All beings in the world would be willing.
C) God would have multiple wills.
D) Will would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
An accident cannot exist without a locus; the Mu'tazilite view of will without a locus collapses because it would make every being share that attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you claimed that God's will could exist without being in His essence, what would that imply about His unity and perfection? Are you willing to accept that your own reasoning might lead to an absurd conclusion about the Creator?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you ever assume they are like created things? This lesson shows that even a small mistake in attributing accidents leads to impossible consequences. Where in your understanding of Allah's names or attributes might you be holding onto an idea that contradicts His necessary perfection?
?
The Mu'tazilites sincerely sought to protect God's unity, yet their position collapsed logically. Have you ever defended a belief about Allah that, upon honest reflection, leads to a contradiction? Will you commit to examining your own assumptions about the divine attributes, even if it means letting go of a comfortable view?
