The Annihilation Analogy and Inconsistency
Analogy with annihilation to show inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy with annihilation (fana') exposes an inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite position: if will can exist without a locus, so can annihilation, leading to a dilemma about why God is described by will but not by annihilation.

Core Idea
If will can exist without a locus, so can annihilation—and that leads to a direct inconsistency.

The Mu'tazilites allowed a will without a locus. The author now uses an analogy: if that is possible, then annihilation (fana')—another accident—should also be possible without a locus. This creates a dilemma: why is God described as willing by such a will but not as annihilated by such an annihilation? The opponents cannot escape this logical trap.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy with annihilation
Read the passage starting with 'its verification: according to them...' The author draws an analogy: just as will can exist without a locus, so can annihilation (fana').
The Mu'tazilite view on annihilation is mentioned: God creates an annihilation without a locus that destroys all originated things at once.
2
Understand the dilemma
The author poses two questions: (1) If God is willing by a will without a locus, why is He not annihilated (fani) by an annihilation without a locus? (2) Why is His being willing by this will more appropriate than His being annihilated by that annihilation?
The same logic applies to others: why would another being be annihilated but not willing?
3
See the inconsistency
The point is that the opponents' principles lead to a contradiction: they allow will without locus but would not allow God to be described by annihilation without locus, yet there is no basis for the distinction.
The passage calls this 'something they cannot escape.'
4
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why this is a strong argument: it uses the opponents' own logic to show an absurd consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو الهذيل العلاف وأبو علي الجبائي وابنه أبو هاشم: إن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة حادثة لا في محل. وما أقمناه من الدلالة قبل هذا على استحالة قيام عرض بنفسه من غير محل يوجد فيه فهو الدليل ها هنا. يحققه أنها إذا قامت لا في محل لم يكن ذات أولى بالاتصاف بها من غيره، فينبغي أن يكون الله تعالى وجميع من في العالم مريدين بتلك الإرادة. وكذا هذا في سائر الأعراض. وهو محال وقد مر ذلك. يحققه أن عندهم كما يجوز وجود إرادة لا في محل، كذا يجوز وجود فناء لا في محل، فإن الله تعالى إذا أراد فناء المحدثات أحدث فناء لا في محل هو مضاد للحادثات أجمع فيفني به الجميع، ولا قدرة له على إفناء بعض العالم مع إبقاء البعض وهذا هو مذهب الجبائي وابنه. فيقال لهما : إذا كان الله تعالى هو المريد بإرادة حادثة لا في محل هلا كان هو الفاني بفناء حاث لا في محل، فلم كان كونه مريدا بهذه الإرادة دون غيره أولى من كونه فانيا بذلك الفناء دون غيره ؟ ولم كان كون غيره فإنما بذلك الفناء دونه أولى من كون غيره مريدا بتلك الإرادة دونه؟ وهذا مما لا محيص لهم عنه، ويدل عليه أن الإرادة لو جاز وجودها لا في محل وإن كان لا يتصور قيام الحياة لا في محل لكان ينبغي أن يجوز وجودها في محل لا حياة فيه، بل وجودها في محل لا حياة فيه أقرب من المعقول من وجودها لا في محل لما أنه من حيث إنها عرض يفتقر إلى المحل لاستحالة قيام الأعراض بذواتها ، ومن حيث إنها من خواص الحي يفتقر إلى وجود الحياة، وعند وجودها لا في محل انعدام كلا الشرطين، وعند وجودها في محل لا حياة فيه وجد أحد الشرطين وهو الشرط العام لجميع الأعراض، وانعدم ما هو الشرط الخاص لوجود ما هو من خواص
And Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf and Abu Ali al-Jubbai and his son Abu Hashim said: Allah is willing by a created will not in a locus. And the proof we have established before this on the impossibility of an accident subsisting by itself without a locus in which it exists is the proof here. Its verification: that if it subsists not in a locus, then no essence is more deserving of being described by it than another, so it would follow that Allah and everyone in the world are willing by that will. And likewise in the case of other accidents. That is impossible, and that has passed. Its verification: that according to them, just as it is possible for a will to exist not in a locus, so it is possible for an annihilation (fana') to exist not in a locus. For if Allah wills the annihilation of originated things, He creates an annihilation not in a locus that is contrary to all originated things collectively, and He annihilates all of them by it, and He has no power to annihilate some of the world while preserving others—and this is the school of al-Jubbai and his son. So it is said to them: If Allah is the willing by a created will not in a locus, why would He not be the annihilator (al-fani) by a created annihilation not in a locus? For why is His being willing by this will to the exclusion of others more appropriate than His being annihilated by that annihilation to the exclusion of others? And why is another's being annihilated by that annihilation to His exclusion more appropriate than another's being willing by that will to His exclusion? This is something they cannot escape. And it indicates that if will could exist not in a locus, even though the subsistence of life not in a locus is inconceivable, then it would be necessary that it be permissible for it to exist in a locus without life. Rather, its existence in a locus without life is closer to reason than its existence without any locus, because from the perspective that it is an accident needing a locus due to the impossibility of accidents subsisting by themselves, and from the perspective that it is a specific property of the living needing the existence of life. And when it exists not in a locus, both conditions are absent. And when it exists in a locus without life, one condition is found—the general condition for all accidents—while the specific condition for the existence of what is a specific property is absent.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that God's will is a created accident not in a locus. It begins by stating the opposing position (Abu al-Hudhayl, al-Jubbai, and his son). The first argument: if an accident exists without a locus, then no essence is more qualified to be described by it, so all beings would be willing—an absurdity. The second argument uses the analogy of annihilation (fana'): if will can exist without a locus, then so can annihilation; God could create such an annihilation, but then it would apply equally to all, and the questioner asks why God is described by will but not by annihilation. This reveals inconsistency. Finally, the author discusses conditions: will, as an accident, requires a locus (general condition), and as a property of the living, it requires life (specific condition). If it exists without a locus, both are absent; if it exists in a non-living locus, one condition is present, which is more reasonable. The argument aims to show that the opponents' position leads to logical problems and contradicts the nature of accidents.

Key Terms
فناء
لا في محل
إرادة حادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites think it is consistent to allow will without locus but deny annihilation without locus for God's description.
Correction: The passage shows that their own principle would force them to accept God as annihilated by annihilation without locus, creating a contradiction.

Checkpoint
What does the author say follows from the Mu'tazilite permission of will without a locus?
A) Annihilation without a locus is also possible.
B) Only will can exist without a locus.
C) Annihilation requires a locus.
D) God cannot will annihilation.

Key Takeaway
The annihilation analogy shows that if a will can exist without a locus, then an annihilation can too, leading to an inescapable inconsistency for the Mu'tazilites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your own life do you apply a different standard to yourself than to others, especially in religious obligations? Will you pause now and ask Allah to grant you consistency and fairness?
?
The passage forces us to see how a flawed assumption leads to absurdity. What assumption about Allah or yourself might you hold that, if followed logically, would lead to an inconsistency in your worship? Will you examine it today and align your belief with sound reasoning?

Conditions for Accidents: General and Specific
Analysis of conditions for accidents (general and specific).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the general condition (a locus) and specific condition (life) for accidents like will, and why existence without a locus is less reasonable than existence in a non-living locus.

Core Idea
An accident needs a locus; a property of the living needs life. Without a locus, both conditions fail.

The final argument analyzes the requirements for an accident like will. As an accident, it needs a locus (general condition). As a property of a living being (min khawas al-hayy), it also requires life (specific condition). The author argues that if will could exist without a locus, both conditions are absent, making it less reasonable than existing in a non-living locus, where at least one condition is met.

Guided Path
1
Read the analysis of conditions
Read the passage starting with 'And it indicates that if will could exist...' The author discusses two conditions: general (accident needs locus) and specific (property of living needs life).
2
Understand the comparison
The author says: if will could exist without a locus (even though life cannot without a locus), then it should be possible for it to exist in a non-living locus. In fact, existence in a non-living locus is 'closer to reason' than existence without any locus.
Because in a non-living locus, the general condition (locus) is present, even though the specific condition (life) is absent.
3
Follow the reasoning
When will exists without a locus, both conditions are absent (no locus, no life). When it exists in a non-living locus, one condition (locus) is present. So the latter is more plausible.
This shows that the Mu'tazilite position is even less reasonable than an alternative they would reject.
4
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how this argument complements the previous ones, focusing on the nature of accidents and their proper conditions.

Proof that Divine Will Cannot Exist Without a Locus
Imam Nasafi gives a brief proof that will cannot exist without a locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that divine will (and all accidents) must exist in a substrate, and why the Mu'tazilite position leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
If will had no locus, everyone would be equally willing—which is absurd.

Imam Nasafi presents a concise reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazilite claim that Allah's will can exist without inhering in a substrate. He shows that if that were possible, then God and all creatures would be equally described by that will, leading to an impossibility. This argument applies to all accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the logical flow: a conditional statement leading to an absurd consequence.
The argument is very compact. Pay attention to the word 'لو' (if) implied in the structure.
2
Understand the reductio structure
Identify the premise: 'if will subsists not in a locus.' Then see the consequence: no essence is more deserving, so all would be willing. This is the absurdity that refutes the premise.
3
Apply the principle to all accidents
Note that the author extends the same reasoning to all accidents. Reflect on why this is a universal rational principle in kalam.
Accidents cannot exist independently; they require a substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بنفسه من غير محل يوجد فيه فهو الدليل ها هنا. يحققه أنها إذا قامت لا في محل لم يكن ذات أولى بالاتصاف بها من غيره، فينبغي أن يكون الله تعالى وجميع من في العالم مريدين بتلك الإرادة. وكذا هذا في سائر الأعراض. وهو محال وقد مر ذلك.
By itself without a locus existing in it, that is the proof here. He verifies it that if it subsists not in a locus, no essence is more deserving of being described by it than another; so it would be necessary that God Most High and all who are in the world be willing by that will. And likewise this applies to all other accidents. That is impossible, and it has been mentioned before.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage presents a concise proof against the Mu'tazilite claim that divine will can exist without a locus (mahall). The author begins by stating that if a will existed by itself without inhering in any substrate, then no particular entity would have a greater right to be described as 'willing' by that will than any other. Consequently, both God and all creatures would equally be willing by that one will—an absurd conclusion. The same reasoning applies to all accidents, not just will. The author then declares this impossibility as already established ('it has been mentioned before'). The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: the Mu'tazilite premise leads to a contradiction, so the premise must be false.

Key Terms
محل (mahall) - locus, substrate
إرادة (irada) - will
عرض ('arad) - accident
ذات (dhat) - essence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof denies that God has will.
Correction: The proof only denies that will can exist without a substrate; it does not deny that God has will.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence the author derives from the assumption that will exists without a locus?
A) Only God would be willing.
B) All beings would equally be willing by that will.
C) Will would become eternal.
D) Will would disappear.

Key Takeaway
Will and all accidents must inhere in a substrate; otherwise, absurd consequences follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will were not a real attribute inhering in His essence, but rather something separate, what would that imply for your trust that His will is perfectly just and wise? How does this proof strengthen your conviction that God's will is uniquely His and not shared with creation?
?
The proof shows that a will without a locus would be absurd. Are there areas in your life where you treat God's will as something distant or detached from His essence, rather than as an expression of who He is? How can you realign your thinking to see His will as intimately connected to His perfect nature?

Objection to the Mu'tazila on Annihilation Without a Locus
Imam Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili claim that annihilation occurs without a locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi uses a reductio ad absurdum argument to refute the Mu'tazili doctrine of annihilation without a locus.

Core Idea
Consistency in reasoning exposes the absurdity of the Mu'tazili position.

Imam Nasafi continues his critique of Mu'tazili theology by targeting their claim that God can annihilate all created things without a locus. This lesson presents a logical objection that forces the Mu'tazila to confront an inconsistency in their own framework: if a will can exist without a locus and be attributed to God, then by the same logic an annihilation without a locus could be attributed to God—but God cannot be annihilated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full literal translation to grasp the argument in English.
Pay attention to the rhetorical questions.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili position
The passage states that the Mu'tazila allow both will and annihilation without a locus. Locate the sentence that describes this.
The view is attributed to al-Jubba'i and his son.
3
Understand the reductio
Nasafi asks: if God is the one willing by a will without a locus, why is He not also the one annihilated by an annihilation without a locus? This shows inconsistency because God cannot be annihilated.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Notice the structure
The argument has three parts: statement of their view, the objection (two rhetorical questions), and the conclusion that they cannot escape.
This logical progression is key to kalam debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أن عندهم كما يجوز وجود إرادة لا في محل، كذا يجوز وجود فناء لا في محل، فإن الله تعالى إذا أراد فناء المحدثات أحدث فناء لا في محل هو مضاد للحادثات أجمع فيفني به الجميع، ولا قدرة له على إفناء بعض العالم مع إبقاء البعض وهذا هو مذهب الجبائي وابنه. فيقال لهما : إذا كان الله تعالى هو المريد بإرادة حادثة لا في محل هلا كان هو الفاني بفناء حاث لا في محل، فلم كان كونه مريدًا بهذه الإرادة دون غيره أولى من كونه فانيا بذلك الفناء دون غيره ؟ ولم كان كون غيره فإنما بذلك الفناء دونه أولى من كون غيره مريدا بتلك الإرادة دونه؟ وهذا مما لا محيص لهم عنه.
He verifies it that according to them, just as it is permissible for a will to exist without a locus, so too is it permissible for an annihilation to exist without a locus. For God Most High, if He wills the annihilation of originated things, creates an annihilation without a locus that is opposed to all originated things, and thus annihilates them all. And He has no power to annihilate part of the world while preserving the rest. This is the doctrine of al-Jubba'i and his son. It is said to them: If God Most High is the one willing by a temporal will without a locus, why is He not the one annihilated by a temporal annihilation without a locus? Why is His being willing by this will to the exclusion of others more appropriate than His being annihilated by that annihilation to the exclusion of others? And why is another's being annihilated by that annihilation to the exclusion of Him more appropriate than another's being willing by that will to the exclusion of Him? This is something from which they have no escape.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument against the Mu'tazilite position. The author states that the Mu'tazila (specifically al-Jubba'i and his son) hold that God can create a will without a locus (mahall). By the same logic, they also accept that God can create an annihilation (fana') without a locus, which would destroy all originated things simultaneously, and God lacks the power to annihilate only part. The author then poses a dialectical objection: If God is the one willing by a temporal will without a locus, why is He not also the one annihilated by a temporal annihilation without a locus? In other words, if it is possible for a will to be created without a locus and be attributed to God as the one willing, then by parity of reasoning, an annihilation could also be created without a locus and be attributed to God as the one annihilated. But God cannot be annihilated, so this is self-defeating. The Mu'tazila have no answer ('no escape'). The passage follows the argumentative order: first stating the Mu'tazili view, then the objection, then the conclusion that they cannot evade.

Key Terms
فناء (fana') - annihilation
محل (mahall) - locus
محدث (muhdath) - originated
الجبائي (al-Jubba'i)
إرادة (irada) - will

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Nasafi is claiming that God can actually be annihilated.
Correction: Nasafi is using a reductio ad absurdum: he is not asserting that God is annihilated, but showing that the Mu'tazili reasoning leads to that impossible consequence.

Checkpoint
What is the objection the author raises against the Mu'tazila regarding annihilation without a locus?
A) Annihilation cannot exist at all.
B) If God can will without a locus, He can also be annihilated by the same logic, which is impossible.
C) Annihilation requires a locus.
D) The Mu'tazila do not believe in annihilation.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi defeats the Mu'tazili claim by showing that accepting annihilation without a locus leads to the impossible consequence that God could be annihilated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you ever unconsciously measure them by human standards? How can you train yourself to affirm God's transcendence without falling into such comparisons? What specific step will you take this week to correct a potentially anthropomorphic belief?
?
This lesson exposes the absurdity of holding a belief without considering its logical consequences. Are there any theological positions you hold that you have not rigorously tested for consistency? Will you commit to examining your beliefs with the same sharp reasoning shown here, even if it challenges your comfort?
?
The Mu'tazila allowed analogy (qiyas) between divine and human actions. How often do you employ casual analogies about God in your own thinking or speech? What harmful consequences could arise from such carelessness, and how will you guard against it starting today?

Will Requires Life and a Locus
Imam Nasafi argues from the nature of will that it requires life and therefore a locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Nasafi's layered argument that will, being an accident and a property of the living, requires both a locus and life, and why its existence without a locus is impossible.

Core Idea
Will needs both a home and life — without either, it cannot exist.

Imam Nasafi builds a tight logical argument to prove that will cannot exist without a locus. He compares two impossible scenarios to show that the more conditions are missing, the more impossible it is. This passage uses the concepts of general and specific conditions and appeals to consensus to solidify the proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two conditions
Will is an accident requiring a locus (general condition), and also a property of the living requiring life (specific condition). The passage presents these as two distinct needs.
Accidents cannot subsist by themselves; they need a substrate. Being a property of the living means it can only occur where life exists.
2
Compare two impossible scenarios
Compare existence without a locus (both conditions absent) vs. existence in a non-living locus (one condition met). Note that the latter is less impossible, yet still impossible.
This comparison uses the principle that the more conditions missing, the stronger the impossibility.
3
Grasp the consensus argument
Even the lesser impossibility (will in a non-living locus) is unanimously rejected by consensus. This strengthens the case for the greater impossibility (will without a locus).
Consensus (ijma') is a source of authority in Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the logical flow
Trace the argument from premise to conclusion: because the easier case is impossible, the harder case is even more so. This is a form of a fortiori argument.
This reasoning is common in kalam to establish necessary conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدل عليه أن الإرادة لو جاز وجودها لا في محل وإن كان لا يتصور قيام الحياة لا في محل لكان ينبغي أن يجوز وجودها في محل لا حياة فيه، بل وجودها في محل لا حياة فيه أقرب من المعقول من وجودها لا في محل لما أنه من حيث إنها عرض يفتقر إلى المحل لاستحالة قيام الأعراض بذواتها ، ومن حيث إنها من خواص الحي يفتقر إلى وجود الحياة، وعند وجودها لا في محل انعدام كلا الشرطين، وعند وجودها في محل لا حياة فيه وجد أحد الشرطين وهو الشرط العام لجميع الأعراض، وانعدم ما هو الشرط الخاص لوجود ما هو من خواص الحي. ثم انعقد الإجماع على استحالة وجودها في محل لا حياة فيه لفقد الشرط الخاص، فلأن يستحيل وجودها لا في محل كان أولى.
And it indicates that: if the existence of will without a locus were permissible—even though the subsistence of life without a locus is inconceivable—then it should be permissible for it to exist in a locus that has no life. Rather, its existence in a locus without life is more reasonable than its existence without a locus, because it, as an accident, depends on a locus due to the impossibility of accidents subsisting by themselves, and because it is among the properties of the living, it depends on the existence of life. When it exists without a locus, both conditions are absent; when it exists in a locus without life, one condition exists—the general condition for all accidents—while the specific condition for the existence of what is among the properties of the living is absent. Then consensus has been reached on the impossibility of its existence in a locus without life due to the absence of the specific condition, so the impossibility of its existence without a locus is even more likely.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage provides a layered argument proving that will cannot exist without a locus. The author begins by drawing an analogy: if will could exist without a locus, then it should at least be possible for it to exist in a non-living locus, because that scenario fulfills one condition (being in a locus) while the other (no locus) fulfills none. The argument is: will is an accident (arad), which as such requires a locus for its existence (general condition). Additionally, will is a property specific to living beings (khawass al-hayy), so it also requires life (specific condition). If will exists without a locus, both conditions are absent. If it exists in a non-living locus, the general condition is met, but the specific condition (life) is absent. Since even that scenario is impossible by consensus (ijma'), the scenario without a locus—where both conditions are absent—is even more impossible (awla). The logical structure is that the easier case (non-living locus) is impossible, so the harder case (no locus) is also impossible. The passage follows the author's order: stating the premise, the two conditions, the comparison, and the concluding consensus.

Key Terms
حياة (hayat) - life
خواص الحي (khawass al-hayy) - properties of the living
إجماع (ijma') - consensus
شرط عام (shart 'amm) - general condition
شرط خاص (shart khass) - specific condition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes life is an accident like will.
Correction: The argument treats life as a separate condition needed for will, not that will itself requires life to be an accident of the same type. Life is a different attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why is the impossibility of will without a locus even greater than its impossibility in a non-living locus?
A) Both conditions are absent when there is no locus.
B) Consensus only applies to the non-living locus case.
C) Will is not an accident.
D) Life can exist without a locus.

Key Takeaway
Will requires both a locus (as an accident) and life (as a property of the living); its impossibility without a locus is even stronger than its impossibility in a non-living locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your will depends on your life and your body—on a heart that beats and a brain that fires. Have you ever thanked Allah for sustaining the very conditions that make your choices possible, knowing that your next breath is His command?
?
The passage shows that will without a locus is unthinkable. But Allah's will is independent of any locus or life. When you plan your future, do you truly submit to His will, or do you act as if your will is self-sufficient?
?
You have will, but only because He gives you life. How does that dependency change the way you use your freedom? Will you today pause before a decision and say, 'If Allah wills'?

Refuting a Self-Caused Divine Will
Nasafi refutes the possibility of a self-caused divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite view that divine will is self-caused leads to absurdities and undermines the proof of a Creator.

Core Idea
A self-caused will would make the Creator unnecessary.

Imam Nasafi presents a logical trichotomy to examine the origin of the divine will. If the will were self-caused, it would open the door to all things being self-caused, negating the need for a Creator. This lesson unpacks the two specific corruptions that follow from self-causation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the trichotomy
Read the opening question that sets up three possibilities for the origin of will.
This is the framework for the entire argument.
2
Examine the first corruption
Identify why self-causation would generalize to all originated things, eliminating the need for a Maker.
3
Examine the second corruption
See how a non-occurring will would leave God non-choosing and unable to act.
This implies compulsion rather than free choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : أحدثت هذه الإرادة بإحداث الله تعالى إياها أم أحدثت بنفسها أم أحدثها غير الله تعالى؟ فإن قلت حدثت بنفسها ففيه فساد من وجهين. أحدهما: أنه لو جاز هذا في الإرادة لجاز في غيرها من الحادثات فبطلت دلالة ثبوت الصانع. والثاني أنها لو لم تحدث لبقي الصانع غير مختار ومن الجائز أنها لا تحدث أبدا، فلا يتصور من الله تعالى إحداث العالم ولا الخروج عن الاضطرار البتة، وحيث ثبت ذلك ثبت له بغيره لا بنفسه، وكل ذلك من أمارات الحدوث. وإن أحدثها غيره فهو فاسد من وجوه. أحدها: أن ذلك الغير إن كان قديما فهو القول بالقديمين، وكذا على قياس أصولهم جعل تلك الإرادة صفة لمن أحدثها أولى من جعلها صفة لمن لم يحدثها ولم تكن قائمة به، وإن كان ذلك الغير محدثا ، فإن حدث بنفسه فيه ما مر من جواز حدوث جميع العالم لا بالصانع، وإن أحدثه الصانع، أن أحدثه لا بإرادة فقد أحدثه مضطرا وهو فاسد، وكذا لو جاز هذا فيه لجاز في جميع المحدثات فتعطلت.
Then it is said to them: Did this will come to be by the bringing into existence of God Most High, or did it come to be by itself, or did someone other than God Most High bring it into existence? If you say it came to be by itself, then there is corruption in two respects. First: if this were permissible for will, it would be permissible for other originated things, so the indication of the existence of the Maker would be invalidated. Second: if it did not come to be, the Maker would remain non-choosing, and it is possible that it never comes to be, so it would not be conceivable for God Most High to bring the world into existence nor to escape from compulsion at all. And since that is established, it is established for Him by another, not by itself, and all of that is among the signs of origination. And if someone other than Him brings it into existence, then it is corrupt in several respects. First: if that other is eternal, then it is the assertion of two eternals. Likewise, on the analogy of their principles, making that will a description of the one who brings it into existence is more appropriate than making it a description of the one who did not bring it into existence and it did not subsist in him. And if that other is originated, then if it came to be by itself, it involves what has preceded regarding the permissibility of the origination of the entire world without a Maker; and if the Maker brought it into existence [but] without will, then He brought it into existence under compulsion, and that is corrupt. And likewise if this were permissible in it, it would be permissible in all originated things, so [the proof of the Maker] would be nullified.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive refutation of all possible accounts of the origin of the Mu'tazilite 'will without a locus'. The author poses a trichotomy: the will (irada) is either (1) created by God, (2) self-created, or (3) created by another. Each option is examined and shown to be impossible. If self-created (2), two problems arise: first, it would open the door to all originated things being self-caused, negating the need for a Creator; second, if it did not come to be, God would remain non-choosing (ghayr mukhtar) and could not act, implying His essence is determined. If created by another (3), then that other is either eternal or originated. If eternal, that leads to dualism (two eternals). Moreover, the attribute of will should be attributed to its originator, not to God. If the other is originated, then either it is self-caused (repeating the previous problem) or it is caused by God without will, implying compulsion (idtirar). The argument proceeds by eliminating all alternatives, thereby reinforcing the orthodox view that God's will is an attribute subsisting in His essence, not a separate accident. The passage follows a rigorous logical division (taqsim) common in kalam.

Key Terms
إحداث (ihdath) - bringing into existence
قديم (qadim) - eternal
محدث (muhdath) - originated
مختار (mukhtar) - choosing, volitional
اضطرار (idtirar) - compulsion
دلالة ثبوت الصانع (dalalat thubut al-sani') - indication of the existence of the Maker

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if will is self-caused, it preserves God's transcendence.
Correction: The argument shows it actually undermines the proof of a Creator and implies God is subject to compulsion.

Checkpoint
What is one of the two corruptions mentioned if the will came to be by itself?
A) It would imply that God is not willing.
B) It would invalidate the proof of the existence of the Maker.
C) It would make the will eternal.
D) It would require two gods.

Key Takeaway
Self-causation of any attribute leads to the collapse of the cosmological argument, so it cannot be attributed to God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time you attributed something to your own ability without recognizing Allah's creation of that event. How does this lesson remind you that even your will to choose is brought into existence by Allah?
?
If you truly believe that your will is dependent on Allah's creation, how should that change the way you approach your daily decisions and reliance on your own plans?
?
The argument shows that a self-caused will would make God unnecessary. In your life, are there areas where you act as if your will is independent of God? What would it look like to submit that will to Him today?

Refuting a Divine Will Created by Another
Nasafi refutes the possibility of divine will being created by another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite view that divine will is created by another leads to either dualism or compulsion.

Core Idea
A will created by another cannot be God's attribute.

The second possibility in the trichotomy is that God's will is created by another being. Imam Nasafi examines this option and shows it leads to two unacceptable conclusions: either the other is eternal (dualism) or it is originated and itself requires explanation. This lesson walks through the subcases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subcases
The author divides the case where another creates the will into eternal other and originated other.
2
Eternal other: dualism
If the other is eternal, that implies two eternal beings, which is against tawhid.
Also, the will should be attributed to its creator, not to God.
3
Originated other: regress
If the other is originated, it must be either self-caused or caused by God without will, leading to compulsion.
Both options are corrupt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then it is said to them: Did this will come to be by the bringing into existence of God Most High, or did it come to be by itself, or did someone other than God Most High bring it into existence? If you say it came to be by itself, then there is corruption in two respects. Second: if it did not come to be, the Maker would remain non-choosing, and it is possible that it never comes to be, so it would not be conceivable for God Most High to bring the world into existence nor to escape from compulsion at all. And since that is established, it is established for Him by another, not by itself, and all of that is among the signs of origination. And if someone other than Him brings it into existence, then it is corrupt in several respects. First: if that other is eternal, then it is the assertion of two eternals. And if that other is originated, then if it came to be by itself, it involves what has preceded regarding the permissibility of the origination of the entire world without a Maker; and if the Maker brought it into existence [but] without will, then He brought it into existence under compulsion, and that is corrupt. And likewise if this were permissible in it, it would be permissible in all originated things, so [the proof of the Maker] would be nullified.

This passage is a comprehensive refutation of all possible accounts of the origin of the Mu'tazilite 'will without a locus'. The author poses a trichotomy: the will (irada) is either (1) created by God, (2) self-created, or (3) created by another. Each option is examined and shown to be impossible. محدث (muhdath) - originated
اضطرار (idtirar) - compulsion
إرادة (irada) - will

Misconception: Some might think God's will could be created by a separate agent.
Correction: That agent would either be eternal (dualism) or originated (infinite regress or compulsion).

If the other that creates the will is eternal, what problem arises?
A) It implies God is not the only eternal being.
B) The will would not be God's attribute.
C) It leads to compulsion.

Key Takeaway
Any external cause for God's will either violates tawhid or leads to an infinite regress, thus the will must be an eternal attribute subsisting in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's will is uncaused and eternal, are there areas in your life where you secretly wish you could influence God's will as if it were weak or changeable? How does this passage expose that as a form of shirk in intent?
?
This passage shows that any external cause for God's will leads to absurdity. Do you truly live as if God's will is absolutely independent of your actions, or do you sometimes act as if your deeds manipulate His decree? What needs to change in your reliance on Him?
?
Nasafi's argument protects the majesty of God's will. Are you comfortable with a God whose will is not subject to any outside influence, even your prayers? How can your dua become a means of submission rather than an attempt to change His mind?

The Impossibility of Will Without a Locus
Proof that will cannot exist without a locus, using substance-accident analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of will cannot exist without a locus (mahall) and how the substance-accident analogy demonstrates this.

Core Idea
Will is an accident that must subsist in a body; it cannot exist independently.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a concise proof that the divine attribute of will cannot exist independently of a locus. By drawing on the inseparable relationship between substance and accident, he shows that the intellect rejects the notion of an accident existing without a substrate. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for our understanding of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two-part structure: a consensus-based argument followed by a verification using substance-accident analogy.
The passage is dense; reading twice helps.
2
Identify the consensus argument
The author first notes consensus (ijma') that will cannot exist in a lifeless locus. Understand why this is a premise for the stronger claim.
This sets up the principle that will requires a specific condition (life).
3
Understand the substance-accident analogy
The verification uses the inseparable relationship between substance (jawhar) and accident (arad). Intellect cannot separate them. Grasp how this analogy applies to will.
Will is treated as an accident that must subsist in a substance (a body).
4
Reflect on the reductio
The argument concludes that allowing an accident without a substance would lead to the absurdity of eternal substances. See why this is considered impossible.
This shows the necessity of a locus for will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحي. ثم انعقد الإجماع على استحالة وجودها في محل لا حياة فيه لفقد الشرط الخاص، فلأن يستحيل وجودها لا في محل كان أولى يحققه: أن الجواهر مع العرض مثلا زمان تأبى العقول انفراد أحدهما عن صاحبه، فلو جاز وجود عرض غیر قائم بجوهر، لجاز وجود جوهر من غير قيام عرض به وهو قلب العقول وفتح باب القول بقدم الجواهر.
Life. Then consensus was reached on the impossibility of its existence in a locus without life due to the loss of the special condition, so it is more appropriate that its existence without a locus be impossible. Its verification: that substances together with accidents, for example, the intellects reject the separation of one from the other. So if the existence of an accident not subsisting in a substance were permissible, then the existence of a substance without an accident subsisting in it would be permissible, which is the inversion of intellects and opening the door to the assertion of the eternity of substances.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the attribute of will (and similarly life) cannot exist without a locus (mahall). The author begins by noting the consensus (ijma') that it is impossible for will to exist in a locus devoid of life because the specific condition (shart khas) is missing. Then he argues that it is even more impossible for it to exist without any locus at all. He verifies this by analogy: substances (jawahir) and accidents (a'rad) are such that the intellect cannot conceive of one existing without the other. If an accident could exist not subsisting in a substance, then a substance could also exist without an accident inhering in it. That would overturn the intellect and open the door to the claim of the eternity of substances (qidam al-jawahir). This establishes that will must be an accident subsisting in a body.

Key Terms
إرادة
محل
جوهر
عرض
قدم الجواهر
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'life' mentioned here refers to the divine attribute of life, but the context is about the physical body and its life, not God's life.
Correction: The passage discusses life as a condition for will in created beings, not the divine attribute of life. The analogy is about physical bodies and their accidents.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the impossibility of an accident existing without a substance?
A) That will must exist in a living body
B) That will can exist without a locus
C) That substances are eternal
D) That life is unnecessary for will

Key Takeaway
Will cannot exist without a locus; it must inhere in a living body, just as an accident cannot exist without a substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your will is a fragile accident that ceases when your body dies. Are you investing your desires and plans in what lasts beyond your breath, or are you building castles on sand?
?
The proof shows that an accident cannot exist without a substance. In the same way, your will cannot act without the permission of the One whose will is not bound by a locus. Does your daily reliance on your own strength reflect this dependence?
?
If even the most basic rational principle—that an accident cannot exist without a substance—leads to the absurdity of a world without a Creator, why do you sometimes live as if God's role is optional?

intermediate
8 min
The Trilemma of Will's Origin: Self-Creation or Creation by Another Refuted
Trilemma refuting self-creation and creation by another for the divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of two possible origins for the divine will—self-creation and creation by another—and why only God's direct creation (without a prior will) is coherent.

Core Idea
Will cannot create itself, nor can another create it—only God's direct action is tenable.

This passage presents a trilemma: Did the will come into being by itself, by another, or by God? The author refutes the first two options through logical arguments, showing they lead to contradictions. Understanding this refutation is crucial for affirming that God's will is an eternal attribute, not a contingent accident. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the trilemma and first refutation
Begin by reading the trilemma carefully. Notice the three options presented: will created by itself, by another, or by God. Then focus on the two corruptions given if will is self-created.
The first corruption shows that if will can self-create, all accidents could, undermining the proof of a Creator.
2
Examine the second corruption of self-creation
The second corruption argues that if will never comes into being, God would remain non-choosing, which is impossible for the Creator. This shows that will must be created by God.
This corruption also implies that if will is self-created, God's act of creation would depend on a contingent will, which is absurd.
3
Analyze the refutation of creation by another
Now consider the second option: will created by something other than God. The author divides this into an eternal other (dualism) or a temporal other. Each leads to further absurdities.
Note the subcases: if temporal and self-created, same problem; if God created it without will, that is compulsion; if with a prior will, infinite regress.
4
Connect to the positive doctrine
The refutation of the first two options leaves only God's direct creation of will. However, this raises the question of how God creates without a prior will, which is addressed in subsequent lessons. For now, understand why the other options are impossible.
The goal is to show that the will cannot be a temporal accident; it must be eternal in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : أحدثت هذه الإرادة بإحداث الله تعالى إياها أم أحدثت بنفسها أم أحدثها غير الله تعالى؟ فإن قلت حدثت بنفسها ففيه فساد من وجهين. أحدهما: أنه لو جاز هذا في الإرادة لجاز في غيرها من الحادثات فبطلت دلالة ثبوت الصانع. والثاني أنها لو لم تحدث لبقي الصانع غير مختار ومن الجائز أنها لا تحدث أبدا، فلا يتصور من الله تعالى إحداث العالم ولا الخروج عن الاضطرار البتة، وحيث ثبت ذلك ثبت له بغيره لا بنفسه، وكل ذلك من أمارات الحدوث. وإن أحدثها غيره فهو فاسد من وجوه. أحدها: أن ذلك الغير إن كان قديما فهو القول بالقديمين، وكذا على قياس أصولهم جعل تلك الإرادة صفة لمن أحدثها أولى من جعلها صفة لمن لم يحدثها ولم تكن قائمة به، وإن كان ذلك الغير محدثا ، فإن حدث بنفسه فيه ما مر من جواز حدوث جميع العالم لا بالصانع، وإن أحدثه الصانع، أن أحدثه لا بإرادة فقد أحدثه مضطرا وهو فاسد، وكذا لو جاز هذا فيه لجاز في جميع المحدثات فتعطلت الإرادة، وإن أحدثه بإرادة منه فأني يتصور إحداث محدث للإرادة بإرادة لم يحدثها المحدث بعد.
Then it is said to them: Did this will come into being by the creation of God, or did it come into being by itself, or did something other than God cause it to come into being? If you say it came into being by itself, then there is corruption in two respects. First: if this were permissible for the will, it would be permissible for other temporal things, so the proof of the existence of the Maker would be invalidated. Second: if it did not come into being, then the Maker would remain non-choosing, and it is possible that it never comes into being at all, so it would not be conceivable for God to create the world nor to escape from compulsion at all, and since that is established, it is established for Him by another and not by itself, and all that are signs of temporality. And if something other than God caused it to come into being, then it is invalid from several aspects. First: if that other is eternal, then that is the assertion of two eternals, and likewise according to the analogy of their principles, making that will an attribute for the one who caused it to come into being is more appropriate than making it an attribute for the one who did not cause it to come into being and it was not subsisting in him. And if that other is temporal, then if it came into being by itself, then there is what has passed about the permissibility of the coming into being of the entire world without the Maker, and if the Maker caused it to come into being (but) He caused it to come into being without will, then He caused it to come into being under compulsion, which is invalid. Likewise, if this were permissible in it, it would be permissible in all temporal things, so the will would be incapacitated. And if He caused it to come into being by a will from Him, then how can it be conceived that a temporal being causes the coming into being of a will by means of a will that the temporal being has not yet caused to come into being?
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage presents a logical objection (istifsar) addressed to those who claim the will is accidental. It asks: is the will created by God, by itself, or by another? The author refutes the first two options in detail. If by itself, then all accidents could be self-creating, undermining the proof of a Creator, and also it would imply that God could remain non-choosing. If by another, then that other must be either eternal (leading to dualism) or temporal (leading to either self-creation problems or creation without will, which is compulsion). The third option (creation by God with a will) leads to infinite regress, which is addressed in the next lesson.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
أحداث (ihdath)
قديم (qadim)
محدث (muhdath)
اضطرار (idtirar)
اختيار (ikhtiyar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if will is not self-created, then it must be created by another being like an angel or a second god.
Correction: The author shows that creation by another leads to absurdities (dualism, compulsion, or infinite regress), so the only coherent position is that God's will is eternal and not created at all.

Checkpoint
What is the first corruption if the will came into being by itself?
A) It would permit other temporal things to be self-creating, invalidating the proof of a Creator
B) It would make God eternal
C) It would require a second creator
D) It would make will eternal

Key Takeaway
The will cannot be self-created or created by another; these lead to logical contradictions, affirming that God's will is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever live as if your own decisions create themselves, forgetting that every intention and choice depends on God's will to be realized? What would change if you started your day by acknowledging that even your capacity to choose comes from Him?
?
The passage warns that if the will were self-created, God could remain non-choosing forever. Have you ever felt anxious that God might not will good for you, like your faith or guidance is left to chance? How does knowing His will is eternal and actively willing your good reshape that fear into trust?
?
When you reflect on a past sin, do you blame external causes (people, environment) as 'creating' your wrong choice? The argument shows that no other being can create your will—you are directly accountable before God. How can you take full ownership of your choices today, without shifting responsibility, and repent sincerely?

Infinite Regress from Creating Will with Will
Author argues that creating will with will leads to infinite regress and materialism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creating the divine will with another will leads to an infinite regress that implies the eternity of the world, which is impossible for the Creator.

Core Idea
Creating will with will leads to an endless chain — a hallmark of materialism.

This passage presents a key argument against the idea that God creates His will by means of another will. The author shows that such a scenario forces an infinite regress, which he links to the materialist position. Let's trace the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two options presented for how God creates the will.
The passage begins with 'وإن أحدثها الباري هذا لا يخلو...'
2
Identify the first option and its consequence
The first option is that God creates the will with a will. The author argues that this leads to an infinite regress. Find the phrase 'فالكلام في الإرادة الثانية كالكلام في الأولى' and understand its implication.
3
Connect to Ma'mar's takwin and the materialists
The author compares this infinite regress to Ma'mar's theory of takwin and to the materialists' view. Reflect on why this comparison is made.
The materialists (dahriyya) argue that the whole universe is based on eternal principles; an infinite chain implies no beginning.
4
Understand the core problem
The problem is that if every will requires a preceding will, the existence of the world depends on an infinite series, which is impossible for a contingent world.
This shows the absurdity of the view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أحدثها الباري هذا لا يخلو إما أن أحدثها بإرادة أو أحدثها لا بإرادة (فإن أحدثها بإرادة ) فالكلام في الإرادة الثانية كالكلام في الأولى، وكذا في الثالثة والرابعة إلى ما لا يتناهى وفيه تعلیق وجود العالم بما لا يتناهى، من المعاني وهو مثل قول معمر في التكوين، وكلا المقولين مناسب لقول أهل الدهر يبطل الكل ببطلان البعض، ويصح الكل بصحة البعض، فمن صحح شيئا منها فقد قال بقدم العالم، ومن أبطل شيئا منها فقد أبطل الكل. وإن أحدثها لا بإرادة فهو في إحداثها إياها مضطر. وقد مر أنه من أمارات الحدث. ولأنه لو جاز إحداث الإرادة لا بإرادة لجاز إحداث جميع العالم لا بإرادة وخرجت هي من حد الإفادة وذلك باطل. ولأنها لو كانت حادثة لا في محل لكانت مقدورة له، ولو كانت هي مقدورة له لكانت أضدادها مقدورة إذ قدرته تعالى تتعلق بالمتضادين. يحققه أن عندهم كل ما لا يتعلق بالضدين فهو اضطرار لا قدرة، وإذا كان كذلك، ثم بوجود هذه الإرادة لا في محل لما كان هو المريد لأوجب ذلك أنه لو حدث بقدرته تعالى ضد من أضداد الإرادة كالسهو والموت والغفلة لكان هو المستحق اسم الساهي والميت والقاتل وذلك فاسد، فكذا هذا.
And if the Creator caused it to come into being, this is not free from either that He caused it to come into being with a will or that He caused it to come into being without a will. (If He caused it to come into being with a will), then the discussion about the second will is like the discussion about the first, and likewise about the third and fourth ad infinitum, and it involves suspending the existence of the world on an infinite chain of meanings. That is similar to the statement of Ma'mar about takwin. Both statements are appropriate to the statement of the materialists: the whole is invalidated by the invalidity of a part, and the whole is valid by the validity of a part. So whoever validates any part of them has asserted the eternity of the world, and whoever invalidates a part of them has invalidated the whole. And if He caused it to come into being without a will, then He is compelled in causing it to come into being. And it has already been mentioned that it is from the signs of temporality. And because if it were permissible to cause the will to come into being without a will, then it would be permissible to cause the entire world to come into being without a will, and it (the will) would depart from the category of causation, and that is false. And because if it (the will) were temporal without a locus, it would be within His power. And if it were within His power, then its opposites would be within His power, since His power is related to opposites. Its verification: according to them, whatever does not involve opposites is compulsion, not power. And if it is so, then, with the existence of this will without a locus, He would not be the willing (murid). This would necessitate that if, by His power, an opposite of will occurred, like forgetfulness, death, or heedlessness, then He would deserve the name 'forgetful', 'dead', or 'heedless', and that is false. So likewise this.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage examines the third option: that God creates the will. It splits into two sub-options: creation with a will or without a will. If with a will, that second will itself would need another will, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible and amounts to the view of the materialists (dahriyya). If without a will, then God would be acting under compulsion (idtirar), which is a sign of temporality. Additionally, if the will were temporal and not in a locus, it would be subject to God's power, but then its opposites would also be subject to His power, meaning that if God brought about an opposite of will (like heedlessness), He would acquire that attribute, which is absurd. Therefore, the will cannot be a temporal accident not subsisting in God; it must be an attribute of the essence.

Key Terms
تسلسل (tasalsul)
تكوين (takwin)
دهرية (dahriyya)
مريد (murid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress is just a theoretical possibility and does not lead to contradiction.
Correction: The author argues that infinite regress in creating the will would entail the eternity of the world, which is false.

Checkpoint
What does the author compare the infinite regress of wills to?
A) Ma'mar's takwin and materialist view
B) The creation of the universe
C) The necessity of God's attributes
D) The concept of compulsion

Key Takeaway
Creating the will with another will leads to an infinite chain that falsely implies the world is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, do you ever stop to consider what ultimate source gave you the will to choose? This lesson shows that assuming an infinite chain of wills leads to absurdity. Is your will really independent, or does it depend on the Creator's first will?
?
The materialists say the whole depends on the parts. Reflect on your daily actions: Do you sometimes act as if your own will is the ultimate cause, ignoring that every will is created by Allah? How can you ground your will in the recognition of Allah as the First Willer?
?
This passage warns that any infinite chain leads to the eternity of the world. In your own life, have you ever thought that your desires or intentions are endless cycles, never tracing back to Allah? Commit today to recognize that every desire must be attributed to the Creator, not to an endless loop.

Compulsion and Absurdity from Creating Will Without Will
Author argues that creating will without will leads to compulsion and absurd consequences for God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creating the will without a will implies compulsion in God and leads to absurd consequences regarding God's attributes.

Core Idea
Creating without will makes God compelled — and opens the door to attributing defects to Him.

The second horn of the dilemma is now examined: God creating the will without a prior will. The author argues that this would render God compelled (mudtarr), a clear sign of temporality. Moreover, he shows that if the will were a temporal accident not subsisting in God, it would be within God's power, but then its opposites would also be possible, leading to the absurdity that God could become forgetful or dead. This lesson unpacks this crucial argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second option. Note the claim that God would be compelled (مضطر).
2
Understand the compulsion argument
The author says that creating without will is compulsion, which is a sign of temporality. He also argues that if it were permissible for will, it would be permissible for everything, and the will would no longer be a cause.
Compulsion is incompatible with divine perfection.
3
Analyze the argument about power and opposites
The author argues that if the will is temporal and not in a locus, it is within God's power. Then its opposites (like forgetfulness) are also within God's power. But if God brought about an opposite, He would acquire that attribute, which is absurd.
This shows that the will cannot be a temporal accident separate from God.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Therefore, the will must be an attribute of God's essence, not a created thing.
The passage ends with 'فكذا هذا' indicating the same conclusion as before.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

That is similar to the statement of Ma'mar about takwin. Both statements are appropriate to the statement of the materialists: the whole is invalidated by the invalidity of a part, and the whole is valid by the validity of a part. So whoever validates any part of them has asserted the eternity of the world, and whoever invalidates a part of them has invalidated the whole. And if He caused it to come into being without a will, then He is compelled in causing it to come into being. And it has already been mentioned that it is from the signs of temporality. And because if it were permissible to cause the will to come into being without a will, then it would be permissible to cause the entire world to come into being without a will, and it (the will) would depart from the category of causation, and that is false. And because if it (the will) were temporal without a locus, it would be within His power. And if it were within His power, then its opposites would be within His power, since His power is related to opposites. This passage examines the third option: that God creates the will. It splits into two sub-options: creation with a will or without a will. God's Will Is an Attribute Subsisting in Him
Final conclusion that God's will is an attribute subsisting in Him, with noted disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's will must be an attribute subsisting in Him and the subsequent disagreement about its eternal or temporal nature.

Core Idea
God's will is an eternal attribute within His essence, not separate.

Having refuted the possibility that God's will could exist independently of Him, the author now establishes that it must be an attribute within His essence. However, a key disagreement remains: is that will eternal or created? This passage presents both the settled conclusion and the open question.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two logical parts: the establishment of a will subsisting in God, and the introduction of a disagreement.
2
Identify the logical moves
The first sentence concludes that since a will not subsisting in God is invalid, the will must subsist in Him. The second sentence introduces a difference of opinion after this point.
The disagreement is not resolved here.
3
Understand the two positions
The disagreement is between those who say the will is eternal (azaliyya) and those who say it is temporal (haditha) within the essence. Reflect on why this distinction matters for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا بطل أن يكون مريدا بإرادة غير قائمة به ثبت أنه مريد بإرادة قائمة به، وإذا ثبت ذلك فقد اختلفوا بعد هذا أنه مريد بإرادة أزلية قائمة به أم بإرادة حادثة في ذات.
Then when it is invalidated that He is willing by a will not subsisting in Him, it is established that He is willing by a will subsisting in Him. And when that is established, they have differed after this as to whether He is willing by an eternal will subsisting in Him or by a temporal will in the essence.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage presents the final conclusion of the preceding arguments. Since it has been proven that God cannot be willing by a will that is not subsisting in Him (because that would lead to the absurdities mentioned), it is established that His will is an attribute subsisting in His essence (qa'ima bihi). The author then notes that there is a disagreement among scholars after this point: some say the will is eternal (azaliyya) and subsisting in God, while others say it is temporal (haditha) within the essence. This sets up the next discussion on whether the divine attributes are eternal or temporal.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
قائمة به (qa'ima bihi)
أزلية (azaliyya)
حادثة (haditha)
ذات (dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the established conclusion after the invalidation of will not subsisting in God?
A) God is not willing
B) God is willing by a will not subsisting in Him
C) God is willing by a will subsisting in Him
D) The will is temporal

Key Takeaway
God's will is an attribute subsisting in His essence, but scholars disagree on whether it is eternal or temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where God's will seems incomprehensible to you, do you ever think that His will is something external to Him that could change? The lesson establishes that God's will is subsisting in Him. How does this truth challenge your tendency to question His decree? Write down one area of your life where you need to fully submit to His will as His own attribute.
?
The author mentions that after establishing the will subsists in Him, scholars disagree on whether it is eternal or temporal. This shows that even scholars have differences on details. In your own journey of faith, are there aspects of God's will that you find troubling? How does knowing that your understanding may be imperfect (as the scholars differ) humble you? What is one step you can take this week to trust in God's will despite not having all answers?
?
Imagine you meet someone who believes that God's will is a separate created thing. From this lesson, what would you say to them? But more importantly, do you live as though God's will is separate from Him—distant or impersonal? How can you cultivate a sense that His will is intimately connected to His essence? Commit to one action today that reflects this belief.

First Argument: Infinite Regress of Wills
Nasafi's first argument: infinite regress if will is created by a will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why positing a created will for God leads to an infinite regress and how that undermines the contingency of the world.

Core Idea
A created will requires another will, leading to an endless chain that makes the world pre-eternal.

Nasafi begins by dismantling the claim that God's will is temporal. He shows that if God creates His will by another will, an infinite regress follows. This is not just a logical puzzle—it has serious theological consequences, such as implying the pre-eternity of the world, which contradicts Islamic teaching.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part of the passage
Read from 'الإرادة، وإن أحدثه بإرادة منه' until 'فقد أبطل الكل'. Notice how Nasafi sets up the dilemma and follows the regress.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
If each act of will needs a prior will to be created, we get an infinite chain. Nasafi compares this to the Mu'tazilite Muʿammar's view on takwīn.
The regress would tie the world's existence to an endless series of wills.
3
Recognize the consequence
Nasafi says that if any part of the chain is valid, the whole chain is valid, which would mean the world is pre-eternal. If any part is invalid, the whole is invalid, which denies God's will.
This shows the absurdity: the temporality of will leads to either pre-eternity or nullification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإرادة، وإن أحدثه بإرادة منه فأني يتصور إحداث محدث للإرادة بإرادة لم يحدثها المحدث بعد. وإن أحدثها الباري هذا لا يخلو إما أن أحدثها بإرادة أو أحدثها لا بإرادة (فإن أحدثها بإرادة ) فالكلام في الإرادة الثانية كالكلام في الأولى، وكذا في الثالثة والرابعة إلى ما لا يتناهى وفيه تعلیق وجود العالم بما لا يتناهى، من المعاني وهو مثل قول معمر في التكوين، وكلا المقولين مناسب لقول أهل الدهر يبطل الكل ببطلان البعض، ويصح الكل بصحة البعض، فمن صحح شيئا منها فقد قال بقدم العالم، ومن أبطل شيئا منها فقد أبطل الكل. وإن أحدثها لا بإرادة فهو في إحداثها إياها مضطر. وقد مر أنه من أمارات الحدث. ولأنه لو جاز إحداث الإرادة لا بإرادة لجاز إحداث جميع العالم لا بإرادة وخرجت هي من حد الإفادة وذلك باطل. ولأنها لو كانت حادثة لا في محل لكانت مقدورة له، ولو كانت هي مقدورة له لكانت أضدادها مقدورة إذ قدرته تعالى تتعلق بالمتضادين. يحققه أن عندهم كل ما لا يتعلق بالضدين فهو اضطرار لا قدرة، وإذا كان كذلك، ثم بوجود هذه الإرادة لا في محل لما كان هو المريد لأوجب ذلك أنه لو حدث بقدرته تعالى ضد من أضداد الإرادة كالسهو والموت والغفلة لكان هو المستحق اسم الساهي والميت والقاتل وذلك فاسد، فكذا هذا.
The will, and if He brings it about by a will from Him, then how can it be conceived that an agent brings about a will by a will which the agent has not yet brought about? And if the Creator brings it about, this is not free from either bringing it about by a will or bringing it about without a will. If He brings it about by a will, then the discussion concerning the second will is like the discussion concerning the first, and likewise for the third and fourth ad infinitum, and that would entail linking the existence of the world to an infinite series of meanings, and it is like the saying of Muʿammar concerning takwīn (origination). And both statements are suitable to the saying of the materialists (ahl al-dahr): the whole is invalidated by the invalidity of a part, and the whole is validated by the validity of a part. Thus whoever validates something of them has posited the pre-eternity of the world, and whoever invalidates something of them has invalidated the whole. If He brings it about without a will, then in bringing it about He is compelled. And it has been mentioned that this is one of the marks of contingency. And because if bringing about a will without a will were permissible, then bringing about the entire world without a will would be permissible, and it would exit from the category of origination, and that is false. And because if it were contingent not in a locus, it would be within His power, and if it were within His power, then its opposites would be within His power, since His power—exalted is He—pertains to opposites. This is confirmed by the fact that in their view, whatever does not pertain to opposites is compulsion, not power. And if that is so, then if this will exists not in a locus, while He is not the willing one, it would necessitate that if by His power an opposite of the will occurred—such as forgetfulness, death, and heedlessness—He would deserve the name 'the forgetful one', 'the dead one', and 'the negligent one', and that is corrupt; thus likewise this.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments against the claim that God's will is created (ḥādith). The first argument addresses the scenario where God creates an act of will by another will, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). This is impossible because it would tie the existence of the world to an endless chain, similar to the view of the Mu'tazilite Ma'mar on takwīn. The second argument considers God creating will without a preceding will, which would make Him act under compulsion (mudṭarr), a sign of contingency. Moreover, if creating will without will were possible, creating the whole world without will would also be possible, negating the need for a will. The third argument examines the scenario where the will is a contingent accident not subsisting in a locus (lā fī maḥall). If it were an accident not in a locus, it would be within God's power. But then its opposites (like forgetfulness) would also be within His power, and if such an opposite occurred, God would be characterized by it (e.g., 'forgetful'), which is blasphemous. Thus all three possibilities fail, proving God's will cannot be temporal.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
تكوين (origination)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God creating His will by another will avoids anthropomorphism.
Correction: It leads to an infinite regress, which is impossible and implies the pre-eternity of the world.

Checkpoint
What does the infinite regress of wills entail according to Nasafi?
A) The world is created in time
B) The world is pre-eternal if any part is valid
C) God's will is simple
D) The regress is finite

Key Takeaway
The first argument shows that a created will requiring another will leads to an infinite regress with unacceptable consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument forces you to confront the infinite regress that arises when you assign a created origin to God's will. In your own life, do you ever find yourself caught in a cycle of seeking approval or meaning from created things, only to realize it never ends? How will you stop that regress today by anchoring your will solely in the Uncreated?
?
Nasafi warns that accepting any part of the infinite chain validates the whole, implying pre-eternity of the world. Are there compromises in your faith—small 'parts' you consider harmless—that you have allowed, thinking the whole of your iman remains intact? Will you examine them and uproot them before they invalidate everything?
?
The materialists' principle that 'the whole is invalidated by the invalidity of a part' is used here against infinite regress. In your own spiritual state, is there a part—a sin, a doubt, a neglect—that is invalidating the whole? What concrete step will you take today to restore that part and thus the whole?

Second Argument: Compulsion Without a Prior Will
Second argument: compulsion if will is created without a prior will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creating a will without a prior will would imply that God acts under compulsion, a sign of contingency.

Core Idea
If God creates will without a will, He acts compelled, which is a mark of contingency.

Nasafi now considers the other horn of the dilemma: what if God creates His will without any preceding will? He argues this would mean God is compelled in His action, which is impossible for the Necessary Being. Moreover, it would make it permissible to create the entire world without will, collapsing the distinction between voluntary and necessary acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument segment
Read from 'وإن أحدثها لا بإرادة' until 'وذلك باطل'. Note the two sub-arguments: compulsion and the reductio ad absurdum.
2
Analyze the compulsion claim
If God creates will without a will, He is compelled (mudṭarr). Nasafi reminds that compulsion is a sign of contingency (ḥādith).
This is based on earlier discussions about marks of contingency.
3
Understand the reductio
If creating will without a will is allowed, then creating the whole world without a will would be allowed, which removes the need for a will altogether. This is absurd because it negates the concept of origination (ifāda).
Thus the second option also fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The will, and if He brings it about by a will from Him, then how can it be conceived that an agent brings about a will by a will which the agent has not yet brought about? And if the Creator brings it about, this is not free from either bringing it about by a will or bringing it about without a will. Commentary
This passage presents three arguments against the claim that God's will is created (ḥādith). The first argument addresses the scenario where God creates an act of will by another will, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Thus all three possibilities fail, proving God's will cannot be temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever convinced yourself that you can act without a clear intention, thinking it doesn't matter? This lesson shows that even for the Necessary Being, acting without will implies compulsion and contingency. How then can you, a contingent being, justify any action without first consciously willing it? Will you commit to never doing anything without first asking yourself: 'Do I truly will this for the sake of Allah?'
?
The reductio in this argument shows that one exception leads to complete breakdown. In your life, are there small 'exceptions' you make that gradually erode your principles? What is one exception you are currently making that, if generalized, would destroy the very purpose of your actions? Write it down and resolve to eliminate it.
?
This lesson teaches that compulsion is a mark of contingency. Reflect on your own feelings of compulsion—times you feel forced to act. Do you see how that reveals your limitedness? How will you transform compulsion into willing submission to Allah's decree?

Third Argument: Contingent Will Not in a Locus
Third argument: a contingent will not in a locus leads to absurd attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a contingent will not subsisting in a locus would make God subject to opposite attributes, which is impossible.

Core Idea
If the will were a contingent accident not in a locus, its opposites would also be possible, implying blasphemous descriptions of God.

Nasafi's third argument tackles a different scenario: what if the created will is not an attribute subsisting in God (lā fī maḥall)? He shows that such a will would be within God's power, and then its opposites would also be within His power. If those opposites occurred, God would be described with negations like forgetfulness or death, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument segment
Read from 'ولأنها لو كانت حادثة لا في محل' to the end. Follow the logical chain.
2
Understand the principle of power over opposites
Nasafi states that God's power pertains to opposites. If the will is within His power, then its opposites are also within His power.
This is based on the kalam principle that power (qudra) relates to both a thing and its opposite.
3
See the objectionable consequence
If an opposite like forgetfulness occurred, God would be called forgetful, etc. Since that is corrupt, the initial assumption is false.
Thus the will cannot be a contingent accident not in a locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإرادة، وإن أحدثه بإرادة منه فأني يتصور إحداث محدث للإرادة بإرادة لم يحدثها المحدث بعد. وإن أحدثها الباري هذا لا يخلو إما أن أحدثها بإرادة أو أحدثها لا بإرادة (فإن أحدثها بإرادة ) فالكلام في الإرادة الثانية كالكلام في الأولى، وكذا في الثالثة والرابعة إلى ما لا يتناهى وفيه تعلیق وجود العالم بما لا يتناهى، من المعاني وهو مثل قول معمر في التكوين، وكلا المقولين مناسب لقول أهل الدهر يبطل الكل ببطلان البعض، ويصح الكل بصحة البعض، فمن صحح شيئا منها فقد قال بقدم العالم، ومن أبطل شيئا منها فقد أبطل الكل. وإن أحدثها لا بإرادة فهو في إحداثها إياها مضطر. وقد مر أنه من أمارات الحدث. ولأنه لو جاز إحداث الإرادة لا بإرادة لجاز إحداث جميع العالم لا بإرادة وخرجت هي من حد الإفادة وذلك باطل. ولأنها لو كانت حادثة لا في محل لكانت مقدورة له، ولو كانت هي مقدورة له لكانت أضدادها مقدورة إذ قدرته تعالى تتعلق بالمتضادين. يحققه أن عندهم كل ما لا يتعلق بالضدين فهو اضطرار لا قدرة، وإذا كان كذلك، ثم بوجود هذه الإرادة لا في محل لما كان هو المريد لأوجب ذلك أنه لو حدث بقدرته تعالى ضد من أضداد الإرادة كالسهو والموت والغفلة لكان هو المستحق اسم الساهي والميت والقاتل وذلك فاسد، فكذا هذا.

?
