?
The logic here forces you to accept that God's will must be eternal and inherent in His essence. Have you ever casually thought of God's will as something separate or changeable? How does this lesson challenge you to refine your understanding of who God is?
?

God's Eternal Will: The Maturidi Position
Nasafi establishes that God is willing by an eternal will and refutes the Karramiyya view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi theologians affirm that God's will is eternal and subsisting in His essence, and how they refute the Karramiyya view that God's will is temporal.

Core Idea
God's will is eternal and subsisting in His essence, not a temporal event.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi continues his discussion of divine attributes by addressing the nature of God's will. After establishing that God is willing by a will that subsists in Him, he presents the disagreement between the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) and the Karramiyya. The former affirm an eternal will, while the latter claim God has a capacity to will and creates temporal wills. Nasafi then refutes the Karramiyya position using prior arguments against temporal events in God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a very compressed style here with multiple layers.
2
Identify the two positions
Locate the Ahl al-Haqq's statement and the Karramiyya's statement. Note how they differ: eternal will vs. temporal will and murīdiyya.
3
Analyze the refutation
Nasafi gives multiple reasons for rejecting the Karramiyya view. Identify each reason: the impossibility of a locus for temporal events, the infinite regress of wills, and the resulting absurdities (temporality of Creator or pre-eternity of world).
Reflect on why these are devastating objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا بطل أن يكون مريدا بإرادة غير قائمة به ثبت أنه مريد بإرادة قائمة به، وإذا ثبت ذلك فقد اختلفوا بعد هذا أنه مريد بإرادة أزلية قائمة به أم بإرادة حادثة في ذات. قال أهل الحق : إنه مريد بإرادة أزلية قائمة بذاته (وكان في الأزل مريدا بإرادة قائمة به) وقالت الكرامية : إنه مريد بمريدية وكان في الأزل أيضا مريدا بمريدية وهي قدرته على الإرادة وإحداث العالم بإرادة حادثة في ذاته – تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا. وقد أبطلنا القول بكون ذاته محلا للحوادث قبل هذا. ثم جميع ما مر من الكلام أن الإرادة حدثت بإرادة أم لا بإرادة ومحدثها هو الله تعالى أم حدوثها بنفسها . كل ذلك يبطل هذا القول . ولست أدري أن هؤلاء الملحدين كيف يتكلمون مع الدهرية وأصحاب الهيولى ، وكيف يثبتون حدوث ذلك مع إجازتهم أن يكون القديم محلا، للحوادث، وقد مر أن القول بجواز ذلك يوجب إما القول بحدوث الصانع أو القول بقدم العالم ، وكل ذلك كفر ومحال . وقد استقصينا قبل هذا في كشف ذلك.
Then, if it is invalidated that He is willing by a will not subsisting in Him, it is established that He is willing by a will subsisting in Him. And when this is established, they differed after this whether He is willing by an eternal will subsisting in Him or by a temporal will in the essence. The people of truth (Ahl al-Ḥaqq) said: He is willing by an eternal will subsisting in His essence (and He was in pre-eternity willing by a will subsisting in Him). And the Karramiyya said: He is willing by a 'will-capacity' (murīdiyya), and He was in pre-eternity also willing by a will-capacity, which is His power over will and the origination of the world by a temporal will in His essence—exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation. And we have already invalidated the claim that His essence is a locus for temporal events before this. Then all the preceding discourse about whether the will occurred by a will or without a will, and whether its originator is God or its occurrence is by itself—all of that invalidates this statement. And I do not know how these heretics speak with the materialists and the proponents of matter (aṣḥāb al-hayūlā), and how they prove the temporality of that while allowing that the pre-eternal (al-qadīm) is a locus for temporal events; and it has been mentioned that the statement based on that permits either the statement of the temporality of the Maker or the statement of the pre-eternity of the world, and all of that is disbelief and impossible. And we have already made an exhaustive investigation in uncovering this.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage is a combined argument and classification. After refuting the temporality of God's will, Nasafi states that God must be willing by a will that subsists in Him. Then the dispute arises: is that will eternal (azalī) or temporal (ḥādith) in the essence? The Ahl al-Ḥaqq (i.e., the Māturīdīs and Ashʿarīs) affirm an eternal will subsisting in God's essence. The Karramiyya hold that God is willing by a 'murīdiyya' (a capacity to will), which is His power to create a temporal will in His essence. Nasafi rejects the latter on multiple grounds: it would make God's essence a locus for temporal events, which was previously disproven; all prior arguments about the impossibility of a temporal will apply; and permitting the pre-eternal to be a locus for temporal events leads to either the temporality of the Creator or the pre-eternity of the world, both of which are absurd and tantamount to disbelief.

Key Terms
إرادة أزلية
مريدية
محل للحوادث
قدم العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God's will is eternal, it implies multiple eternal entities separate from God.
Correction: The Maturidi view holds that the will is an attribute inherent in God's essence, not a separate entity.

Checkpoint
What is the Karramiyya position on God's will?
A) God is willing by an eternal will subsisting in His essence
B) God is willing by a capacity to will (murīdiyya) and creates a temporal will in His essence
C) God is willing by an eternal will but not subsisting in Him
D) God is not willing at all

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya view that God's essence contains temporal wills leads to absurdities; Maturidis affirm that God's will is eternal and subsisting in His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin repeatedly, do you secretly hope that God's will toward you can change, as if He might 'soften' or 'relent'? How does the truth that His will is eternal and unchanging confront that hope—and what does it demand of your repentance today?
?
If God's will cannot be affected by your actions, then every blessing and every trial is already determined by His eternal will. Does your heart truly rest in that sovereignty, or do you still bargain with God as if your deeds could shift His decree? What would it look like to submit to that truth right now?
?
The Karramiyya thought they protected God's freedom by allowing temporal wills in His essence. But you know that leads to absurdity. Have you ever, in your own mind, attributed 'change' to God (e.g., 'God was angry but now is pleased')? Confess that subtle anthropomorphism and affirm with your lips: God's will is one, eternal, and unchanging.

Refutation of Multiplicity of Wills and the Linguistic Argument
Nasafi refutes Karramiyya claim of multiple temporal wills and argues God wills all by one will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Karramiyya claim of multiple temporal wills is false, and how the linguistic argument supports the doctrine of a single divine will.

Core Idea
God's will is one, not many; language itself refutes the Karramiyya.

Imam Nasafi continues his theological argument against the Karramiyya sect, who claimed that God has as many wills as there are objects of will. In this passage, he first dismantles their linguistic reasoning, then reaffirms the orthodox position that God wills all things by a single eternal will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is divided into two main arguments.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage first attacks a linguistic claim about the derivation of 'willing' from the attribute of will. Then it presents the Karramiyya position and refutes it.
3
Understand the linguistic refutation
Nasafi argues that if the essence were not willing by the will subsisting in it but by a separate 'will-capacity', that would contradict how Arabic derives agent-names from attributes.
This is a key point against the Karramiyya's attempt to separate the attribute from the agent.
4
Understand the multiplicity refutation
The Karramiyya claim that each object of will requires a separate temporal will leads to an infinite number of wills if objects are infinite. Nasafi counters that God's one eternal will suffices for all objects.
5
Reflect on the analogy from the witnessed
The Karramiyya analogized from human experience where each act of will is separate. Nasafi implicitly rejects this analogy because God's will is not subject to temporal succession.
This is a common mistake in kalam: applying human limitations to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول : إن الذات الذي قامت به الإرادة ليس بمريد بها بل بغيرها وهو مريديته وهي قدرته على الإرادة وكذا الذات ليس بخالق وما الخالق القائم به بل القدرة على التخليق خروج عن معارف أهل اللسان وخلاف على جميع أهل اللغة في بيانهم مآخذ الاشتقاقات وهو باطل. ثم إذا ثبت هذا نقول : إنه تعالى مريد لمراداته أجمع بإرادة واحدة. وزعمت الكرامية: أن لكل مراد إرادة تحدث في ذاته فكانت إرادته على عدد مراداته واعتبروا بالشاهد ولنا أنه ما من مراد من مراداته تعالى إلا ويجوز تعلق إرادته
Then, the statement that the essence in which the will subsists is not willing by it but by something else, namely its 'will-capacity' (murīdiyya), which is its power over will, and likewise the essence is not a creator, and what creates is the power to create—this is a departure from the understandings of the people of language and a contradiction to all linguists in their explanation of the origins of derivations, and it is false. Then, when this is established, we say: He—exalted is He—is willing for all His objects of will by a single will. And the Karramiyya claimed that for every object of will there is a will that occurs in His essence, so that His wills equal the number of His objects of will, and they analogized by what is witnessed. And for us, there is no object of will among His objects of will except that the attachment of His will to it is permissible.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Karramiyya doctrine of multiple temporal wills. Nasafi first attacks their linguistic reasoning: if the essence is not willing by the will subsisting in it but by a separate 'murīdiyya' (a power to will), this contradicts how language derives agent-names from attributes. Similarly, saying that the essence is 'not creator' but 'the power to create' is the creator is unprecedented in Arabic semantics. After dismissing that, Nasafi reaffirms the Ahl al-Ḥaqq position: God wills all His objects of will by one single will. The Karramiyya claim that each object of will requires a separate temporal will arising in God's essence, such that there would be as many wills as objects—they analogize from created beings. Nasafi counters that God's will, being eternal and all-encompassing, can attach to any object of will without multiplicity or temporality. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is that since God's will is eternal, it can be related to all objects of will; no temporal succession or multiplicity is needed.

Key Terms
إرادة واحدة (single will)
مريديته (his will-capacity)
مآخذ الاشتقاقات (origins of derivations)
مراد (object of will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karramiyya believe that God's will is multiple and temporal, analogous to human will.
Correction: God's will is one, eternal, and attaches to all objects of will without multiplicity or temporality.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with the Karramiyya claim that each object of will requires a separate temporal will?
A) It implies God has an infinite number of wills
B) It makes God's will limited
C) It contradicts linguistic derivations
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
God's will is one, eternal, and attaches to all objects of will without multiplicity or temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's will as single and eternal, what mental habit in your life subtly assumes that God's will changes based on your actions? This week, what specific step can you take to replace that assumption with the conviction that every event is already encompassed by one eternal decree?
?
The Karramiyya's error was drawing an analogy from human experience to God. In what area of your life do you find yourself unconsciously limiting Allah's attributes—whether His mercy, justice, or power—based on human limitations? What concrete action will you take this week to remind yourself of His absolute transcendence?
?
Nasafi's argument hinges on the precision of Arabic language. How seriously do you prioritize learning the language of the Qur'an to truly understand Allah's names and attributes? What is one practical step you will commit to in the next 48 hours to deepen your knowledge of an Arabic term related to Allah's will?

6 min
The Two Positions: Ahl al-Haqq and Karramiyya on Divine Will
Nasafi states the Ahl al-Haqq and Karramiyya positions and begins refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contrasting positions of Ahl al-Haqq and the Karramiyya regarding God's will, and the initial refutation based on the impossibility of contingencies in the divine essence.

Core Idea
God's will is eternal and subsisting in His essence, not a created event.

Imam Nasafi begins by clearly stating the orthodox position: God is willing with an eternal will that is inherent to His essence. He then presents the deviation of the Karramiyya sect, who claim that God's will is a power to will and that He creates the world through a will that arises in His essence. This lesson introduces these two views and Nasafi's initial refutation, which rests on the principle that the eternal essence cannot be a locus for contingent events.

Guided Path
1
Read the two positions side by side
Read the Arabic passage once, taking note of how each group describes God's will. The Ahl al-Haqq use 'eternal will subsisting in essence'; the Karramiyya use 'will-power' and 'created will in essence'.
2
Identify the key difference
The Ahl al-Haqq see the will as eternal and inherent; the Karramiyya see it as a power that creates a will in time within the divine essence.
3
Understand the first refutation
Nasafi refers to a prior proof that the essence cannot be a locus for contingencies. If it could, it would lead to the temporal origination of God or the eternity of the world—both impossible.
This refutation sets the stage for later arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل الحق : إنه مريد بإرادة أزلية قائمة بذاته وكان في الأزل مريدا بإرادة قائمة به وقالت الكرامية : إنه مريد بمريدية وكان في الأزل أيضا مريدا بمريدية وهي قدرته على الإرادة وإحداث العالم بإرادة حادثة في ذاته – تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا. وقد أبطلنا القول بكون ذاته محلا للحوادث قبل هذا. ثم جميع ما مر من الكلام أن الإرادة حدثت بإرادة أم لا بإرادة ومحدثها هو الله تعالى أم حدوثها بنفسها . كل ذلك يبطل هذا القول . ولست أدري أن هؤلاء الملحدين كيف يتكلمون مع الدهرية وأصحاب الهيولى ، وكيف يثبتون حدوث ذلك مع إجازتهم أن يكون القديم محلا للحوادث، وقد مر أن القول بجواز ذلك يوجب إما القول بحدوث الصانع أو القول بقدم العالم ، وكل ذلك كفر ومحال . وقد استقصينا قبل هذا في كشف ذلك. ثم القول : إن الذات الذي قامت به الإرادة ليس بمريد بها بل بغيرها وهو مريديته وهي قدرته على الإرادة وكذا الذات ليس بخالق وما الخالق القائم به بل القدرة على التخليق خروج عن معارف أهل اللسان وخلاف على جميع أهل اللغة في بيانهم مآخذ الاشتقاقات وهو باطل. ثم إذا ثبت هذا نقول : إنه تعالى مريد لمراداته أجمع بإرادة واحدة. وزعمت الكرامية: أن لكل مراد إرادة تحدث في ذاته فكانت إرادته على عدد مراداته واعتبروا بالشاهد ولنا أنه ما من مراد من مراداته تعالى إلا ويجوز تعلق إرادته تعالى به على التعين فلو قيل : إنها تتعلق بالبعض دون البعض مع جواز تعلقها بما يتعلق به لكان اختصاصها بما اختصت به بجعل جاعل وتخصيص مخصص لا باقتضاء ذاتها لاستواء الكل في ذلك، والاختصاص بتخصيص المخصص وجعل الجاعل من أمارات الحدث. وقد أقمنا الدلالة على أنها أزلية فاندفع عنها تخصيص المخصص وجعل الجاعل، فكانت المرادات أجمع إرادة واحدة ولهذا قلنا: إنه تعالى عالم للمعلومات أجمع بعلم واحد، وكذا هذا في القدرة والسمع والبصر والكلام والتكوين. وظهر بهذا الفرق بين الأزلي من هذه الصفات وبين المحدث منها ، والله الموفق.
The people of truth said: He is willing with an eternal will subsisting in His essence, and He was in pre-eternity willing with a will subsisting in Him. The Karramiyya said: He is willing by a 'will-power' (muridiyya), and He was in pre-eternity also willing by a will-power, which is His power to will and to bring the world into existence by a created will in His essence – exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation. And we have already nullified the statement that His essence is a locus for contingent events before this. Then all that has passed of discussion – whether the will came into being by a will or not by a will, and its originator is Allah or it came into being by itself – all of that nullifies this statement. And I do not know how these atheists argue with the Dahriyya and the proponents of hyle, and how they prove the temporal origination of that while permitting the eternal to be a locus for contingencies, and it has passed that the statement permitting that necessitates either the statement of the temporal origination of the Maker or the statement of the eternity of the world, and all of that is disbelief and impossible. And we have exhaustively treated this before in exposing it. Then the statement: that the essence which has the will subsisting in it is not willing by it but by something else, namely its will-power, which is its power to will; likewise the essence is not creator, and the creator is not the one subsisting in it but rather the power to create – this is a departure from the understandings of the people of language and a contradiction of all linguists in their exposition of the roots of derivations, and it is false. Then when this is established we say: He, exalted, is willing for all His objects of will with one will. The Karramiyya claimed that for every object of will there is a will that occurs in His essence, so His wills are as many as His objects of will, and they analogized to the witnessed. And for us: there is no object of will among His objects of will except that His will's attachment to it is possible determinately. So if it were said that it attaches to some without others while the possibility of its attachment to what it attaches to exists, then its specification to what it is specified to would be by a making of a maker and a specifying of a specifier, not by the requirement of its essence because all are equal in that; and specification by the specifying of a specifier and the making of a maker are signs of contingency. And we have established the proof that it is eternal, so the specifying of a specifier and the making of a maker are repelled from it; thus all objects of will become one will. And because of this we say: He, exalted, is knower of all known things with one knowledge, and likewise this applies to power, hearing, sight, speech, and bringing-into-being. And by this the difference between the eternal among these attributes and the contingent among them appears. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and positive proof concerning the nature of God's will. The author first states the position of Ahl al-Haqq: God is willing with an eternal will that subsists in His essence. He then presents the view of the Karramiyya, who claim that God's will is a 'muridiyya' – a power to will – and that He creates the world by a created will that occurs in His essence. The author refutes this by referring to an earlier proof that God's essence cannot be the locus of contingent events. He then argues that regardless of how one explains the origination of a will in God's essence (whether by another will or not, whether by God or self-originating), it is all invalid. He sarcastically questions how these people can argue against materialists (Dahriyya and hylists) while themselves allowing the eternal to be a locus for contingencies, which leads either to the temporality of God or the eternity of the world – both impossible. Next, he attacks the Karramiyya linguistic claim that the essence is not called 'willing' by the will that inheres in it but by a separate 'muridiyya', arguing this contradicts linguistic principles of derivation. After establishing the falsity of their view, he presents the proof that one will suffices for all objects of will. The argument: any object of will is equally possible for God's will to attach to. If the will attached to some objects but not others, that would require a specifier (mukhassis) to cause the specification, which is a mark of contingency. But the will is proven eternal, so it cannot be contingent. Therefore, no specifier is needed, and the will applies to all objects equally as one will. He then analogizes to knowledge, power, etc., and concludes that this shows the difference between eternal attributes and contingent ones.

Key Terms
إرادة
أزلية
الكرامية
محل للحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Karramiyya, what is 'muridiyya'?
A) An eternal will subsisting in God's essence.
B) A power to will that is distinct from the will itself.
C) A created will that occurs in God's essence.
D) Both the power to will and the created will.

Key Takeaway
The orthodox view holds that God's will is eternal and inseparable from His essence, while the Karramiyya mistakenly believe in a will that occurs in God's essence, which compromises His eternality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's will, do you ever imagine it as something that 'happens' in time? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and what does that mean for your trust in God's decrees?
?
The Karramiyya claimed that God's will for creation is a new event within His essence. In your life, do you sometimes act as if God's will is reactive to your actions rather than eternal? How would acknowledging His eternal will change your patience in trials?
?
Nasafi says that allowing contingencies in God's essence leads to either making God temporal or the world eternal. Reflect: have you ever held any belief, even subtly, that compromised God's absolute perfection? What would it cost you to correct that?

intermediate
5 min
Refutation of the Linguistic Claim of the Karramiyya
Nasafi refutes the Karramiyya's linguistic separation of will from the name 'willing'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Karramiyya's argument that God is not called 'willing' by the will inherent in Him but by a separate 'muridiyya' is linguistically and theologically invalid.

Core Idea
The linguistic derivation from attributes to names is consistent; the Karramiyya's separation is arbitrary.

After establishing the futility of allowing contingencies in the divine essence, Nasafi turns to a specific argument of the Karramiyya: that the essence which has will is not called 'willing' by that will, but by something else, namely 'muridiyya' (will-power). Similarly, they claim that the essence is not 'creator' but the power to create is. Nasafi refutes this by appealing to the understanding of the people of language, arguing that this innovation contradicts the principles of Arabic derivational morphology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Karramiyya claim
They separate the attribute from the name: the essence is not willing by the will that inheres but by a separate power.
2
Identify Nasafi's refutation
He calls this a departure from the 'understandings of the people of language' and a contradiction of all linguists regarding the roots of derivations.
3
Reflect on the importance of linguistic consistency
Theology must respect natural language meanings; arbitrary redefinitions break the link between attribute and name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل الحق : إنه مريد بإرادة أزلية قائمة بذاته وكان في الأزل مريدا بإرادة قائمة به وقالت الكرامية : إنه مريد بمريدية وكان في الأزل أيضا مريدا بمريدية وهي قدرته على الإرادة وإحداث العالم بإرادة حادثة في ذاته – تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا. وقد أبطلنا القول بكون ذاته محلا للحوادث قبل هذا. ثم جميع ما مر من الكلام أن الإرادة حدثت بإرادة أم لا بإرادة ومحدثها هو الله تعالى أم حدوثها بنفسها . كل ذلك يبطل هذا القول . ولست أدري أن هؤلاء الملحدين كيف يتكلمون مع الدهرية وأصحاب الهيولى ، وكيف يثبتون حدوث ذلك مع إجازتهم أن يكون القديم محلا للحوادث، وقد مر أن القول بجواز ذلك يوجب إما القول بحدوث الصانع أو القول بقدم العالم ، وكل ذلك كفر ومحال . وقد استقصينا قبل هذا في كشف ذلك. ثم القول : إن الذات الذي قامت به الإرادة ليس بمريد بها بل بغيرها وهو مريديته وهي قدرته على الإرادة وكذا الذات ليس بخالق وما الخالق القائم به بل القدرة على التخليق خروج عن معارف أهل اللسان وخلاف على جميع أهل اللغة في بيانهم مآخذ الاشتقاقات وهو باطل. ثم إذا ثبت هذا نقول : إنه تعالى مريد لمراداته أجمع بإرادة واحدة. وزعمت الكرامية: أن لكل مراد إرادة تحدث في ذاته فكانت إرادته على عدد مراداته واعتبروا بالشاهد ولنا أنه ما من مراد من مراداته تعالى إلا ويجوز تعلق إرادته تعالى به على التعين فلو قيل : إنها تتعلق بالبعض دون البعض مع جواز تعلقها بما يتعلق به لكان اختصاصها بما اختصت به بجعل جاعل وتخصيص مخصص لا باقتضاء ذاتها لاستواء الكل في ذلك، والاختصاص بتخصيص المخصص وجعل الجاعل من أمارات الحدث. وقد أقمنا الدلالة على أنها أزلية فاندفع عنها تخصيص المخصص وجعل الجاعل، فكانت المرادات أجمع إرادة واحدة ولهذا قلنا: إنه تعالى عالم للمعلومات أجمع بعلم واحد، وكذا هذا في القدرة والسمع والبصر والكلام والتكوين. وظهر بهذا الفرق بين الأزلي من هذه الصفات وبين المحدث منها ، والله الموفق.
The people of truth said: He is willing with an eternal will subsisting in His essence, and He was in pre-eternity willing with a will subsisting in Him. The Karramiyya said: He is willing by a 'will-power' (muridiyya), and He was in pre-eternity also willing by a will-power, which is His power to will and to bring the world into existence by a created will in His essence – exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation. And we have already nullified the statement that His essence is a locus for contingent events before this. Then all that has passed of discussion – whether the will came into being by a will or not by a will, and its originator is Allah or it came into being by itself – all of that nullifies this statement. And I do not know how these atheists argue with the Dahriyya and the proponents of hyle, and how they prove the temporal origination of that while permitting the eternal to be a locus for contingencies, and it has passed that the statement permitting that necessitates either the statement of the temporal origination of the Maker or the statement of the eternity of the world, and all of that is disbelief and impossible. And we have exhaustively treated this before in exposing it. Then the statement: that the essence which has the will subsisting in it is not willing by it but by something else, namely its will-power, which is its power to will; likewise the essence is not creator, and the creator is not the one subsisting in it but rather the power to create – this is a departure from the understandings of the people of language and a contradiction of all linguists in their exposition of the roots of derivations, and it is false. Then when this is established we say: He, exalted, is willing for all His objects of will with one will. The Karramiyya claimed that for every object of will there is a will that occurs in His essence, so His wills are as many as His objects of will, and they analogized to the witnessed. And for us: there is no object of will among His objects of will except that His will's attachment to it is possible determinately. So if it were said that it attaches to some without others while the possibility of its attachment to what it attaches to exists, then its specification to what it is specified to would be by a making of a maker and a specifying of a specifier, not by the requirement of its essence because all are equal in that; and specification by the specifying of a specifier and the making of a maker are signs of contingency. And we have established the proof that it is eternal, so the specifying of a specifier and the making of a maker are repelled from it; thus all objects of will become one will. And because of this we say: He, exalted, is knower of all known things with one knowledge, and likewise this applies to power, hearing, sight, speech, and bringing-into-being. And by this the difference between the eternal among these attributes and the contingent among them appears. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and positive proof concerning the nature of God's will. The author first states the position of Ahl al-Haqq: God is willing with an eternal will that subsists in His essence. He then presents the view of the Karramiyya, who claim that God's will is a 'muridiyya' – a power to will – and that He creates the world by a created will that occurs in His essence. The author refutes this by referring to an earlier proof that God's essence cannot be the locus of contingent events. He then argues that regardless of how one explains the origination of a will in God's essence (whether by another will or not, whether by God or self-originating), it is all invalid. He sarcastically questions how these people can argue against materialists (Dahriyya and hylists) while themselves allowing the eternal to be a locus for contingencies, which leads either to the temporality of God or the eternity of the world – both impossible. Next, he attacks the Karramiyya linguistic claim that the essence is not called 'willing' by the will that inheres in it but by a separate 'muridiyya', arguing this contradicts linguistic principles of derivation. After establishing the falsity of their view, he presents the proof that one will suffices for all objects of will. The argument: any object of will is equally possible for God's will to attach to. If the will attached to some objects but not others, that would require a specifier (mukhassis) to cause the specification, which is a mark of contingency. But the will is proven eternal, so it cannot be contingent. Therefore, no specifier is needed, and the will applies to all objects equally as one will. He then analogizes to knowledge, power, etc., and concludes that this shows the difference between eternal attributes and contingent ones.

Key Terms
مريدية
اشتقاقات
أهل اللسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject the Karramiyya's claim that God is not called 'willing' by the will inherent in Him?
A) Because it contradicts the teachings of the Karramiyya themselves.
B) Because it violates the universal linguistic principle of deriving active participles from verbal nouns.
C) Because it does not affect the belief in God's will.
D) Because it denies the existence of God's will.

Key Takeaway
The linguistic link between an attribute and the name of the one possessing it is direct and governed by established derivational patterns; the Karramiyya's attempt to insert an intermediary is linguistically invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the names of God like al-Murīd or al-Khāliq, do you instinctively consider the linguistic link between the name and the attribute? What does it reveal about His essence that you might otherwise overlook?
?
Are you willing to submit your theological reasoning to the authority of Arabic linguistic consensus, even when your own logic suggests a different path? What hidden pride might resist this submission?
?
If God's attributes directly give rise to His names, how should that transform the way you call upon Him (du'a) or describe Him to others? Will you commit to learning one name's derivation this week?

Divine Attributes
advanced
8 min
Proof That One Eternal Will Suffices for All Objects of Will
Nasafi proves that one eternal will suffices for all objects of will using the argument from specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument proving that God's eternal will, being one, encompasses all objects of will without multiplicity.

Core Idea
An eternal will needs no specifier; hence it applies equally to all objects of will as one will.

Having refuted the Karramiyya's position, Nasafi now presents the positive proof that God's will is one and suffices for everything He wills. The argument hinges on the principle that if the will were multiple or specified to some objects, it would require a specifier, which is a sign of contingency. But since the will is proven eternal, it is beyond specification, covering all objects uniformly.

Guided Path
1
Follow the argument from premise to conclusion
Start with the possibility of the will attaching to any object determinately. Then consider what if it attaches only to some.
2
Understand the role of 'specifier'
If the will attaches to some objects but not others, that specification must come from an external specifier, which indicates contingency.
3
Apply the eternity of the will
Since the will is eternal, it cannot be contingent; therefore no specifier is needed, and it applies to all objects equally, making the will one.
4
Connect to other attributes
Nasafi analogizes to knowledge, power, etc., showing the same principle applies.
This connection will be explored in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل الحق : إنه مريد بإرادة أزلية قائمة بذاته وكان في الأزل مريدا بإرادة قائمة به وقالت الكرامية : إنه مريد بمريدية وكان في الأزل أيضا مريدا بمريدية وهي قدرته على الإرادة وإحداث العالم بإرادة حادثة في ذاته – تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا. وقد أبطلنا القول بكون ذاته محلا للحوادث قبل هذا. ثم جميع ما مر من الكلام أن الإرادة حدثت بإرادة أم لا بإرادة ومحدثها هو الله تعالى أم حدوثها بنفسها . كل ذلك يبطل هذا القول . ولست أدري أن هؤلاء الملحدين كيف يتكلمون مع الدهرية وأصحاب الهيولى ، وكيف يثبتون حدوث ذلك مع إجازتهم أن يكون القديم محلا للحوادث، وقد مر أن القول بجواز ذلك يوجب إما القول بحدوث الصانع أو القول بقدم العالم ، وكل ذلك كفر ومحال . وقد استقصينا قبل هذا في كشف ذلك. ثم القول : إن الذات الذي قامت به الإرادة ليس بمريد بها بل بغيرها وهو مريديته وهي قدرته على الإرادة وكذا الذات ليس بخالق وما الخالق القائم به بل القدرة على التخليق خروج عن معارف أهل اللسان وخلاف على جميع أهل اللغة في بيانهم مآخذ الاشتقاقات وهو باطل. ثم إذا ثبت هذا نقول : إنه تعالى مريد لمراداته أجمع بإرادة واحدة. وزعمت الكرامية: أن لكل مراد إرادة تحدث في ذاته فكانت إرادته على عدد مراداته واعتبروا بالشاهد ولنا أنه ما من مراد من مراداته تعالى إلا ويجوز تعلق إرادته تعالى به على التعين فلو قيل : إنها تتعلق بالبعض دون البعض مع جواز تعلقها بما يتعلق به لكان اختصاصها بما اختصت به بجعل جاعل وتخصيص مخصص لا باقتضاء ذاتها لاستواء الكل في ذلك، والاختصاص بتخصيص المخصص وجعل الجاعل من أمارات الحدث. وقد أقمنا الدلالة على أنها أزلية فاندفع عنها تخصيص المخصص وجعل الجاعل، فكانت المرادات أجمع إرادة واحدة ولهذا قلنا: إنه تعالى عالم للمعلومات أجمع بعلم واحد، وكذا هذا في القدرة والسمع والبصر والكلام والتكوين. وظهر بهذا الفرق بين الأزلي من هذه الصفات وبين المحدث منها ، والله الموفق.
The people of truth said: He is willing with an eternal will subsisting in His essence, and He was in pre-eternity willing with a will subsisting in Him. The Karramiyya said: He is willing by a 'will-power' (muridiyya), and He was in pre-eternity also willing by a will-power, which is His power to will and to bring the world into existence by a created will in His essence – exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation. And we have already nullified the statement that His essence is a locus for contingent events before this. Then all that has passed of discussion – whether the will came into being by a will or not by a will, and its originator is Allah or it came into being by itself – all of that nullifies this statement. And I do not know how these atheists argue with the Dahriyya and the proponents of hyle, and how they prove the temporal origination of that while permitting the eternal to be a locus for contingencies, and it has passed that the statement permitting that necessitates either the statement of the temporal origination of the Maker or the statement of the eternity of the world, and all of that is disbelief and impossible. And we have exhaustively treated this before in exposing it. Then the statement: that the essence which has the will subsisting in it is not willing by it but by something else, namely its will-power, which is its power to will; likewise the essence is not creator, and the creator is not the one subsisting in it but rather the power to create – this is a departure from the understandings of the people of language and a contradiction of all linguists in their exposition of the roots of derivations, and it is false. Then when this is established we say: He, exalted, is willing for all His objects of will with one will. The Karramiyya claimed that for every object of will there is a will that occurs in His essence, so His wills are as many as His objects of will, and they analogized to the witnessed. And for us: there is no object of will among His objects of will except that His will's attachment to it is possible determinately. So if it were said that it attaches to some without others while the possibility of its attachment to what it attaches to exists, then its specification to what it is specified to would be by a making of a maker and a specifying of a specifier, not by the requirement of its essence because all are equal in that; and specification by the specifying of a specifier and the making of a maker are signs of contingency. And we have established the proof that it is eternal, so the specifying of a specifier and the making of a maker are repelled from it; thus all objects of will become one will. And because of this we say: He, exalted, is knower of all known things with one knowledge, and likewise this applies to power, hearing, sight, speech, and bringing-into-being. And by this the difference between the eternal among these attributes and the contingent among them appears. And Allah is the giver of success.

Commentary

He sarcastically questions how these people can argue against materialists (Dahriyya and hylists) while themselves allowing the eternal to be a locus for contingencies, which leads either to the temporality of God or the eternity of the world – both impossible. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will is multiple, corresponding to each act of will (e.g., one for creating, one for guiding, etc.).
Correction: Nasafi proves that one eternal will suffices for all objects because specification would imply contingency, which cannot apply to the eternal will.

Checkpoint
What is the key reason Nasafi gives for rejecting multiple wills in God?
A) Multiple wills would imply multiple gods.
B) Multiple wills would require a specifier, which indicates contingency, but the will is eternal.
C) The Karramiyya said so.
D) Multiple wills would make God's actions inconsistent.

Key Takeaway
The eternity of God's will precludes the need for a specifier, proving that a single will encompasses all objects of will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson proves God's will is one and unchanging. In your life, where have you assumed that God's will is fragmented or contradictory because your desires conflict with His decree? How can you actively submit your will to His one perfect will starting today?
?
The Karramiyya projected human limitations onto God by imagining multiple wills. In what specific areas do you still think of God as having human-like limitations? What practical steps can you take to internalize His transcendence and oneness in will?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
4 min
Analogy to Other Divine Attributes and Conclusion
Nasafi extends the proof from will to other attributes and concludes with the distinction between eternal and contingent attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi extends the argument from will to other divine attributes (knowledge, power, etc.) and concludes that this clarifies the distinction between eternal and contingent attributes.

Core Idea
The same logic that proves one will proves one knowledge, one power, etc., defining the nature of Allah's attributes.

After establishing the unity and eternity of the will, Nasafi draws an analogy to the other divine attributes: knowledge, power, hearing, sight, speech, and bringing-into-being (takwīn). He asserts that just as one will suffices for all objects of will, one knowledge suffices for all known things, etc. This analogy reinforces the principle that eternal attributes are simple and all-encompassing, unlike contingent attributes which are multiple and limited. The lesson also serves as a conclusion, highlighting the fundamental difference between the eternal attributes of Allah and contingent attributes of creation.

Guided Path
1
Note the extension of the argument
Nasafi claims the same reasoning applies to knowledge, power, hearing, sight, speech, and takwīn.
2
Understand the implication
Each attribute is one, eternal, and encompasses all its objects without multiplicity.
3
Grasp the final distinction
This is the criterion to differentiate between eternal attributes (which are one and all-encompassing) and contingent attributes (which are multiple and limited).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل الحق : إنه مريد بإرادة أزلية قائمة بذاته وكان في الأزل مريدا بإرادة قائمة به وقالت الكرامية : إنه مريد بمريدية وكان في الأزل أيضا مريدا بمريدية وهي قدرته على الإرادة وإحداث العالم بإرادة حادثة في ذاته – تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا. وقد أبطلنا القول بكون ذاته محلا للحوادث قبل هذا. ثم جميع ما مر من الكلام أن الإرادة حدثت بإرادة أم لا بإرادة ومحدثها هو الله تعالى أم حدوثها بنفسها . كل ذلك يبطل هذا القول . ولست أدري أن هؤلاء الملحدين كيف يتكلمون مع الدهرية وأصحاب الهيولى ، وكيف يثبتون حدوث ذلك مع إجازتهم أن يكون القديم محلا للحوادث، وقد مر أن القول بجواز ذلك يوجب إما القول بحدوث الصانع أو القول بقدم العالم ، وكل ذلك كفر ومحال . وقد استقصينا قبل هذا في كشف ذلك. ثم القول : إن الذات الذي قامت به الإرادة ليس بمريد بها بل بغيرها وهو مريديته وهي قدرته على الإرادة وكذا الذات ليس بخالق وما الخالق القائم به بل القدرة على التخليق خروج عن معارف أهل اللسان وخلاف على جميع أهل اللغة في بيانهم مآخذ الاشتقاقات وهو باطل. ثم إذا ثبت هذا نقول : إنه تعالى مريد لمراداته أجمع بإرادة واحدة. وزعمت الكرامية: أن لكل مراد إرادة تحدث في ذاته فكانت إرادته على عدد مراداته واعتبروا بالشاهد ولنا أنه ما من مراد من مراداته تعالى إلا ويجوز تعلق إرادته تعالى به على التعين فلو قيل : إنها تتعلق بالبعض دون البعض مع جواز تعلقها بما يتعلق به لكان اختصاصها بما اختصت به بجعل جاعل وتخصيص مخصص لا باقتضاء ذاتها لاستواء الكل في ذلك، والاختصاص بتخصيص المخصص وجعل الجاعل من أمارات الحدث. وقد أقمنا الدلالة على أنها أزلية فاندفع عنها تخصيص المخصص وجعل الجاعل، فكانت المرادات أجمع إرادة واحدة ولهذا قلنا: إنه تعالى عالم للمعلومات أجمع بعلم واحد، وكذا هذا في القدرة والسمع والبصر والكلام والتكوين. وظهر بهذا الفرق بين الأزلي من هذه الصفات وبين المحدث منها ، والله الموفق.

What does Nasafi identify as the distinguishing factor between eternal attributes and contingent attributes?
A) Eternal attributes are multiple, while contingent are one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah’s single knowledge encompasses every detail of your life—your thoughts, intentions, and actions—how does that change the way you live this very day? What specific thought or action will you be more mindful of because you know He knows it?
?
If Allah’s one power controls everything that happens to you, including your successes and failures, are you truly relying on Him alone? What is one area of your life where you are acting as if your own multiple efforts are sufficient, and how will you turn to His single power starting now?

The passage distinguishes between eternal attributes (like Allah's) and contingent ones (like ours). In what ways have you been imagining Allah's attributes as limited or human-like? What one step will you take today to correct that misconception in your heart and worship Him as He truly is?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Unity of the Eternal Divine Attributes
Concludes argument on divine attributes, showing their unity and distinction from temporal ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine attributes—especially the will—must be unitary and eternal, and how this distinguishes them from temporal attributes.

Core Idea
God's one eternal will encompasses all objects of will without particularization.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla concludes a proof about the unity of God's attributes. The author argues that if the eternal will were specific to some objects but not others, it would require an external specifier—a sign of temporality. Since we have already established the eternity of God's attributes, the will must be one and undivided. This reasoning extends to all attributes: knowledge, power, hearing, sight, speech, and bringing-into-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the conditional structure starting with 'If it were said...'.
2
Identify the hypothetical objection
The author considers the possibility that the will relates to some objects but not others. This sets up the need for a response.
3
Follow the argument
See how the author argues that such particularization would require an external maker or specifier, which are signs of temporality. Then he invokes the established proof of eternity to reject that possibility.
The key move is that the attributes are already proven eternal, so particularization cannot apply.
4
Apply the reasoning to other attributes
Recognize that the same argument applies to knowledge, power, hearing, sight, speech, and takwīn. All are one unitary eternal attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى به على التعين فلو قيل : إنها تتعلق بالبعض دون البعض مع جواز تعلقها بما يتعلق به لكان اختصاصها بما اختصت به بجعل جاعل وتخصيص مخصص لا باقتضاء ذاتها لاستواء الكل في ذلك، والاختصاص بتخصيص المخصص وجعل الجاعل من أمارات الحدث. وقد أقمنا الدلالة على أنها أزلية فاندفع عنها تخصيص المخصص وجعل الجاعل، فكانت المرادات أجمع إرادة واحدة ولهذا قلنا: إنه تعالى عالم للمعلومات أجمع بعلم واحد، وكذا هذا في القدرة والسمع والبصر والكلام والتكوين. وظهر بهذا الفرق بين الأزلي من هذه الصفات وبين المحدث منها، والله الموفق.
He is exalted. If it were said: They (the will) relate to some but not others, despite the possibility of relating to all, then their specificity to what they are specific to would be by a maker's making and a specifier's specifying, not by their essence's necessity, because everything is equal in that. And specification by a specifier's specifying and a maker's making are signs of temporality. And we have established the proof that they are eternal, so the specifying of a specifier and the making of a maker are repelled from them. Thus all objects of will became (one will). For this we said: He, exalted, is knower of all objects of knowledge with one knowledge, and similarly for power, hearing, sight, speech, and bringing-into-existence. And by this the distinction between the eternal among these attributes and the temporal among them becomes clear. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage concludes a proof regarding the unity of divine attributes, specifically the divine will (irāda). It argues that if the eternal will related to some objects of will but not others, that particularization would require an external specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) or maker (jā‘il), which are signs of temporality (ḥadath). Since we have already proven that the attributes are eternal, no such particularization is possible. Therefore, all objects of will are willed by a single eternal will (irāda wāḥida). This same reasoning applies to knowledge (‘ilm), power (qudra), hearing (sam‘), sight (baṣar), speech (kalām), and bringing-into-existence (takwīn)—each is one eternal attribute. The passage thus establishes the distinction between eternal (azalī) and temporal (muḥdath) attributes, emphasizing the unity and simplicity of God's eternal attributes.

Key Terms
إرادة
أزلية
حدث
صفات
تخصيص
جاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God has multiple wills because He wills many things.
Correction: This passage clarifies that His will is one eternal attribute covering all objects of will, because multiplicity would require external specification, which is a sign of temporality.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the divine will cannot relate to some objects of will but not others?
A) Because God's knowledge necessitates equal relation
B) Because particularization would require an external specifier, which is a sign of temporality
C) Because the will is multiple by nature
D) Because objects of will are all equal in essence

Key Takeaway
God's eternal attributes are each one and undivided, because any multiplicity would require an external cause, which is impossible for the Eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is one undivided act, encompassing every event from the smallest to the greatest, then how can you ever feel that anything happens outside His control? In what area of your life are you still acting as if God's will is fragmented, as if some things escape His single decree? Call out that area to yourself right now.
?
You affirm that God's knowledge is one and all-encompassing. But when you pray, do you truly believe He already knows your need and is already acting from His single eternal will? What would change in your dua if you internalized that His knowledge and will are not multiple or separated by time?
?
The lesson says that if God's will were particularized, it would need a maker — and that is impossible for the Eternal. Now look at your own faith: do you sometimes imagine that God's attributes are like human attributes, multiple and shifting? Where in your heart do you still measure God by your own limits? Repent from that and commit to affirming His absolute oneness.

Hikmah as an Eternal Attribute of Allah
The author discusses ḥikmah as a divine attribute, its eternal nature, and Ash'ari's distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Māturīdī and Ashʿarī positions on the attribute of wisdom (ḥikmah) differ, and why Ashʿarī made a distinction between wisdom as knowledge and wisdom as action.

Core Idea
Wisdom is an eternal attribute of perfection, but Ashʿarī distinguishes its essence from its action.

This passage from Tabṣirat al-Adilla tackles the divine attribute of wisdom (ḥikmah). The author first affirms that wisdom is a praiseworthy attribute, then clarifies the Māturīdī position that Allah possesses wisdom eternally, regardless of whether it is considered knowledge or action. He then contrasts this with Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī’s nuanced view, which distinguishes between wisdom as an attribute of essence (when meaning knowledge) and as an attribute of action (when meaning action). The passage also includes an explanation from Ibn Fūrak about why Ashʿarī gave a divided answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the numbered parentheses in the Arabic—they are footnote markers from the edition.
2
Identify the three theological positions
The passage presents the Māturīdī position, then the Ashʿarī position, then Ibn Fūrak's explanation of Ashʿarī's stance. Find each one and notice how they are introduced.
3
Distinguish attributes of perfection vs deficiency
The passage starts by classifying ḥikmah as an attribute of praise and perfection, and foolishness as deficiency. Understand why this framing matters for the discussion.
This sets the context that wisdom is necessarily attributed to Allah.
4
Reflect on Ashʿarī's distinction
Consider why Ashʿarī distinguishes between wisdom as knowledge (attribute of essence) and wisdom as action. How does Ibn Fūrak's explanation about linguistic differences help understand this?
The common misunderstanding is that Ashʿarī denies ḥikmah as an attribute entirely—this text shows he affirms it as an attribute of essence when it means knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الحكمة من صفات المدح والكمال. والسفه من صفات النقص، فكان الله تعالى موصوفا بأنه حكيم، ووردت به نصوص لا خفاء بها، وهو يتعالى عن الوصف بالسفه ثم (2) الحكمة سواء كانت من قبيل العلم أو من قبيل الفعل والعمل أو كانت اسما لهما فالله تعالى موصوف بها (3) في الأزل عندنا على ما مر من بيان مذاهبنا. وأبو الحسن الأشعري ذهب إلى أن الحكمة إذا أريد بها العلم فهي صفة ذات، وإذا أريد بها الفعل فلا، وهذا (4) هو قياد مذهبه. قال أبو بكر بن فورك (5): وأصلها للعلم عند الأشعري وإنما قسم الجواب لأنه عرف اختلاف أهل اللغة فيها،
Then wisdom (al-ḥikmah) is among the attributes of praise and perfection. And foolishness (al-safah) is among the attributes of deficiency. So God Almighty is described as being Wise (ḥakīm), and texts have come with this, no ambiguity about them, and He is exalted above being described with foolishness. Then wisdom, whether it is of the category of knowledge or of the category of action and deed, or is a name for both, God Almighty is described by it in pre-eternity according to us, as has passed in the exposition of our schools. And Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī held that wisdom, if intended as knowledge, is an attribute of essence, and if intended as action, it is not; and this is the leadership of his school. Abū Bakr ibn Fūrak said: Its origin for al-Ashʿarī is knowledge, and he divided the answer only because he recognized the difference of the people of language regarding it.
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage deals with ḥikmah as a divine attribute. The author first establishes that ḥikmah is a praiseworthy attribute of perfection, while safah is a deficiency; thus Allah is described as Ḥakīm (Wise) by clear texts. Then the author addresses the nature of this attribute: according to the Māturīdī school, whether ḥikmah is considered as knowledge, as action, or as a name encompassing both, Allah is described by it in pre-eternity (fī al-azal), consistent with their earlier doctrine. In contrast, Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī distinguished: if ḥikmah means knowledge, it is an attribute of essence (ṣifat dhāt), but if it means action, it is not (meaning it is an attribute of action). The author then cites Ibn Fūrak explaining that for Ashʿarī, the root of ḥikmah is knowledge, and he gave a divided answer only because he was aware of the differing linguistic definitions of ḥikmah. This shows Ashʿarī's position is a compromise between the linguistic and theological dimensions.

Key Terms

صفات النقص
حكيم
صفة ذات

أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ashʿarī denies ḥikmah as an attribute of Allah entirely.
Correction: Ashʿarī affirms ḥikmah as an attribute of essence when it means knowledge, but not as an attribute of action; he does not deny it entirely.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ashʿarī as explained in this passage, what is the case when ḥikmah is intended as knowledge?
A) It is an attribute of action.
B) It is an attribute of essence.
C) It is neither an attribute of essence nor action.
D) It is a created attribute.

Key Takeaway
The attribute of wisdom is eternal in Allah according to both schools, but the Ashʿarī school distinguishes between its essence (knowledge) and its act, while the Māturīdī school considers it a single eternal attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful trial that seems pointless, do you truly affirm that Allah’s wisdom encompasses both His perfect knowledge and His wise actions? How will you specifically remind yourself of this divine attribute in your next hardship?
?
Is there an area of your life where you secretly doubt the wisdom of Allah's decree—perhaps because you reduce wisdom to mere knowledge, forgetting that His actions are also perfectly wise? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to surrender to His wisdom in that area.

The Attribute of Wisdom (al-Hikma) and Scholarly Disagreements
Discusses the divine attribute of wisdom and differing views of al-Ash'ari, Ibn Furak, and al-Qalanisi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine attribute of wisdom and the different scholarly views on whether it is an attribute of essence or action.

Core Idea
Wisdom is praised; scholars differ on whether it is knowledge or action.

This passage from Tabsirat al-Adilla establishes that wisdom is an attribute of perfection belonging to Allah. It then presents the positions of the Maturidis, al-Ash'ari, Ibn Furak, and al-Qalanisi, showing how each school defines wisdom in relation to knowledge and action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and its literal translation to get the author's wording.
2
Identify the common ground
Notice that all agree God is described as wise and that wisdom is praiseworthy. Find the statement that God is described as wise by texts.
This is the foundation.
3
Distinguish the three positions
The passage reports views of al-Ash'ari, Ibn Furak's clarification, and al-Qalanisi. Write down what each says about whether wisdom is knowledge or action.
Compare them to the Maturidi position stated first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكمة صفة الله تعالى في الأزل. ثم الحكمة من صفات المدح والكمال. والسفه من صفات النقص، فكان الله تعالى موصوفا بأنه حكيم، ووردت به نصوص لا خفاء بها، وهو يتعالى عن الوصف بالسفه. ثم الحكمة سواء كانت من قبيل العلم أو من قبيل الفعل والعمل أو كانت اسما لهما فالله تعالى موصوف بها في الأزل عندنا على ما مر من بيان مذاهبنا. وأبو الحسن الأشعري ذهب إلى أن الحكمة إذا أريد بها العلم فهي صفة ذات، وإذا أريد بها الفعل فلا، وهذا هو قياد مذهبه. قال أبو بكر بن فورك: وأصلها للعلم عند الأشعري وإنما قسم الجواب لأنه عرف اختلاف أهل اللغة فيها. وأبو العباس القلانسي جعلها من باب الفعل لا غير فكأنه ذهب إلى أنها من الإتقان أو المنع.
Wisdom is an attribute of Allah, exalted, in pre-eternity. Moreover, wisdom is among the attributes of praise and perfection. And foolishness is among the attributes of deficiency. Thus Allah, exalted, is described as wise, and texts about this have come which are not hidden, and He is exalted above being described with foolishness. Moreover, wisdom, whether it belongs to the category of knowledge or to the category of act and deed, or is a name for both of them, Allah, exalted, is described with it in pre-eternity according to us, as has passed in the exposition of our schools. And Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari went to the view that wisdom, if knowledge is intended by it, is an attribute of essence, but if act is intended by it, then it is not; and this is the guide of his school. Abu Bakr ibn Furak said: Its root for al-Ash'ari is knowledge, and he only divided the answer because he knew of the disagreement of the people of language concerning it. And Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi made it of the category of act exclusively, as if he went to the view that it is from perfection or prevention.
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of wisdom (al-hikma) and presents three scholarly positions. The author begins by stating that wisdom is an attribute of praise and perfection, while foolishness (al-safah) is an attribute of deficiency. God is described as wise by explicit texts. Then, regardless of whether wisdom is classified as knowledge or action, the Maturidi school affirms it in pre-eternity. Next, the author reports the view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari: if wisdom means knowledge, it is an attribute of essence (i.e., eternal), but if it means action, it is not an essential attribute. Ibn Furak clarifies that for al-Ash'ari wisdom is fundamentally knowledge, and his division in answer came because he acknowledged linguistic disagreement. Finally, al-Qalanisi held that wisdom is exclusively a matter of act, interpreting it as perfection or prevention. The passage thus maps the major Kalam views on whether wisdom is a substantive attribute of God or a description of His actions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think wisdom is merely an action and not an eternal attribute.
Correction: The Maturidi position affirms wisdom in pre-eternity regardless of whether it is knowledge or action, showing it is an attribute of God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari regarding the attribute of wisdom (al-hikma)?
A) It is a matter of knowledge and a matter of action equally.
B) It is a matter of knowledge only (when knowledge is intended, it is an attribute of essence).
C) It is a matter of action only.
D) It is neither knowledge nor action.

Key Takeaway
The divine attribute of wisdom is affirmed by all, but scholars differ on whether it is an essential attribute (knowledge) or an action; the Maturidi school affirms it in pre-eternity as either or both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The scholars debated whether wisdom is knowledge or action. How does this affect your trust in God’s actions when you cannot see the wisdom behind your trials? What is one situation where you need to remind yourself that God’s wisdom, whether as knowledge or act, is perfect?
?
The passage contrasts praise (wisdom) and deficiency (foolishness). Examine your day: where have you acted foolishly instead of wisely? What concrete step can you take tonight to align your actions with God’s attribute of wisdom?
?
The Maturidi school affirms wisdom in pre-eternity regardless of category. Do you live as if God’s wisdom preceded all events, including your mistakes? Repent of one decision where you acted as if you knew better than God.

The Established Attributes of the Maker
Lists proven attributes of God and asserts their rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive list of divine attributes that Maturidi scholars consider proven through rational and scriptural evidence.

Core Idea
God's attributes are rationally established and distinct from all creation.

Having built the foundation from the beginning of the book, the author now summarizes the attributes of God that have been firmly proven. This summary serves as a reference point for all subsequent discussions, reminding us why these attributes are necessary and how they distinguish the Creator from everything created. Pay close attention to the list, as each term carries specific theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage identifying each attribute
Read the Arabic slowly, noting each attribute mentioned after 'أن صانع العالم'. Count them and consider their meanings.
The list includes 12 attributes, ending with the negation of corporeality and similarity.
2
Reflect on the claim of evidence
The author says evidence was provided 'in a manner that leaves no doubt'. Ask yourself: what kind of evidence could establish these attributes without relying on blind faith?
3
Contrast with created things
Notice the negations: not a body, not an accident, not a substance, not similar to anything created. Why are these negations necessary to protect God's transcendence?
This prevents anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ثبت مما بينا من أول الكتاب إلى ها هنا أن صانع العالم موجود قائم بذاته باقي قادر سميع بصير متكلم خالق مريد حكيم، واحد ليس بجسم، ولا عرض ولا جوهر، ولا مشبه لشيء من المحدثات. وقد أقمنا الدلالة على كل من ذلك على وجه لا يبقى لمن تأمل في ذلك وأنصف ولم يكابر شبهةً ولا ريبةً. ثم بعد ذلك اتفق الناس أن القول بما يؤدي إلى إبطال شيء من هذه الصفات الثابتة له أو إثبات شيء من هذه المعاني المستحيلة عليه قول باطل وقعوا بعد هذا الاتفاق في الاختلاف في مسائل كثيرة لما أنه يثبت بعضهم معنى لا يؤدي إلى القول ببطلان شيء من هذه الصفات، ولا إلى إثبات ما يستحيل عليه عند هذا القائل وفناء غيرهم لما أنه يؤدي عنده إلى أحد هذين الأمرين وهو فاسد، فنتكلم في كل مسألة منها على ما يوجبه الدليل السمعي والعقلي بقدر الكفاية والإمكان - إن شاء الله تعالى.
Then it is established from what we have explained from the beginning of the book up to this point that the Maker of the world is existent, self-subsisting, eternal, powerful, hearing, seeing, speaking, creating, willing, wise, one, not a body, nor an accident, nor a substance, nor similar to anything created. And we have provided evidence for each of that in a manner such that for one who reflects on that and is fair and does not argue stubbornly, there remains no doubt or uncertainty. Then after that, the people agreed that saying what leads to negating any of these established attributes of His or affirming any of these impossible meanings of Him is false speech. They fell, after this agreement, into disagreement on many issues: because some affirm a meaning that does not lead to saying that any of these attributes are false, nor to affirming what is impossible for Him according to this speaker, while others perish because it leads according to them to one of these two matters, and it is corrupt. So we will speak in each issue of them according to what the scriptural and rational evidence necessitates, to the extent of sufficiency and possibility – if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 591

Commentary
This passage is a concluding summary that recapitulates the divine attributes established earlier and explains the nature of subsequent disagreements. The author lists the proven attributes: existence, self-subsistence, eternity, power, hearing, sight, speech, creation, will, wisdom, unity, and that God is not a body, accident, substance, or similar to any created thing. He asserts that evidence was provided convincingly. Then, he notes that all people agree that denying these attributes or affirming what is impossible for God is false. However, disagreement arises because some affirm additional meanings that do not contradict these established attributes, while others affirm meanings that (according to them) entail negation or impossibility, which is corrupt. The author thus announces that he will discuss each disputed issue according to scriptural and rational evidence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God can be described in anthropomorphic terms because of certain Quranic verses.
Correction: This passage explicitly negates that God is a body, accident, or substance, and denies any similarity to creation. Any interpretation suggesting otherwise violates these established negations.

Key Takeaway
The Creator's attributes are rationally proven and include both positive descriptions and negations that distinguish Him from all created beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If you were to die tonight, could you honestly say that you have reflected on God's attributes as described here, or have you simply skimmed over them? What will you change in your daily remembrance to make these attributes more real to you?

Consensus and the Origin of Disagreements
Explains universal agreement on core attributes and the basis for subsequent disagreements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why later theological disagreements arise despite universal agreement on the core attributes of God.

Core Idea
Disagreements in Kalam stem from affirming additional attributes without contradicting established ones.

After establishing the essential attributes, the author turns to a remarkable observation: all rational people agree that denying these attributes or affirming impossible ones is false. Yet, despite this consensus, disagreements persist. How? Because some theologians affirm additional divine meanings that do not violate the agreed-upon attributes, while others reject those meanings precisely because they fear contradiction. This passage sets the stage for the detailed discussions to follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal agreement
Read the first part of the passage: what do all people agree on? Highlight the phrase 'اتفق الناس أن القول بما يؤدي إلى إبطال شيء من هذه الصفات... باطل'.
The agreement is about the falsehood of undermining the established attributes.
2
Spot the source of disagreement
The text says disagreements occurred 'لما أنه يثبت بعضهم معنى لا يؤدي إلى بطلان شيء من هذه الصفات... وفناء غيرهم لما أنه يؤدي عنده إلى أحد هذين الأمرين'. What does this mean?
Disagreement arises from affirming extra meanings, not from denying the core.
3
Understand the author's methodology
The author announces he will discuss each disputed issue according to scriptural and rational evidence. Note the terms 'الدليل السمعي والعقلي'.
This establishes the dual source of knowledge: revelation and reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ثبت مما بينا من أول الكتاب إلى ها هنا أن صانع العالم موجود قائم بذاته باقي قادر سميع بصير متكلم خالق مريد حكيم، واحد ليس بجسم، ولا عرض ولا جوهر، ولا مشبه لشيء من المحدثات. وقد أقمنا الدلالة على كل من ذلك على وجه لا يبقى لمن تأمل في ذلك وأنصف ولم يكابر شبهةً ولا ريبةً. ثم بعد ذلك اتفق الناس أن القول بما يؤدي إلى إبطال شيء من هذه الصفات الثابتة له أو إثبات شيء من هذه المعاني المستحيلة عليه قول باطل وقعوا بعد هذا الاتفاق في الاختلاف في مسائل كثيرة لما أنه يثبت بعضهم معنى لا يؤدي إلى القول ببطلان شيء من هذه الصفات، ولا إلى إثبات ما يستحيل عليه عند هذا القائل وفناء غيرهم لما أنه يؤدي عنده إلى أحد هذين الأمرين وهو فاسد، فنتكلم في كل مسألة منها على ما يوجبه الدليل السمعي والعقلي بقدر الكفاية والإمكان - إن شاء الله تعالى.
Then it is established from what we have explained from the beginning of the book up to this point that the Maker of the world is existent, self-subsisting, eternal, powerful, hearing, seeing, speaking, creating, willing, wise, one, not a body, nor an accident, nor a substance, nor similar to anything created. And we have provided evidence for each of that in a manner such that for one who reflects on that and is fair and does not argue stubbornly, there remains no doubt or uncertainty. Then after that, the people agreed that saying what leads to negating any of these established attributes of His or affirming any of these impossible meanings of Him is false speech. They fell, after this agreement, into disagreement on many issues: because some affirm a meaning that does not lead to saying that any of these attributes are false, nor to affirming what is impossible for Him according to this speaker, while others perish because it leads according to them to one of these two matters, and it is corrupt. So we will speak in each issue of them according to what the scriptural and rational evidence necessitates, to the extent of sufficiency and possibility – if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 591

Commentary
This passage is a concluding summary that recapitulates the divine attributes established earlier and explains the nature of subsequent disagreements. The author lists the proven attributes: existence, self-subsistence, eternity, power, hearing, sight, speech, creation, will, wisdom, unity, and that God is not a body, accident, substance, or similar to any created thing. He asserts that evidence was provided convincingly. Then, he notes that all people agree that denying these attributes or affirming what is impossible for God is false. However, disagreement arises because some affirm additional meanings that do not contradict these established attributes, while others affirm meanings that (according to them) entail negation or impossibility, which is corrupt. The author thus announces that he will discuss each disputed issue according to scriptural and rational evidence.

A) They rejected some of the core attributes.
B) They disagreed on the meaning of 'false speech'.

D) They disagreed about the number of core attributes.

Key Takeaway
Theological disagreements often concern additional attributes, not the core ones, and the Māturīdī approach uses both scripture and reason to resolve them.

?
You likely agree on core attributes like God's existence and power. But when you defend an additional belief (like seeing God in the afterlife, or the nature of His will), do you honestly evaluate whether it conflicts with those core attributes? Or do you simply follow your preference?
?
The author commits to using both scripture and reason. In your own journey, when has your reason conflicted with a plain reading of scripture? Did you dismiss the rational challenge, or did you seek a deeper understanding? What will you do the next time that happens?

الكلام في جواز رؤية الله تعالى في العقل ووجوبها بالسمع: هل يرى الله سبحانه وتعالى؟ اختلف الناس في جواز رؤية الله تعالى. قال أهل الحق: إن الله تعالى جائز الرؤية يعرف ذلك بالدليل العقلي، ويراه المؤمنون في الآخرة بعد دخولهم الجنة. يثبت ذلك بالدلائل السمعية. وقالت المعتزلة والنجارية والزيدية من الروافض : إن الله تعالى ليس بجائز الرؤية، بل يستحيل ذلك عليه ولا يراه أحد لا في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة، لأن المحال لا يستبدل باختلاف الأزمنة والدهور، ولا باختلاف الأمكنة والبقاع فلن يصير جائزا البتة، فلا يرى لا في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة وإنما اختلفوا لأن الخصوم ظنوا أن القول بالرؤية يوجب إثبات ما يستحيل على الله تعالى وأهل الحق قد عرفوا أنه لا يؤدي إلى ذلك. أدلة الخصوم على نفي الرؤية: احتج الخصوم في ذلك بقوله تعالى: لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ الآية فالله تعالى تمدح بانتفاء الرؤية عن ذاته، إذ الإدراك بالبصر ليس إلا الرؤية كما تمدح بأسمائه الحسنى وصفاته العلا في سياق الآية وسياقها، فكان حاصل الآية التمدح بثبوت هذه الصفات اللاتي هي صفات الكمال، وبنفي ما لا يليق بصفاته، وفي إثبات ما نفى إزالة التمدح وإثبات النقص كما هو في نفي ما أثبت بقوله: بَدِيعُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وقوله: خَلِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَكِيلٌ وقوله: وَهُوَ اللَّطِيفُ الْخَبِيرُ وذلك لا يجوز وليس يستقيم التمدح بنفي الإدراك في الدنيا دون الآخرة، لأن إثبات ما تمدح بنفيه نقص، وإثبات النقص عليه وإزالة ما
Discussion on the permissibility of seeing Allah by reason and its obligation by revelation: Is Allah, glorified and exalted, seen? People differed on the permissibility of seeing Allah. The people of truth said: Allah, exalted, is possible to be seen; that is known by rational evidence, and the believers will see Him in the Hereafter after entering Paradise; that is established by scriptural evidences. The Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, and Zaydiyya among the Rafida said: Allah, exalted, is not possible to be seen; rather, it is impossible for Him, and no one will see Him, neither in this world nor in the Hereafter, because the impossible does not change with difference of times and ages, nor with difference of places and regions, so it will never become possible at all. Thus He is not seen, neither in this world nor in the Hereafter. They only differed because the opponents thought that affirming seeing necessitates affirming what is impossible for Allah, while the people of truth knew that it does not lead to that. The proofs of the opponents for denying seeing: The opponents argued in that with His saying: 'Vision comprehends Him not' (6:103). So Allah, exalted, praised Himself by negating vision from His essence, for perception by sight is nothing but vision, just as He praised Himself with His beautiful names and high attributes in the context of the verse and its sequel. So the outcome of the verse is praise for affirming these attributes which are attributes of perfection, and for negating what is not fitting for His attributes. And affirming what He negated would remove the praise and affirm deficiency, just as in negating what He affirmed in His saying: 'Originator of the heavens and the earth' (6:101) and His saying: 'Creator of everything, and He is over all things a Guardian' (39:62) and His saying: 'And He is the Subtle, the Aware' (6:103). That is not permissible, and it is not proper to praise oneself by negating perception in this world but not in the Hereafter, because affirming what He praised Himself by negating is a deficiency, and affirming deficiency upon Him and removing...
Source pages p. 591

Commentary
This passage opens the discussion on the beatific vision (ru'ya). It begins by posing the question: is Allah to be seen? The people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) hold that seeing Allah is rationally possible and that believers will see Him in the Hereafter, as proven by scriptural evidence. The Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, and Zaydiyya Rafida assert that seeing Allah is absolutely impossible, both in this world and the next, because impossibility does not change with time or place. The author explains that the disagreement stems from the opponents' assumption that affirming the vision would imply attributes unbecoming of God, while Ahl al-Haqq recognize that it does not. The passage then presents the opponents' first proof: the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Vision comprehends Him not). Their reasoning is that God praises Himself by negating vision (comprehension by sight), so affirming vision would contradict this self-praise and imply deficiency. They analogize this to other verses where God praises Himself by affirming attributes like creating and subtle knowledge—negating or affirming the opposite would remove the praise. They argue that the negation cannot be limited to this world only, because that would imply deficiency if vision were possible in the Hereafter. The passage cuts off mid-argument.

Key Takeaway
The vision of Allah is rationally possible and scripturally promised; opponents argue it would contradict God's self-praise in the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' is a praise. When you read or recite it, do you feel awe at His transcendence, or have you ever felt that it makes Him distant? How can you transform that verse into a promise of intimacy rather than a barrier?
?
