The Mu'tazili Views on the Visible and the Principle of Non-Transfer
Nasafi outlines the Mu'tazili disagreement on visible entities and declares the non-transfer of judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim defined the visible and why Nasafi argues that the judgment of vision does not transfer from the sensible world to God.

Core Idea
What is visible in the world does not set the rules for what is possible for God.

Nasafi begins by presenting two Mu'tazili views on which entities are visible in the sensible world. Al-Jubba'i includes certain accidents like colors and spatial configurations, while Abu Hashim restricts visibility to bodies and colors. Nasafi then asserts the crucial principle: none of this can be transferred to the unseen realm, because the conditions of vision are tied to physicality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views
Read al-Jubba'i's claim and Abu Hashim's claim carefully. Notice what each includes as visible.
Al-Jubba'i includes 'akwān' (motion, rest, etc.) while Abu Hashim excludes them.
2
Understand the principle of non-transfer
Nasafi states that 'nothing of this transfers to the unseen.' Ask yourself why this principle matters for the debate on seeing God.
3
Reflect on the implication
If the judgment does not transfer, then one cannot use analogy from the world to prove God is visible.
This sets up the next lesson on the conditions.

وزعم الجبائي أن بعض الأعراض وهو الألوان وأنواعها معروفة، والأكوان الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق والقرب والبعد يرى، كما أن الأجسام ترى، فكانت الرؤية عنده متعلقة بأحد هذه المعاني، وهي الجسم واللون والكون. وزعم أبو هاشم أن المرئي هي الأجسام والألوان، وأما الأكوان فليست بمرئية، وشيء من ذلك لا يتعدى إلى الغائب فلا يتعدى الحكم وهو جواز الرؤية. قالوا والشرط أيضا لا يتعدى، فإن شرط الرؤية أن يكون بين الرائي والمرئي مسافة حتى إن المرئي لو التصق بآلة الرؤية انعدمت الرؤية. وكذا اتصال الشعاع وانطباع المرئي في آلة الرؤية شرط عند من لا يثبت الرؤية إلا الأجسام، فإن المذهب عند رئيسهم وهو النظام أن المحسوسات لا تدرك إلا بالمماسة المجاورة، فإن اليد لا تستدرك الحرارة والبرودة والملامسة والخشونة والصلابة واللين، إلا بالمحاسة الحاصلة بين آلة الحس وبين المحسوس، وكذا الطعوم لن تستدرك إلا بالمماسة الحاصلة بين آلة الذوق وهي اللسان واللهوات وبين الطعام، وكذا الشم على هذا فإن الرائحة عنده جسم ينفصل عن الجسم المشموم ويتصل بخياشيم الشام ليحصل له العلم بذلك، وكذا الصوت عنده جسم وهو الهواء المضغوط فينفصل عن الجسم فينتقل في الجو إلى أن يدخل صماخ السامع فيقع بينهما مماسة فيحصل له العلم بالصوت، فكذا لابد من مماسة حاصلة بين آلة الرؤية وبين المرئي فينفصل شعاع من عين الرائي فيتصل بالمرئي فيحصل بينهما مماسة فيعلم بالمرئي، والمماسة لا تجوز إلا على الأجسام. ويصير بيان هذا الشرط تحقيقا لجعل النظام علة الرؤية كونه جسما، إذ المماسة لا تتصور إلا بين جسمين. وعند بعضهم يشترط أيضا انطباع صورة المرئي في العين التي هي آلة الرؤية، كما ينطبع في المرآة صورة الأجسام المقابلة لها، لولا ذلك الانطباع لما حصل العلم بالمرئي، دليله انعدام الرؤية عند زوال الانجلاء عن العين وبطلان الانطباع وتبدل البصر بالعمى، ولا يتصور انطباع الصورة إلا في حق الجسم إذ لا صورة لغيره. قالوا: وكذلك الضوء الحاصل في هواء المسافة الممتدة بين الرائي والمرئي شرط لحصول الرؤية، دليله انعدامها عند ارتفاع الضوء بطريان ظلام الليل، وذا محال في حق الله تعالى فصح بهذا ما ادعينا من انعدام تعدي علة الرؤية وشروطها إلى الغائب ولا يتعدى الحكم أيضا. يحققه أن أحدهما إما العلة وإما الشرط، لو تعدى وحده بدون صاحبه لا يتعدى الحكم، فإذا لم يتعديا جميعا أولى أن لا يتعدى هذا كله لبيان المفارقة بين الشاهد والغائب في العلة والشروط، ومنع التعدية لانعدام المساواة.
Al-Jubba'i claimed that some accidents, namely colors and their types, are known, and the accidents of being (motion, rest, conjunction, separation, proximity, distance) are seen, just as bodies are seen. Thus in his view, vision is attached to one of these meanings: body, color, or accident of being. Abu Hashim claimed that the visible are bodies and colors, but accidents of being are not visible. None of this transfers to the unseen, so the judgment (permissibility of vision) does not transfer. They said: The condition also does not transfer. For the condition of vision is that there be a distance between the seer and the seen, so that if the seen adhered to the organ of vision, vision would cease. Likewise, the connection of the ray and the impression of the seen in the organ of vision is a condition for those who affirm vision only for bodies. Indeed, the doctrine of their leader, an-Nazzam, is that sensibles are only perceived through contact and proximity. For the hand does not perceive heat, cold, touch, roughness, hardness, or softness except through the sensation occurring between the sense organ and the sensed. Likewise tastes are only perceived through contact occurring between the organ of taste—the tongue and palate—and the food. Likewise for smell, for in his view the odor is a body that separates from the smelled body and connects to the nostrils so that knowledge of it is obtained. Likewise sound is in his view a body, which is compressed air; it separates from the body and travels through the air until it enters the ear canal, then there occurs between them contact, and thus knowledge of the sound is obtained. Thus there must be contact between the organ of vision and the seen, so a ray separates from the eye of the seer and connects to the seen, thus there occurs between them contact, and thereby the seen is known. Contact is only possible for bodies. This condition serves to realize an-Nazzam's making the cause of vision the fact that it is a body, since contact is only conceivable between two bodies. And according to some, it is also required that the image of the seen be impressed in the eye, which is the organ of vision, just as the image of bodies facing it is impressed in a mirror; if not for that impression, knowledge of the seen would not be obtained. Its evidence is the absence of vision when clarity leaves the eye, the failure of impression, and the change of sight to blindness. Impression of an image is only conceivable for a body, since there is no image for other than it. They said: Similarly, the light present in the air of the distance extending between the seer and the seen is a condition for obtaining vision; its evidence is its absence when light is removed by the advent of night's darkness. And that is impossible with respect to God. Thus what we claimed is proven: the absence of transfer of the cause and conditions of vision to the unseen, and the judgment does not transfer either. This is verified by the fact that one of them is either the cause or the condition; if one transferred alone without its companion, the judgment would not transfer. So when both do not transfer, it is even more fitting that it does not transfer. All this is to clarify the distinction between the witnessed and the unseen in terms of cause and conditions, and to prevent transfer due to lack of equality.
Source pages p. 594

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the vision (ru'yah) of God, specifically refuting the possibility of establishing it by analogy from the sensible world (shahid). The author, Nasafi, presents the views of two Mu'tazili scholars, al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim, on what is visible. Al-Jubba'i claimed that certain accidents—colors and the 'akwān' (motion, rest, conjunction, separation, proximity, distance)—are visible along with bodies. So in his view, vision attaches to body, color, or 'kawn'. Abu Hashim restricted the visible to bodies and colors, excluding the 'akwān'. Nasafi then notes that none of these views can be transferred to the unseen (ghā'ib), so the judgment of permissibility of vision does not transfer either. Then the discussion turns to the conditions of vision in the shahid. The first condition mentioned is distance between seer and seen; if the object is too close, vision fails. The second condition is the connection of a ray (shu'ā') or the impression (inṭibā') of the seen in the eye, according to those who only affirm vision for bodies. The author elaborates an-Nazzam's theory that all sensations require contact (mumāssa) between the sense organ and the object. He gives examples: touch, taste, smell, sound—all require contact. Thus vision also requires contact via a ray emitted from the eye meeting the object. Since contact only occurs between bodies, this condition implies that the object of vision must be a body. Some also require the impression of the image in the eye, which again applies only to bodies. Another condition is light; without light, vision is impossible. All these conditions are physical and specific to bodies. Nasafi argues that these conditions cannot apply to God, who is not a body. Therefore, the cause and conditions of vision do not transfer to the unseen, and the judgment (that God is visible) does not transfer either. The conclusion is that one cannot use analogy from the sensible world to prove that God is visible.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الشاهد
الغائب
الجسم
اللون
الكون

Key Takeaway
The disagreement among Mu'tazilites about what is visible in the world shows that there is no unified basis to transfer the possibility of vision to God.

?

The Physical Conditions of Vision and Their Inapplicability to God
Nasafi enumerates the physical conditions for vision in the sensible world and argues they do not transfer to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical conditions required for vision in the sensible world—distance, contact, impression, and light—and why they cannot apply to God.

Core Idea
Vision in the world depends on physical conditions that are impossible for the non-physical God.

In this passage, Nasafi turns to the conditions of vision in the sensible world. He lists several: distance between seer and seen, the connection of a ray or impression of the image in the eye, and the presence of light. He then argues that all these conditions presuppose that the object of vision is a body. Since God is not a body, these conditions—and consequently the judgment of visibility—cannot be transferred to the unseen.

Guided Path
1
List the conditions of vision
Read the passage and identify the conditions Nasafi mentions: distance, ray/impression (with an-Nazzam's theory of contact), and light.
Take notes on each condition and why it is required.
2
Understand an-Nazzam's theory of sensation
An-Nazzam argues that all senses require contact between the organ and the object. See how he applies this to touch, taste, smell, sound, and finally vision.
This leads to the requirement of contact for vision.
3
Analyze why each condition implies corporeality
For each condition, note how it can only apply to bodies. For example, contact only between bodies, impression only of images of bodies, light only affects physical objects.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since none of these conditions can apply to God, the judgment that God is visible cannot be transferred from the sensible world.
This is the central point against using analogy for divine vision.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المحسوسات لا تدرك إلا بالمماسة المجاورة، فإن اليد لا تستدرك الحرارة والبرودة والملامسة والخشونة والصلابة واللين، إلا بالمحاسة الحاصلة بين آلة الحس وبين المحسوس، وكذا الطعوم لن تستدرك إلا بالمماسة الحاصلة بين آلة الذوق وهي اللسان واللهوات وبين الطعام، وكذا الشم على هذا فإن الرائحة عنده جسم ينفصل عن الجسم المشموم ويتصل بخياشيم الشام ليحصل له العلم بذلك، وكذا الصوت عنده جسم وهو الهواء المضغوط فينفصل عن الجسم فينتقل في الجو إلى أن يدخل صماخ السامع فيقع بينهما مماسة فيحصل له العلم بالصوت، فكذا لابد من مماسة حاصلة بين آلة الرؤية وبين المرئي فينفصل شعاع من عين الرائي فيتصل بالمرئي فيحصل بينهما مماسة فيعلم بالمرئي، والمماسة لا تجوز إلا على الأجسام. ويصير بيان هذا الشرط تحقيقا لجعل النظام علة الرؤية كونه جسما، إذ المماسة لا تتصور إلا بين جسمين. وعند بعضهم يشترط أيضا انطباع صورة المرئي في العين التي هي آلة الرؤية، كما ينطبع في المرآة صورة الأجسام المقابلة لها، لولا ذلك الانطباع لما حصل العلم بالمرئي، دليله انعدام الرؤية عند زوال الانجلاء عن العين وبطلان الانطباع وتبدل البصر بالعمى، ولا يتصور انطباع الصورة إلا في حق الجسم إذ لا صورة لغيره. قالوا: وكذلك الضوء الحاصل في هواء المسافة الممتدة بين الرائي والمرئي شرط لحصول الرؤية، دليله انعدامها عند ارتفاع الضوء بطريان ظلام الليل، وذا محال في حق الله تعالى فصح بهذا ما ادعينا من انعدام تعدي علة الرؤية وشروطها إلى الغائب ولا يتعدى الحكم أيضا. يحققه أن أحدهما إما العلة وإما الشرط، لو تعدى وحده بدون صاحبه لا يتعدى الحكم، فإذا لم يتعديا جميعا أولى أن لا يتعدى هذا كله لبيان المفارقة بين الشاهد والغائب في العلة والشروط، ومنع التعدية لانعدام المساواة.
Al-Jubba'i claimed that some accidents, namely colors and their types, are known, and the accidents of being (motion, rest, conjunction, separation, proximity, distance) are seen, just as bodies are seen. Thus in his view, vision is attached to one of these meanings: body, color, or accident of being. Abu Hashim claimed that the visible are bodies and colors, but accidents of being are not visible. None of this transfers to the unseen, so the judgment (permissibility of vision) does not transfer. They said: The condition also does not transfer. For the condition of vision is that there be a distance between the seer and the seen, so that if the seen adhered to the organ of vision, vision would cease. Likewise, the connection of the ray and the impression of the seen in the organ of vision is a condition for those who affirm vision only for bodies. Indeed, the doctrine of their leader, an-Nazzam, is that sensibles are only perceived through contact and proximity. For the hand does not perceive heat, cold, touch, roughness, hardness, or softness except through the sensation occurring between the sense organ and the sensed. Likewise tastes are only perceived through contact occurring between the organ of taste—the tongue and palate—and the food. Likewise for smell, for in his view the odor is a body that separates from the smelled body and connects to the nostrils so that knowledge of it is obtained. Likewise sound is in his view a body, which is compressed air; it separates from the body and travels through the air until it enters the ear canal, then there occurs between them contact, and thus knowledge of the sound is obtained. Thus there must be contact between the organ of vision and the seen, so a ray separates from the eye of the seer and connects to the seen, thus there occurs between them contact, and thereby the seen is known. Contact is only possible for bodies. This condition serves to realize an-Nazzam's making the cause of vision the fact that it is a body, since contact is only conceivable between two bodies. And according to some, it is also required that the image of the seen be impressed in the eye, which is the organ of vision, just as the image of bodies facing it is impressed in a mirror; if not for that impression, knowledge of the seen would not be obtained. Its evidence is the absence of vision when clarity leaves the eye, the failure of impression, and the change of sight to blindness. Impression of an image is only conceivable for a body, since there is no image for other than it. They said: Similarly, the light present in the air of the distance extending between the seer and the seen is a condition for obtaining vision; its evidence is its absence when light is removed by the advent of night's darkness. And that is impossible with respect to God. Thus what we claimed is proven: the absence of transfer of the cause and conditions of vision to the unseen, and the judgment does not transfer either. This is verified by the fact that one of them is either the cause or the condition; if one transferred alone without its companion, the judgment would not transfer. So when both do not transfer, it is even more fitting that it does not transfer. All this is to clarify the distinction between the witnessed and the unseen in terms of cause and conditions, and to prevent transfer due to lack of equality.
Source pages p. 594

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the vision (ru'yah) of God, specifically refuting the possibility of establishing it by analogy from the sensible world (shahid). The author, Nasafi, presents the views of two Mu'tazili scholars, al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim, on what is visible. Al-Jubba'i claimed that certain accidents—colors and the 'akwān' (motion, rest, conjunction, separation, proximity, distance)—are visible along with bodies. So in his view, vision attaches to body, color, or 'kawn'. Abu Hashim restricted the visible to bodies and colors, excluding the 'akwān'. Nasafi then notes that none of these views can be transferred to the unseen (ghā'ib), so the judgment of permissibility of vision does not transfer either. Then the discussion turns to the conditions of vision in the shahid. The first condition mentioned is distance between seer and seen; if the object is too close, vision fails. The second condition is the connection of a ray (shu'ā') or the impression (inṭibā') of the seen in the eye, according to those who only affirm vision for bodies. The author elaborates an-Nazzam's theory that all sensations require contact (mumāssa) between the sense organ and the object. He gives examples: touch, taste, smell, sound—all require contact. Thus vision also requires contact via a ray emitted from the eye meeting the object. Since contact only occurs between bodies, this condition implies that the object of vision must be a body. Some also require the impression of the image in the eye, which again applies only to bodies. Another condition is light; without light, vision is impossible. All these conditions are physical and specific to bodies. Nasafi argues that these conditions cannot apply to God, who is not a body. Therefore, the cause and conditions of vision do not transfer to the unseen, and the judgment (that God is visible) does not transfer either. The conclusion is that one cannot use analogy from the sensible world to prove that God is visible.

Key Terms
الشروط
المماسة
انطباع الصورة
الضوء
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if vision requires distance, then God must be at a distance to be seen, which is impossible, so God cannot be seen.
Correction: Nasafi's point is not that distance itself is impossible for God, but that all these conditions presuppose physicality. The core issue is that God is not a body, so any condition that requires a body fails.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi argue that the condition of light cannot apply to God?
A) God is light
B) Light is a physical phenomenon and God is not physical
C) God does not need light to see
D) Light was created after God

Key Takeaway
The physical conditions of vision—distance, contact, impression, and light—all presuppose a physical object, and therefore cannot be used to argue for the possibility of seeing God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself trying to imagine God using human senses, like picturing His 'look' or 'location'? After reading this passage, what does your desire to visualize God reveal about your own assumptions—and will you let this lesson challenge that habit?
?
If the conditions of vision all presuppose a physical object, then your longing to 'see' God (perhaps in Paradise) is not a simple desire for sensory experience. What does this lesson reveal about the limits of your imagination when it comes to the divine? Are you willing to trust that seeing God in the afterlife will be something entirely beyond physical sight?
?
Nasafi's argument strips away every physical condition from God. Does your heart find this freeing or unsettling? Take a moment: Is there part of you that secretly wishes God were more 'imaginable'? What would it mean for your worship to fully accept that He is beyond all your senses?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
4 min
The Linguistic and Rational Definition of Vision Cannot Apply to God
Nasafi argues that the vision claimed for God does not fit the linguistic and rational definition of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that the conventional definition of vision, based on language and reason, cannot be applied to God.

Core Idea
Vision requires a body, facing, alignment; God is beyond such conditions.

Nasafi begins a new section analyzing the essence of vision (nafs ar-ru'yah) itself. He argues that the term 'vision' as understood by people of language and intellect has specific prerequisites. Since God does not meet these, claiming that God is seen would require redefining the term without linguistic or rational warrant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Nasafi starts with a clear statement about what is not known as vision.
The Arabic is incomplete, but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the conditions of vision
Look for the list of conditions that define vision in language and reason: being a body or accident, facing, alignment, etc.
These are all physical or spatial conditions.
3
Understand why they cannot apply to God
Reflect on Islamic theology: God is neither a body nor an accident, and He is not in a direction. So He cannot meet any of these conditions.
4
Grasp the implication
Nasafi’s point is that affirming vision of God would require using the term in a sense not recognized by language or reason, which is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الكلام من حيث نفس الرؤية فإنهم يقولون : إن ما تدعون في حق الصانع لن يعرف رؤية عند أهل اللسان، وفي المعارف بين أرباب العقول فإن رؤية ما ليس بجسم ولا عرض ولا مقابلة بين الرائي وبينه، ولا محاذاة ولا اتصال شعاع ولا بتقليب المقلة، وتوجيه الوجه إليه والإقبال عليه، واستدراك قدره،
As for the discourse from the perspective of the essence of vision, they say: What you claim regarding the Creator is not known as vision according to the people of language, nor in the knowledge among people of intellect. For the vision of that which is not a body, nor an accident, nor is there facing between the seer and it, nor alignment, nor connection of a ray, nor by turning the eyeball, nor turning the face toward it and approaching it, nor perceiving its measure...
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This passage begins a new section titled 'al-Kalam fi nafs ar-ru'yah' (discourse on the essence of vision). Here Nasafi shifts from discussing the conditions of vision in the sensible world to a direct analysis of what the word 'vision' (ru'yah) means. He states that what the opponents claim regarding the vision of the Creator is not known as 'ru'yah' either in language (lisān) or in the knowledge of rational people. The reason is that the ordinary concept of vision requires certain things: the object must be a body or an accident; there must be facing (muqābala) between seer and seen; alignment (muḥādāh); connection of a ray (ittiṣāl shu'ā'); turning the eyeball (taqlīb al-maqla); turning the face toward the object and approaching it; and perceiving its measure (istidrāk qadrih). Since God does not possess any of these attributes, the term 'vision' cannot be applied to Him in its usual sense. The passage is cut off, but the argument is clear: the claim that God is seen cannot be sustained because it does not match what is called vision in common usage or in rational epistemology. Therefore, those who affirm vision of God would have to redefine 'vision' in a way that is not linguistically or rationally warranted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'vision' can be used for any kind of perception, even without physical conditions.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the term 'vision' has a specific meaning in language and reason, requiring physical conditions like facing and alignment. Without these, it is not considered vision.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is the vision claimed for the Creator not considered true vision?
A) Because it is impossible for God to be seen
B) Because it does not meet the linguistic and rational conditions of vision (e.g., being a body, facing)
C) Because the Qur'an denies it
D) Because the Prophet said so

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that the conventional concept of vision requires physical conditions that cannot be attributed to God, so the term cannot be applied to Him in its usual sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah as having a 'face' or 'hand' in a literal sense? What does Nasafi's argument reveal about that mental image? Commit to replacing any anthropomorphic picture with the declaration 'Laysa kamithlihi shay''.
?
When you read that vision requires 'facing, alignment, ray connection'—do you feel a resistance to accepting that Allah cannot be seen in this way? What is the root of that resistance in your heart? Make a sincere intention to submit to Allah's transcendence even when it feels abstract.
?
If the vision of Allah in the afterlife is a reality, yet this lesson shows it cannot be physical vision as we know it, how does that reshape your hope? Are you content to trust what Allah has promised without trying to define it? Repent of any insistence on defining the undefined.
